Neighborhood mom gives but never takes
Guest11:05 a.m.
QUESTION: Hi Carolyn, Happy Halloween!

There is a ring of moms in my neighborhood, we've all become friends as many of us are staying home with our young children. One mom, "Paige," loves to make treats for the kids, give them all little gifts (for example she left them all a "boo basket" last night that they found this morning upon waking up), and invite people to send their kids over for a couple hours if we have an errand to run. It is all so lovely and kind, and yet she NEVER lets anyone reciprocate. I have invited her countless times to let me watch her daughter while she steps out (as a bonus, this would help entertain my kids, who love playing with hers), but she always says no even if I know she does not have an alternative. There is no real space for anyone else to do things like the boo baskets because she always does it so well and so early that we can't really compete.

Is it ok that some people are just givers who never want to take? (But, I don't want it to be thought that my family takes and doesn't give!)
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: There may be no space to give back, I've definitely seen this -- but is there no space for conversation, either? One-on-one with Paige: "You do so much for the rest of us -- always so kind and thoughtful. I am not comfortable, though, with how lopsided things have become. I have never been a 'taker.' If you won't let me reciprocate in kind by watching your daughter, then will you at least name something I can do to put the accounts more in balance?"

She may still rebuff you, but at least then you will be on the record with your discomfort with taking advantage. Not to get too alarmist or put ideas in your head, but a mom who always hosts and never ever ever lets other parents reciprocate may have had a bad experience herself as a child in another's care and this may be her way of being vigilant without denying her kid a full community social life. Just ... I don't know. There are plenty of other possible explanations but it rang a bell, let's say.

The second thing this conversation accomplishes -- again, assuming Paige doesn't respond with some concrete suggestion for you -- is to free you from any expectation that she will say yes to balance. Now you can take it upon yourself to do something nice for Paige on your own. Pay attention, see where she shops for treats or how she decorates or what she does for fun or whatever, and buy her gift cards periodically and generally spoil her a little. Presumably she won't give things back?

Husband's therapist seems to want him to cheat.
Guest11:35 a.m.
QUESTION: Several months ago I found some old messages my husband had sent to young women on an "arrangements" website. It was apparent he never followed through, but I was devastated. After lots of tears and pleading from him, I agreed to stay with him, but only if he agreed to therapy.

The problem is, his therapist, a guy, has emboldened him. Before, he was emphatic that he was in the wrong, that it was a moment of weakness, and that it would never happen again. But now, he's almost indignant, saying our sex drive used to be the same but now mine is much lower, and that I have a responsibility to tend to his needs, one way or the other. He's pushing me to have sex (when he knows I'm less interested), to take libido-enhancing drugs (which he knows I'm against), and even that I should agree to let him be fill his needs with these "arrangements" since his love for me has not changed (using marital money and exposing me to disease).

The worst part is that he's weaponizing his therapist against me. All this pushing is at his therapist's behest, and when I push back, and tell him to stop violating my boundaries, I'm met with "but my therapist said X" and then he won't budge. Someone wrote on chat a few months ago about a spouse using their therapist as a marital appeals court, and that's what it feels like.

Truthfully I want him to stop seeing this therapist who is putting all these marriage-ending ideas in his head, and to stick with his first instincts -- that he screwed up and won't do it again. But I'm the one who insisted on therapy so I don't know how I do this. Advice?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I can see why you want the therapist off the scene. Your husband's "first instincts" response sounded much more in your favor -- mainly because it let you believe this would all go away and you could go back to the marriage you thought you had before your awful discovery.

Thing is, that whole "my bad" and "I'll never do it again" reaction is a panic response from someone who doesn't want to break anything. It's scary to think it might all be over in an instant. So people, when caught, tend to do what your husband did and try desperately, reflexively to hold the status quo together. This doesn't make the underlying stuff go away.

I (and you) don't know that the therapist is saying to your husband. But I have a pretty good idea, if he's doing his job, that he's getting at the unhappiness and frustration that led your husband to sneak around on websites. And urging your husband to talk to you instead. Wouldn't that make sense, when you see it spelled out that way?

The therapist could be guiding your husband (not advising, remember, that's not what they do) to say out loud how unhappy he is that you decided unilaterally without conversation or remediation effort that sex would dwindle to near nothingness, and that he would have no recourse because you were also deciding unilaterally that it was monogamy or bust -- again without conversation.

This is, arguably, a fair beef -- not the unilateral decision on sex, because that is what it is (the alternative being rape), but the "without conversation." I say arguably because two people can differ on what "no" conversation or effort means.

It's also possible your husband came home from this work and spun it as "My therapist says you owe me X." You don't know how well he's representing the therapy. And no means no regardless, once conversation is had -- but it's discussion of options and recourse between you.

So, two possible ways out of this blind alley. One is to see if your husband (and the therapist) will agree to have you join him for a session. The other is to enter couples counseling in addition to your husband's individual. Pretty common configuration. You could also do both, sequentially. But expecting to shoo away the problem with the therapist is not realistic. I'm sorry.

Needing a break doesn't mean I don't care
Guest11:57 a.m.
QUESTION: Hello. I‚Äôm having a difficult time conversing with my teenager (age 17) about politics and world events. We‚Äôre mostly on the same page as far as values and concerns. The difficulty lies in my needing time away from all the doom scrolling and negative energy. He wants to talk about politics and what is wrong in the world all the time. I do not. We do have lots of conversations about these topics, but I cannot handle it 24/7. When I say I don't feel up to continuing a conversation, he says ‚Äúwell I can‚Äôt just ignore that this stuff is happening.‚Äù When I try to talk about something positive, his response is to find the negative. It‚Äôs like we live in a Debbie Downer episode.

I‚Äôve explained that needing time away from negative news doesn‚Äôt mean I‚Äôm ignoring it, don‚Äôt agree with him, don‚Äôt think it‚Äôs real, or don‚Äôt care about it. We‚Äôve talked about focusing on what you can do now, rather than spending all your time agonizing about an uncertain future. We‚Äôve talked about finding pleasure in small things, which will invariably turn into a conversation about something negative about that nice thing. (Like, I say the flowers in the garden look so pretty right now and he says, not for long with how we‚Äôre ruining the environment). It‚Äôs not like I don‚Äôt think about these issues, but I find value in spending time appreciating things just to appreciate them regardless of where we‚Äôre headed as a society.

Some background in case it helps ‚Äì my child does suffer from anxiety and depression (as do I) and is already on medication and in therapy (as am I). He is also on the spectrum and tends to obsess over topics.

I wish I could help him see the value in turning off the negativity, and that doing so doesn't mean you don't care. I don‚Äôt know how to do that beyond what I‚Äôm already doing, but I would love some suggestions. As I'm writing this I'm also realizing that I am sad because it seems like my child doesn't respect me or see that I do really care about this stuff. I'm not sure there's an answer for that part.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: All the elements of this -- the over-talking, the doom looping, the anxiety and depression, the parenting challenge -- all point to relief in the form of a concrete action framework.

For example, your kid needs things he can do right now, vs. talking about focusing on things he can do now. Canvassing/phone banking if he's into politics, omg food banks need everything -- any community organizing or volunteering. But apolitical works too, like an animal shelter.

You and your kid would get along better if you were doing this productive thing together, including talking about it for specific organizational purposes, instead of talking generally about all the problems.

Your correct diagnosis of negativity exhaustion, and your prescription of "talk about something positive," would land more effectively if it were part of a cycle of effectiveness: You will do your Productive Thing better if you take this rest period to restore your productivity. This is where your kid is right now. Bonus, this is also correct -- the most productive people are the ones who rest. So you won't be trying to sell flowers to your kid in the vacuum of pleasestoptalkingIneedflowers!!! anymore, which obviously doesn't resonate with him. You can even incorporate two other truths: Beauty and peace are what you're fighting the negativity for, right? So it makes sense to devote time to appreciating those, too. And, you can also actively cultivate them. The values don't promote themselves. Plant and nurture, literally and figuratively.

Do, though, take the "it seems like my child doesn't respect me" out of it. He's 17, there's a good, developmental chance he's actively hating you right now. He's just doing his job, though, of differentiating himself from you, and the more personally you take it, the harder you make it for him. Just love him and trust he loves you through all the aaaaaaarghggggghhhh (wait -- can you mentally conjure the Tasmanian Devil cartoon sound? Imagine that's his psyche till 18-ish. It helps.)

avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Into 2/3 and 1/3, unless one of the parties wants to treat everyone, and I'm answering this incredibly obvious question only because I hear there are still people out there who try to stick the single with half because it's "easier," and NO, people do not get to make their crappy arithmetic skills anyone else's problem. Which includes forcing the single person into the awkward "nope" position.

Was that the question?

Therapy as a condition
Guest12:17 p.m.
QUESTION: I once made therapy a condition of staying with a partner. The partner did get into therapy (desperately didn't want to have to leave our home), but did it for my reasons not theirs. Of course it didn't go the way I hoped. The person has to be in therapy for their own reasons, not on someone else's orders. My then-partner tried and did make a little progress but not with the issues that had led me to set that condition. Later (after we broke up), my ex started therapy for other reasons--their own reasons--and made much more progress over a period of a few years. I learned a lot of lessons from that relationship and breakup in terms of what is mine and what is the other person's. I would never ever set that condition again.

Needing a break doesn't mean I don't care
Guest12:17 p.m.
QUESTION: Advice I give to everyone: pick your lane. Pick your movement or issue and focus your time, energy, attention, money there. Trust that the people in the other movements are doing their work in their lane just like you're doing work in yours. They will tap you in when they need you; you will tap them in when you need them. One of us can't solve every problem -- and if we spend all our time trying to solve everything, we'll be too burnt out to solve anything. Also rest and joy are resistance :)

Or talking to a therapist or other medical professional to see what possibilities are out there to improve things for both of you? Where is the line?
Guest12:59 p.m.
QUESTION: Where is the line between a reasonable ask of a partner and asking them to change who they are?

I'm not talking about abuse - I'm talking about (for example), a complete one-sided overhaul of a communication style that otherwise works in other areas of their life (both personally and professionally).

I've been struggling with this for over a year - both in and out of therapy - and still don't feel like I have a good answer for this. I feel like my spouse married a direct, confident, assertive, gets-stuff-done person and then seems to resent the fact that their spouse is direct, confident, assertive, and gets-stuff-done.

I also recognize that it can be a lot to be with a person like me, and that there's (always) room for me to improve as a person and inject more thoughtfulness into my communication. I think I've gotten a lot better at asking questions and reigning in places where assertiveness could veer into steamrolling.

But I also don't think that change in communication can be one sided, and he resists my (and our couples therapist's) ongoing point that we need to meet in the middle on communication changes because there's two of us involved.

Any insight on the line between reasonable behavior modification vs change who you fundamentally are would be appreciated. I'm willing to - and have - done the former, but I'm not willing to do the later, and I'm starting to suspect that's where we're stuck.

And I am so, so, tired and have even less energy than normal to navigate this given *gestures vaguely.*
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Hard to answer, because such a line is personal, of course.

But i think there is a fair way to look at it generally that takes into account different personalities.

Being human has two types of "work." There's the work of life management, such as chores and a job and keeping cars safe and registered. Then there's the work of self-management, which includes hygiene, emotional regulation, social awareness. Oversimplified, but I'm too slow as it is.

When you're with someone, you need to manage your workload in both. Hold up your share of the chores and other life management, and not let the self-management slide.

Good so far.

To my mind, a couple isn't working when being in it demands too much work in either category. Now, their life work might just be crazy -- young kids, etc. -- and as long as they're sharing it and both equally flat out exhausted with "too much" work, then okay. That's temporary and they can get through it, and true sharing means they're a good pair. It's lopsidedness that will doom them -- when one is doing everything and the other says "You wanted kids I didn't!" or whatever.

That is also true of self-management work. If you're kind of a mess and your partner asks you to get some help, and you're in therapy and it's hard, but you're learning healthier ways, then your self-management work will go up for a bit but ultimately go down as you get healthier. That's a good thing.

If instead you're being asked by your partner to, basically, suppress yourself into a little box (because you're "loud" or whatever), then you're going to work yourself to exhaustion on the self-management side, trying to be someone you're not.

That, to me, is the line: when being with someone doesn't make your life better, but instead drains or exhausts you (in a chore sense or self-management sense), with no prospect of improvement.

Hope that helps.

Bad Wife?
Guest1:18 p.m.
QUESTION: Women in my family have the luck of going grey in their late 30's. I'm 41 and just about completely grey. Our kids are 10 and 8. My husband is asking me to dye my hair as most of the women in my family do, but I really am resisting. Its expensive, time consuming, and I don't like the chemicals (no shade to women who dye....just not me). My husband does have social anxiety and since we met in college, there are things I do to help both of us in public - I'm aware he needs to sit at the end of a row or table, know where two exits are at all times, and will review party list of names/jobs/relationships before we go out. If a place is too loud/crowded, we leave or I plan to go alone. He's saying that doesn't like the grey because of what people think - they could think I'm the kids' grandmother or that I am older than I am. He is asking me to consider dying my hair - for him. He sees it as another small thing to do to make him comfortable. He has even said good partners, good wives do things for one another and he has asked me to "strongly consider" dying my hair. He says that he thinks once I'm in my 50's everyone else will have grey. Great - so I'm looking at 15yrs of dying my hair? I'm I a bad wife for not doing it? An uncaring person if this really could help him feel more comfortable socially and ease outings?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Yeah no. Sorry husband. Feeling better with multiple exits is not the same as "I need my wife to look a certain version of hot." But you can tell him I said nice try üôÑ

Plus, if we're going to address the anxiety here seriously, making him comfortable is not the answer. His facing your gray and getting used to it is the winner. Anxiety is fear of something that isn't happening. Accommodating an anxiety = feeding a fear of something that isn't happening. Meaning, feeding an irrational fear.

Meaning, if he is not in treatment first, and then coming up with evidence-based solutions second, then I'm side-eyeing the effectiveness of the lot of it.

Here are my biases:

1. I think pretty gray hair looks just as pretty as any other color hair.

2. Hair dye maintenance is a colossal butt pain.

3. I think fewer dye jobs in real life are pretty or persuasive than we want (a direct extension of 2, so I feel bad saying 3, since it's really just that people have other priorities for their time and $ than to get perfect gray cover, so good for them, and they certainly don't need me critiquing the results).

Anxiety fact-check is readily available. Making his world smaller-easier is exactly counter to the recommended approach.

53
Feel bad all the time
Guest1:41 p.m.
QUESTION: Why do I feel bad about myself all the time? I feel regret for things in my past, take things personally, obsess on what I may have said wrong, etc. I‚Äôm an accomplished person now retired and hate perseverating like this. I struggle to break out of this negative internal dialogue. Any tips?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Please make an appointment to see your primary care person, then follow the breadcrumbs from there. There is no need to resign yourself to suffering and then tough this out as if it can only be about personal failings. Perseveration can be a symptom of a medical issue -- or something else, but the medical issue has the lowest barrier to entry as far as exploring causes, plus it's the logical starting point for that kind of detective work.

Meantime, as your health permits, consider also adding physical activity and mind-consuming activities. Busy is good. If you're moving forward on the two paths -- pursuing diagnosis plus keeping occupied with purpose -- you might find some quick relief.

Paige

Is it possible to be a happy working mom?
Guest Oct 24, 11:08 a.m.
QUESTION: Hi Carolyn, here goes. Is it possible to be a happy mom who works a 9-to-5 job?

I always assumed I would stay home with my kids, at least during the early years. That is how I was raised‚Äîmy mom was home throughout our entire childhoods and eventually (when my youngest sibling was 18) went to go work as an in-home aide. On reflection, she really disliked working as an aide and often made comments about wishing she had other options. Those comments were in my head as I sought out my first adult jobs. I work in HR at a midsize company and my work is predictable but challenging.

My husband and I are expecting our first baby in March and I'm starting to plan for parental leave and beyond. I am finding that, despite my original plans, I don't want to leave my job. It also doesn't make sense right now, because I bring in roughly 60% of our family's income (my husband is a schoolteacher).

Most of my friends either don't have kids yet or are stay-at-home moms. I don't have a great model for what working motherhood works like except in my coworkers, who seem frazzled and complain about their kids all the time. Obviously it's because mine is still in utero, but I can't imagine complaining that bitterly about my baby and assume it has to do with the competing demands of their lives.

I guess my overall question is whether there are working moms who are fulfilled the way stay-at-home moms are, or whether I'm letting money make a decision for me that should be moral/emotional?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist

ANSWER: I have so many answers fighting to get out, they're all jammed into each other. I'm just going to pull them out one at a time and probably get the order all wrong.

First, I suggest you talk to your co-workers vs. observe them. Not in a judging way, either -- do not , do not come at them with, "I can't imagine complaining that bitterly about my baby." Because defenses will go up so fast they may knock you backward. If you approach in an "obviously there's some exaggeration here but you're scaring me" way, and, "I'd love to hear some good stuff," then you have a much better chance at truth. There could be an enormous gap between what they're blurting out in moments of exasperation and what they deeply feel.

Second, as long as we're doing the "most of my friends" thing -- most of my friends (vast majority) worked while they were actively rearing their kids. And not only were most of them also happy, the relatively few unhappinesses were often not related to the combination of working outside the home and being parents. So, that it might not be "possible" is astonishing to me, really. In the way that it would be astonishing to ask, "Is is possible to be a happy breather of air?"

Yes, it's challenging. Time is an issue. But being home is challenging for different reasons. Working outside the home or not isn't what makes or breaks a household. The trick is to choose what is right for your specific circumstances -- you and your partner, if you have one, and your kids, and your finances, and so on.

So, third item. If I were to construct an is-it-possible-to-be-happy scenario, I might use this phrasing: Is it possible to be happy with an "I had always assumed ___" outlook on life? Because to me, the building blocks for a satisfying life aren't in this choice or that choice or the other demographic. They're here: in openness to making a joyful, meaningful life of what comes.

You're a few steps into that with your epiphany that you don't want to leave your job. Despite what you "had always assumed," the life course you're on has its own ideas.

So, keep going with that. Keep challenging your assumptions with reality. Your husband, for example, as a teacher, might have a schedule that aligns well enough with your child(ren)'s over the years to tame some of the frazzle. You may be able to hire help or organize better around some of the stress. You may find that "venting" is not your parent-bonding language; no judgment.

(click for more)
Post has 0 replies and 494 Likes
494Press Enter to expand
In the doghouse
Guest Oct 24, 11:18 a.m.
QUESTION: My brother and sister-in-law have been using me as their dog-sitter since we moved close to each other about ten years ago. I never ask them to reciprocate, since one of my dogs can be a little destructive when unsupervised; I put my dogs in a kennel when I travel.

Their dog (we‚Äôll call him Sid) is a bit of a hassle. I take care of him in my house. Sid is quite a bit older than my two dogs, so every walk turns into two‚Äîa long one for mine and a short one (distance-wise, but almost as long time-wise since he walks so slowly) for Sid. Sid needs five pills daily, eye drops, ears cleaned, teeth brushed‚Äîis not terribly cooperative for any of it.

They‚Äôve stopped giving me food for him, since my dogs eat the same kibble, but it‚Äôs expensive (about $45 per week per dog).

They‚Äôve even asked me to drop him off when they get home, since they‚Äôre so exhausted from their travels.

It used to be they would take me out to dinner for a thank-you, buy me a souvenir if it was a pleasure trip, etc., but even that seems to have stopped in the last couple of years.

I have confronted them on occasion regarding one thing or another. The first time they didn‚Äôt give me food, they said, ‚Äúit‚Äôs just for the weekend.‚Äù The next time: ‚Äúwe have more at our house; you can come get some if you need it‚Äù (it‚Äôs a 20-minute drive each way). Once we went out to dinner together just after I had cared for Sid for several days, which I thought was my thank-you dinner. When they asked me to split the check, I ‚Äújoked‚Äù that taking care of Sid for the weekend didn‚Äôt pay as well as it used to. They did not say, ‚Äúoh, right, never mind then,‚Äù but collected their $60 (kennels in our area cost $40-$65 per night).

We‚Äôre all rather well-off, and on the one hand, it feels churlish to insist on them buying me dinner and gifts, or to refuse to pay for Sid‚Äôs food. But on the other hand, I feel I‚Äôm being taken completely for granted. Rather than a close relative who‚Äôs taking care of their dog for free, they‚Äôre treating me like some sort of expensive concierge pet-sitting service, or as if they‚Äôre doing me a huge favor when I take care of Sid, rather than the other way around.

They know I‚Äôm not going to refuse to take care of Sid when they need me to; I wouldn‚Äôt do that to Sid (he‚Äôs never been in a kennel in his life, and can‚Äôt start at his age). So how do I address this?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist

ANSWER: Draw lines where you hold the pen. Never, for example, drop off Sid for them. I mean really. I expect that will mean you have him an extra day, which will then mean more work than the drive would have been, but if the point is to make a point, then that will make your point.

Partner OCD / State of Our House
Guest Oct 24, 11:50 a.m.
QUESTION: Dear Carolyn,

This is my second time writing in. I wrote over the summer about my husband‚Äôs OCD (I was ‚ÄúWalking on Eggshells‚Äù).

My husband has a high-stress job, and together we‚Äôre raising four kids in a busy, blended household. He‚Äôs a wonderful man and deeply committed to our family, but he has strong OCD tendencies ‚Äî his way of controlling what he can when everything else feels chaotic.

Here‚Äôs the hard part: when he‚Äôs on his way home from work or a trip, I can feel my body tense. I rush to clean the counters, vacuum, put away dishes, make sure no toys are out ‚Äî all before he walks through the door. If anything is out of place (a cup in the sink, a blanket on the couch, a toy in the driveway), he immediately starts straightening and cleaning, often before saying hello.

By nature, I‚Äôm more relaxed about clutter, but over time I‚Äôve come his way because I know how important order is to him. We‚Äôre both tidy now, but I feel like I‚Äôm living under constant pressure to keep things perfect so he doesn‚Äôt spiral into cleaning mode. It‚Äôs exhausting, and I hate that I sometimes dread his return because of the energy shift it brings.

For a while, we even had cleaners come every other week, but that ended up creating more stress than relief ‚Äî if a plant was moved half an inch or a picture frame slightly askew, it really bothered him. I managed them, and eventually it became more work than it was worth.

I also worry about what this teaches our kids ‚Äî that a toy on the floor or crumbs under a chair are somehow ‚Äúwrong.‚Äù

We‚Äôve talked about it many times, but not much changes. He refuses counseling, saying he‚Äôll ‚Äúwork on it,‚Äù but I don‚Äôt see real progress. Medicine isn‚Äôt something he‚Äôs willing to consider either. I love him and want to be understanding, but this dynamic feels heavy and unsustainable.

How can I support him without losing my peace ‚Äî or turning our home into a stress zone for everyone else?

Signed,
Walking on Eggshells (and Crumbs)
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist

ANSWER: Obv you can't make him go. But you can present to him this breakdown of the unmet terms of the deal, and that the result is his untreated anxiety is affecting the entire family. This can't be what he wants -- can it? Ask him. Acknowledge that it's a hard threshold to cross for anyone, and you're sympathetic. But this stress is hard too and the alternatives only get harder.

You may have to initiate those alternatives -- separation for the kids' sake -- but that is many steps away and I hope wont' be necessary, once you make it clear that his getting help is not negotiable now that his anxiety is affecting all and his self-help plan is stalled.

Not wild about my daughter‚Äôs fianc√©
Guest Oct 24, 11:51 a.m.
QUESTION: Oldest daughter is engaged to a young man; they have been dating three years. She is an accomplished professional with degrees to match, and he hasn‚Äôt come close to finishing undergrad and works as a grocery manager for a chain store. They are worlds apart (suburb vs. country living). He seems a bit on the spectrum and he takes everything personally. Both see therapists. Talking to him is difficult and we haven‚Äôt conversed in over a year. He wanted my blessings to marry my daughter via a phone call. He thinks my nose is out of joint.My husband and I are concerned with red flags that she will settle on this person and they are so disparately worlds apart that it is scary to see how she might be left holding the bag. She still lives at home but pays rent. Her therapist hasn‚Äôt nudged her to grow and evolve like a person her age should‚Ä¶.she is socially awkward and doesn‚Äôt seem to want to do much beyond work, home and visiting her boyfriend. She wants to buy a house; we have suggested she rent and be I. Her own for awhile. i don‚Äôt have tough conversations anymore because it ends up in a screaming match.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist

ANSWER: By the end, you're admitting (I don't think that's too strong a word) that your daughter has serious challenges of her own ... but kind of blaming her therapist for them. And having screaming matches with your kid about how you think she should be living her life.

I want you to reread because maybe then you'll see all the finger pointing, all the things you think everyone else is doing wrong. With their lives -- not yours.

Then I hope you will see that as your cue to excuse yourself from this picture. Talk to your own therapist if that will help you with the difficult feelings. Yes, it's hard to watch people make choices that you think will make their lives harder. But they're not your choices to make. Not anymore.

And the more you try to impose your will on your kid, the more urgent you make it for her to push back against you. And the worst decisions we make (collective "we") are always the pushes back against a smothering authority.

Give her the space any adult deserves. Give her the trust you would have wanted to have at her age -- even if you don't think she's ready for it yet, or has earned it. Sometimes you have to put it there because it's (past) time, and let them figure it out.

Re Happy Working Mom

QUESTION: The thing that struck me is that you work in HR, so consider that you are probably more likely to hear complaints than other professions and I don't even mean in a professional capacity. This may be going on here. So don't assume that these people are miserable parents constantly complaining. You might just seem like a friendly outlet that will let them vent for short bursts.

Former gym rat
Guest Oct 24, 12:16 p.m.
QUESTION: Prior to the pandemic, I was a huge gym rat and used to work out all the time. I got out of it when the gym closed and never went back. But my husband will. not. let. it. go. He keeps nagging me. (I have not gained weight, changed size or anything else.) I have used "Not happening. Next question." and he has now stooped to trying to embarrass me about this by mentioning it in front of friends who are all "But you LOVED to work out!" Yeah, and now I don't. Ugh.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist

ANSWER: Or, simpler, just leave. The room, party, restaurant, bar, whatever. Evening over.

Time to have this out with him I guess:

"What is it going to take for this to stop? Do I have to ask you something over and over, even though I have the answer, until you break? Do I have to embarrass you in front of our friends? Do I have to say out loud to you, 'Yes, I KNOW you liked me better when I was a gym rat, I KNOW you are really bugged that I don't go any more, but I am still not going back' -- will that do it? What? Please tell me so I can be free of this b-------."

The weight/size thing is a red herring. Even if you did change some, this is a one "back-off" issue. You get to manage yourself as you see fit.

Where a spouse gets to express concern is in engagement-with-life management, and even then, it's both nuanced and about you (you-indiv and you as a couple). Meaning, this I-want-so-I-embarrass-you crap would never fly.

SAHM v. Working Mom

Re: SAHM happiness
Guest Oct 24, 1:11 p.m.
QUESTION: I‚Äôm maybe over rotating but this phrasing was triggering: that this decision should be ‚ÄúMORAL‚Äù/emotional. It feeds into the mommy wars and it‚Äôs just so destructive to think that any choice (working, not working, part time working) has inherently higher MORAL value. They are both valid, good choices.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist

ANSWER: 2. I think we can make moral calculations without Princess Bride-rolling down the slope into judging everyone else. (I am a cockeyed optimist, what can I say.) One family might decide it's morally imperative to have Mother home -- and yes, that has bad mommy war history. But one might also see moral value in having Dad home, to show that role model -- especially for a baby boy? Or for a girl? (Discuss.) One might see moral weight in having both parents contribute paid work. Etc. It's all on the table, I think -- when it can be of course. Practical considerations often make the rest of it moot.

Feeling Unprotected and Losing Trust in My Marriage
Guest Oct 17, 11:02 a.m.
QUESTION: I‚Äôve been married for two years (together for four), and while my husband is a great person in many ways, the biggest strain on our relationship has been his family. They‚Äôve consistently disrespected me, and despite my husband saying he understands, he rarely takes firm action. He tends to avoid conflict and often gives in to their requests‚Äîespecially financial ones‚Äîwhich has made me feel unprotected and unseen.

We tried couples therapy, but it didn‚Äôt help much, because I don‚Äôt think the counselor was a good fit. I‚Äôve communicated my boundaries repeatedly, yet he continues to prioritize keeping the peace with his family over my comfort. Recently, I suggested opening a joint account to rebuild trust and create transparency, but he refused, saying he didn‚Äôt want ‚Äúlittle fights about purchases.‚Äù But when I asked if I could have the passwords to his accounts and he said that was fine.

I‚Äôm exhausted from being the only one fighting for our marriage. I still care about him, but I‚Äôm starting to wonder if we‚Äôre simply incompatible‚Äîif I‚Äôm holding on out of love or fear of leaving. I want peace and emotional safety, but I don‚Äôt know if I can have that here anymore.

What would you do in my situation? Can a marriage survive when one partner won‚Äôt fully protect you from their family? Do I need to be stronger? I‚Äôm afraid of the dynamic if we have a child.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Whoo. I'm uncomfortable answering a question with the central conflict presented in such vague terms. Your husband's family is hitting him up for cash, I take it? Is he giving it to them without running it by you first? Is it $5, $500, $5,000? I can see different answers at the extremes. Plus, I can see "counselor wasn't a good fit" = counselor wasn't good, or counselor wasn't agreeing with you.

However, I can answer part of your, "What would you do in my situation?" especially from my perch in my late 50s: I wouldn't go into anything serious with someone conflict averse. Not a large purchase, not a child, not a business deal, maybe not even plans on Saturday night -- nothing where our interests were combined and I had to count on this person to stand up for those interests. Because I know now that I couldn't.

So, if you are confident that your husband is beholden to his family, and that you are the one he's comfortable disappointing, then you need to say that to him now.

Friend back with abuser - update
Guest Oct 17, 11:16 a.m.
QUESTION: Original poster of last week's chat question here. My friend was being nicer...but she g ot kicked out from her main friendgroup and now she's being isolated, cold, and distant. I'm trying to be a safe presence like everybody said, but it's getting more and more challenging as she now just ghosts people. Not sure how to handle it:(thank you all for supporting me last time.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: You're welcome.

I am not going back to the transcript to check details, so this will be a general answer:

You handle it by making regular check-ins with the expectation that you will not get regular-friend reciprocation out of it. Meaning, she will ghost you, or take a kind of rudely long time to respond, or respond defensively, or respond right away by leaning on you hard as if you've talked every day for years when in fact this is the first time she's reached out in months. Because, think of it this way, all her emotional dials and gauges are in their red "unhealthy" zones or else she wouldn't be where she is.

What you're offering isn't a reciprocal friendship (yet) -- it's an escape plan. When she's ready for it. And for it to be effective, what she needs is to get accustomed enough to your presence to think of you when she's in a full cortisol event.

This is the role a person signs up for, if they want to help a loved one get out of an abusive relationship who is still in the cycle of going back and telling themselves no really, it will be different this time.

Should I fake grief I'm not feeling
Guest Oct 17, 11:41 a.m.
QUESTION: My father died 2 weeks ago very suddenly and unexpectedly. I‚Äôm not actually grieving since we‚Äôd been estranged for the past 3 years. My entire childhood he was either neglectful or emotionally abusive. In plain language he either ignored me or let me know what a disappointment I was to him since I wasn‚Äôt outgoing or popular. I was 12 when my mom found out he was cheating because he got his girlfriend pregnant. He moved out of the state with his new wife and didn‚Äôt bother visiting me or letting me visit him for the next 5 years. When I finally was allowed to visit him and his new family I couldn‚Äôt believe how affectionate and involved he was with my half-sister. When she was 10, the difference in the way he treated her and me at that age was so stark, I had to I talk to him about it. He told me that he was able to love my sister unconditionally, like he never could with me. It was devastating to hear but he blamed it on his ‚Äústage of life‚Äù and said I shouldn‚Äôt take it personally. How do you not take that personally? That was it for me, I stopped seeing or communicating with him. He either didn‚Äôt notice or didn‚Äôt care. I stayed in contact with my sister by sending birthday/Christmas gifts, texting, Facetime, and on Instagram. Since our dad died, my sister has been calling/texting me nonstop, wanting to pour out her grief on someone who ‚Äúunderstands‚Äù. Her parents separated last year (infidelity again) so her mother isn‚Äôt exactly heartbroken. I love my sister but I can‚Äôt possibly share in her grief even a little bit. The only grief I‚Äôm feeling is for the father I never had. Especially since I found out that my father told my sister that my mom was to blame for our recent estrangement. That lie would be funny if it weren't infuriating since my mom never even thinks or talks about him. Do I need to fake grief I‚Äôm not feeling for my sister‚Äôs sake?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Yikes.

No, you do not have to fake grief you do not feel, to comfort the sister you do not identify with.

I do appreciate that you're considering it. It is a kindness toward her that shows the true extent of your grace. Clearly she is not to blame for any harm your father did to you. But still, so many people struggle to separate their difficult feelings so effectively. Many wouldn't even know their sister in this situation, much less be in regular touch, having reached a "Just can't" point with it all.

So if anyone can thread the needle, I'm thinking you can -- of letting her know you feel for her in her grief, and understand in the sense of knowing what she has lost, but not in the sense of having had the same father she did.

From there, you can offer her whatever you're willing. There's lots of range there, 0 to full gusher, and it's entirely up to you. The zero is to ask her kindly to direct her grief to another source of support, "since I'm going through my own process right now"; or saying you can listen as long as it's understood that the two of you are not in the same place; or you can set a time to check in once a week, or whatever. It really is your call.

I am sorry for your loss in your childhood.

My FIL chose ‚ÄúGrandaddy‚Äù, and our 4 year old went with it. A few months ago our second, now 2, started calling him ‚ÄúGrandy‚Äù. Assuming that she would grow out of it eventually, we didn‚Äôt make a big deal out of it, just continued using his preferred when we spoke of/to him. But, our 4 yo has started using the shortened name, too. We know it bothers him, so how should we handle it?
avatar
Carolyn Hax

ANSWER: 1. He can correct your 4-year-old, if it bothers him. "I prefer you call me Grandaddy, thank you. Like you used to. Your [brother/sister] can't pronounce it yet, which is why Grandy is okay." You also keep referring to him as Grandaddy, to him and about him to both of your children, out of respect to your FIL, but it is not your job to correct your child on this.

2. Is he always this much of a tool? There is no gift more precious on earth than a grandchild's spontaneous naming. Smh.

Reply to: "Feeling Unprotected and Losing Trust in My Marriage"
Guest Oct 17, 12:26 p.m.
QUESTION: Regarding money and accounts, it would be helpful to know if you both have your own careers / incomes and/or if one or both of you have your own money from before the relationship started.

However, for almost 50 years spanning three major relationships, my rule has always been: "There are three types of money: My money, your money, and our money. We have frank conversations regarding how much money is needed to run the family, pay the mortgage, etc., and where that money is going to come from. And that balance can and probably will change over time. However, once we have a current agreement, we have three bank accounts: Mine, Yours, and Ours. As long as the 'Ours' account is properly and fairly funded, what happens with the money in 'My' account and 'Your' account is only the concern of that individual. I am not entitled to make judgments about your spending and you are not entitled to make judgments about my spending. However, for emergency and life-event situations, we do always make sure to share logins/passwords on the accounts."

Lastly if you don't think the therapist was a good fit, find a different one, even if your husband won't participate (but I think he probably will). And if the next one also does not seem like a good fit, in consultation with your husband if he is participating, find yet another therapist. Keep looking until you get it right. Though it may not be or feel "fair", you are going to have to drive this train.

Wishing you the best!
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: [Found it!]

Thanks for the useful steps.

Though I would put a cap on the number of therapists I'd load into the Pez dispenser, trying to find one who could express things just so to finally close the uncloseable gap.

Not fake grief
Guest Oct 17, 12:32 p.m.
QUESTION: Even in intact families, the loss of a parent can hit differently - each child‚Äôs relationship with each parent can be so different. Your grief, over the father you never had, is real grief, just as real as hers is; I think it‚Äôs important for you to acknowledge that and honor it, just as you can honor and acknowledge your sister‚Äôs grief. As Carolyn suggests, I think that is the needle you can thread - acknowledge that you‚Äôre both grieving, but for different reasons, since you had very different relationships with your father, without necessarily going into any detail. Bungee Jumper Dad to Be
Guest Oct 17, 12:48 p.m.
QUESTION: As I read this week's question from a very pregnant woman so furious at her husband, I thought back to when I was pregnant and how mad I got at my husband when he didn't make it to a doctors appointment with me. In fairness, he was super supportive and went to like 90% of the appointments. So why was I so mad? Probably because it showed that at the end of the day, he could walk away (have the beer, eat the sushi etc etc) and I couldn't. So maybe that's the root of at least some of her anger - he can tap out, go on the wild trip, act like there's no baby coming and she.. just can't. The biology isn't fair on this one.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Right, it's not. That cuts both ways, of course. The feeling of being pregnant is just astonishing intimacy with ... life, really, but specifically with the coming new member of the family. It absolutely comes at a cost, just as that freedom to bungee jump comes at a cost.

Comfort with the tradeoffs is why a lot of fits of anger don't happen, at missed appointments or with ridiculous last hurrahs or whatever.

When they do happen, they're not necessarily bad or wrong. They've just got lots of possible explanations -- because there's so much complexity to this stage of life, so much change, and responsibility, so much riding on another person, so much anxiety swirling around in there. Plus, like I said, some hormones adding rocket fuel to it all. So which one is the root of the anger? Any one of them could do it, depending on the couple's history.

Anger is a tap on the shoulder that there's something unexplored or unexpressed that might need some attention. Certainly some walking off, first, to get enough feelings out of the way to figure out what it is.

Recovering after fianc√© cheats.
Guest Oct 17, 1:25 p.m.
QUESTION: My fianc√© of three years cheated on me and immediately pleaded depression, anxiety, and disassociation. She started therapy and said that she was losing herself in our relationship and needed to work on herself, so we're essentially are broken-up while she does this work. I continue to support her (she is in school with no resources to continue if I don't) as I committed to doing. It's been three months now and she has been consistent in saying that she can't be in a relationship with anyone until she learns to love herself.

I've had a long journey through therapy and cheated myself under similar circumstances in a prior relationship, so I have more understanding than most and I also believe that people can change and learn if they are open with themselves.

But, I feel like she avoiding dealing with the hurt and trauma that she caused me and I don't know how to call this out while being supportive of her process. She's said the expected "I'm sorry for causing you pain by my actions", but we have not done any work on taking accountability, owning decisions, or figuring out where things fell apart that led to the cheating. As each day goes by, I feel like she's "getting away with it" under the guise of doing the work on herself.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: If you are broken up, then she did not get away with it. The consequence was losing you. You are merely honoring a prior commitment toward her schooling (the rest of which I will leave to the lawyers, though I have to think you have other options there if you want them).

If you are merely apart while she goes through school and counseling, with the understanding that you are still engaged, then you are not broken up. In that case, I see your point of her not really having consequences.

That's just the surface-y stuff. I mention it, though, because it seems really mushy, and mushiness always makes things worse. You're looking to her to figure out where you stand, and she seems to be telling you in multiple ways that she can't even figure out who she is. So my suggestion there is to treat yourself as someone who is broken up. It's cleaner, it makes sense (her getting lost to you doesn't sound promising regardless), it's Square 1.

Meanwhile, tho, you're leaving out the inner stuff completely: conscience. Feeling utterly regretful/bad/shamed/worthless/contemptible, just a sampling off the cheaters-who-don't-love-themselves menu, is not my idea of "getting away with" anything, so if she feels terrible, then she's paying dearly for "the hurt and trauma that she caused" you.

She's not saying it to you, maybe, but, back to the beginning: Taking her at her word, her issue was a feeling of self-erasure around you. That, to me, is a person who needs to feel a lot stronger before she's coming to you with anything. Certainly more than a few months.

Meanwhile 2, your inner stuff. If you two indeed are broken up, then your "figuring out where things fell apart" process is yours to do now on your time. Stop looking to her fix it, or you. Anyway, broken up officially or not, she's "consistent" in telling you: She can't. So stop asking. You were betrayed, yes. But whether she "owes" you is immaterial.

Your topic line is "Recovering after fiancee cheats." Recovering is about you, not her. Keep that in mind so you don't lose your focus. Step back, stop looking to be made whole, work (again) on you.

In-law correcting grandkids
Guest Oct 3, 11:05 a.m.
QUESTION: Hi Carolyn & Nuts! I have an in-law question. I will preface by saying my MIL and I do not get along nor see eye-to-eye. I avoid her but my husband and small children (1 & 4) go to visit his parents when I'm travelling for work. Everyone wins. On the rare occasion I do visit with them, how do I handle my MIL correcting my children? I'm trying to understand the different balance of "my house, my rules" with "my children, my decision". For example, she wants the 4 yo to sit at the table to eat his whole dinner and not leave until everyone is finished, asked to be excused, etc. In theory, I agree and we are working to instil manners (please tell me boys eventually chew with their mouth closed without constant reminders!) but he's 4 and sitting longer than 10 minutes while adults talk is boring. It also grates me to have her correcting my children when it's not her job. Should I tell my children that Grandma has different rules at her house and to listen to her or should I push back when she corrects my children? She is not the type of person one can have a rational conversation with, so it's either stay quiet or push back in the moment.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I need a bomb suit to take apart this question.

First, please let go of the idea that no one else can correct your kids but you. Parents who give stinkeye to anyone who dares say "no" to their preciouses have always bugged me. You and your husband are the top of the discipline chain, yes, and therefore you get the last word. However, the "village" concept is a good and necessary one that absolutely falls apart if you refuse to empower the village to tell your kid "no" sometimes.

I (really really) get how hard it is to grant that power to someone you don't like or trust. But it is worth it to take the deep breaths and make yourself apply an across the board standard of village access. Meaning, the village can correct your kid, even villagers you loathe, as long as the correction itself is not actively harmful -- because the institution of the village is worth supporting. And yes, that includes telling your kids (and reminding often) that rules in Place A are different from rules in Place B. That's just life. Home to home, school to home, etc. There's nuance to who's in charge, but you'll get there over time.

There are also definitely times to step in and protect your kid from people who undermine your childrearing methods or who push really bad, unhealthy or outdated methods themselves. The actively-harmful exception. Your MIL has one that I see immediately -- the "eat his whole dinner" thing is a resounding NOPE, I don't care whose table it is. Insisting kids push food down past their satiety point is just so ridiculously moronic and unhealthy that it has to be a hill you die on. Food issues are notoriously hard to "uninstall" once they're in there (since you can't just quit food) so make sure your husband is extra super crystal clear on this too. No clean plate BS ever. Omg.

I am once again reminded how hard it is to type when I'm flapping my arms in exasperation.

BTW, sounds like your kids will quickly find MIL visits stultifying, which means you're going to win this one. Don't overplay your hand.

Suicidal teen
Guest Oct 3, 11:31 a.m.
QUESTION: My husband and I recently discovered that our young teen is depressed and has contemplated suicide. I found out by going through their phone after noticing they seemed ‚Äúoff‚Äù and didn‚Äôt want to talk to me. Teen feels betrayed and angry that I went through their phone. We took them to their pediatrician for an evaluation, and teen refused to talk at all or to consider an antidepressant or nonmedical interventions. We went to a therapist twice and teen refused to speak at all. I have tried drawing teen out, waiting patiently, explaining how suicide cannot be undone and we will get through this together, they are not alone‚Ä¶but nothing seems to get through. Teen did confirm that there was no event that triggered this. They‚Äôre just sad. They did say talking to their friends helps. But I don‚Äôt know what to do. I‚Äôm barely sleeping and every time teen is out of sight I‚Äôm wondering if they‚Äôre ok. I asked if they‚Äôd see a different therapist and got completely shut down. I am at a loss and don‚Äôt know how we continue in this liminal space where teen is suffering but refusing help.

My husband and I spend time with our teen, doing what they enjoy but also luring them outside for walks. It‚Äôs met with a lot of resistance but we‚Äôre trying.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Raising kids is a scary business -- you know that famous description, it's like having your heart out walking around in someone else's body? -- and what you describe here is just peak terrifying. I am so sorry all of you are going through this. The combination of the pain you're in, the fear and the sense of helplessness would keep anyone up at night.

Since your teen refuses to talk to a therapist, it's all the more imperative that you and your husband work with one, asap. One who has the training and experience to work with troubled adolescents. This is where you get your guidance to work with your kid. I would advise you to get this counsel even if your teen had agreed to talk to someone. Being a parent ends up being about 99 percent winging it on the fly, but there are times when you really need professional instructions, and this is one of them.

Something else that will be a therapist question but that I want to plant in your mind upfront: The phone is a problem. I know I don't know this, but I know. The link between it and depression and teens is established, for one -- and you say "young" teen, which means an extra degree of vulnerability. Talking to their friends helps because that is human. That is what we are more prepared to handle. I note this as a therapist question because I don't want to send you barreling in after their phone on the strength of my warning here. Proceed with licensed guidance. But until you get there, yes to the spending time together, on other things, outside, etc., off the digital feed.

Also -- get eyes on in school. This comes with a risk of ticking off your kid for saying too much, so you always have to weigh that against the value of more awareness. But a cautious talk with the counselor at school (ergo confidential) can be your starting point.

AITA?
Guest Oct 3, 11:51 a.m.
QUESTION: Hi, Carolyn! I recently made a spur of the moment plan to travel with a friend to Europe for an event taking place next summer. Tickets to the event, as well as travel, aren't cheap, but I can afford it and know that it will be fun. My husband was present when my friend I made these plans and immediately purchased the tickets. Later, he told me that he was hurt that I made plans for a big trip when he and I had discussed planning a big trip, as well, although we hadn't finalized our destination or time frame. I told him that the plans with my friend do not preclude or replace our plans together. At that point, he balked at the cost, saying that if I had enough money to make spontaneous plans for a pricey trip, he wished I'd put them toward a loan we took out last year for home improvements. I admit, I'm not as fiscally responsible as I'd like to be, and told him that I see his point. I am going to move some funds around so that we can pay off this debt together in the near future. Still, I think what stung him is that I do have the resources to be able to make spontaneous plans for a trip like this, although I don't do it often. We've been married for a little over a year, living together for almost 10 years, and have come up with system for managing our joint income and expenses that works for us. I typically pay from my personal account for any non-essential splurges that I want for myself. He knows that my income and investments are more than his own, due to many factors, but mostly because he raised and put two children through college, as well as suffered through an expensive divorce. I don't want to "flaunt" my ability to spend more freely than he does, but I also want to be able to do the things that I can reasonably afford even when they don't involve him. We discussed the issue the same night and he is not harboring a grudge, but what is a better way to handle this in the future?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: If he knows all about the separate splurge account, including how much is in it, and is/has always been okay with it on principle, then I don't see the issue here. It also seems as if he knew it was there when you took out the loan, so I'm a little confused on that -- are you too spendy or did you agree to keep your separate splurge money no matter what? Maybe you and he need some better communication there.

But as for the emotional side of it: His feelings were hurt, he spoke up, you talked about it, you both figured out that your trip was just the manifestation in real time of what the splurge account allows, and now there's nothing to see here. Sure, maybe having the plans happen in front of him wasn't ideal, but spontaneity is pretty great, too, and he could also have spoken up, or tugged you gently aside to say "Hey, does this affect our trip?" and you could have assured him then that it didn't.

Even in a good relationship, it doesn't seem realistic that you'd never have hurt feelings ever. More likely, when mature people do feel hurt: 1. They're going to be able to talk about it. 2. They're going to find out it was a misunderstanding. 3. Or they're going to be able to admit/forgive mistakes. 4. Or find a way to accept each other's differences.

No one needs to be TA.

Re: Suicidal teen
Guest Oct 3, 12:05 p.m.
QUESTION: I also have teens with mental health struggles, and you're right - it's terrifying. And I know that it continues to be terrifying because when my best friend was going through a tough time last year, her mom called me to ask if I thought she was going to be ok. (We are in our mid-40s.)
That same best friend was hauled off to counseling in her early teens, and she has said that she felt terrible about it because she felt like her parents were trying to "fix" her because she was the different one in the family. So I'd say that's the small piece of wisdom that I've used from her struggles in dealing with my own kids - make it clear that you're not trying to fix them or make them different. That you just want them to be comfortable in themselves.
Good luck - and don't forget you're not alone.

avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: These are all true, and I've been at this same message for almost 30 years, and it's a great point to make with kids from the youngest ages (please!), though if you get to it late then it's still a great message -- but. If a person, even a kid-person, refuses to talk to a therapist, there is not a whole lot that other people can do to make them. And such accurate and well-meaning encouragement as you suggest, to someone who has dug in, can backfire.

There can be an involuntary commitment for someone who is at risk of harming self or others, but that's about it.

This is a major contributor to the sense of helplessness.

Sibling‚Äôs shows of grief drag me into uncomfortable spot
Guest Oct 3, 12:34 p.m.
QUESTION: Sibling has a podcast and instagram presence that appears to be geared toward creating a name for themself and developing a clientele for coaching/empowerment/creativity consulting services. It is a side project of theirs, and since our parent passed away 2 1/2 years ago, there has been a marked turn toward including grief related content and ‚Äúcelebrations‚Äù of parent.

The content comes across to me as an attempt to monetize our parent, photos of our parent and other family members (including me and my kids) and photos of our parent‚Äôs art. This includes links to a clothing site where people can buy items printed with sibling‚Äôs collages that incorporate photos of parent and parent art.

We don‚Äôt have a great relationship, and that goes back many years. I‚Äôve tried asking to go to family counseling, sibling hasn‚Äôt been willing.

Every time I see one of these posts, I get deeply uncomfortable. Do I have any standing to ask them to stop? How would I go about it without setting off a new round of fire (sib is reactive and can be nasty)?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: You don't have standing to ask them to stop podcasting, posting or talking about grief or your parent, no. It's their business, you don't have to like it. Parent's art, depends on who owns it, I would think.

But you absolutely have standing to ask sib to stop using images of you and your kids. Since sib is reactive and nasty (sorry, I think we've all more than had it with that combination), I'd talk with a lawyer first to find out what recourse i have. Then, with that in my pocket, I'd talk to sibling myself, and go to my the legal Plan B only if I had to and still wanted to.

Once I was sure about the photos, I'd unfollow this sib's venture entirely after that. I can't see why you'd pay attention to something nonessential that upsets you. Hit "unfollow" and it stops, magic.

Eldersitting
Guest Oct 3, 1:12 p.m.
QUESTION: We are once again in the thick of fall sports season. My kids are finally old enough that when they aren‚Äôt on the field, they watch, hang out with friends, and I can relax, actually watch the games, chat with parent friends, and decompress a bit from work, shuttling kids around, cooking, laundry, etc. my in-laws recently relocated closer to us, and they want to go to the games, virtually all of them. Due to some mild cognitive changes in my MIL and my FIL never having managed these sorts of things, they need support. They are always cold but never dress warmly enough, even with reminders. They need chairs and water and snacks, which they can‚Äôt carry. It feels like packing for toddlers again. I like them as people and have a good, if not extraordinarily close, relationship with them. But did I clarify: all the games? My husband does some assistant coaching and team managing so generally isn‚Äôt the point person for this. When I‚Äôve mildly suggested skipping a cooler evening game, they won‚Äôt hear of it: they have the link to the schedule and just show up. My husband says just don‚Äôt bring all the things, and let them adjust their attendance accordingly. I can‚Äôt watch them shiver and end up giving them my jacket, water, whatever. Can I set a limit on the number of games per weekend, or a minimum temperature, and tell them that‚Äôs it? Or do I just accept this is the highlight of their social week and keep hauling all the stuff, setting it up, etc.?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I just big-sighed this one. On the one hand, it's small stuff, it's the grandparents, it's tough to be lonely/old/slipping, it's a kindness you'll want from your kids or their partners someday. On the other hand -- really? It's all on you? You can't be hands-free for one freaking game? I do feel for you on the idea of gazing at the calendar at that mythical day when you are in charge of no one's care, then it suddenly flips forward years and years and you can't stop it üò±

So here's what I've got.

1. Your kids are old enough to chip in toward their grandparents' comfort, no? Isn't this a lesson in care, kindness, intergenerational understanding? Not every game, but one shift on a rotating schedule, a kid or kids can do the setup.

2. If your husband can't step out to help get them set up for one day of a rotation himself (can he?), then he can get involved in the stocking of supplies in 3.

3. The needs are predictable enough to pack most or all supplies in a car and leave them there. Yours, theirs, your husband's? And can you put the chairs, blankets, bags of snacks in a cart or wagon. There you go. I say if you have money to throw at the problem (it's not a ton), then start chucking.

4. The limit is fine, too. Number of games or minimum temp, fine -- but is that really up to you? I would think they could just go regardless.

For parent of suicidal teen
Guest Oct 3, 1:20 p.m.
QUESTION: I was that kid, exactly. Years later my mother told me she took me to see someone because I had stopped talking. They shuffled me around to well-dressed people sitting behind large desks, and I refused to talk with them too. Silence.

Eventually I told my mother I thought it wasn‚Äôt helping. She said ok, then we‚Äôll stop. I remember clearly thinking the time, no, you‚Äôre supposed to say let‚Äôs try something else but I couldn‚Äôt get myself to say that.

At the clinic I remember being walked past art therapy rooms and thinking ‚Äòthat looks interesting‚Äô but no one ever noticed my head turn as we walked by and I was never invited in.

So maybe if the kid won‚Äôt talk, try something that doesn‚Äôt require talking and maybe talking will happen eventually. Face-to-face in an office, sitting in a straight-back chair while someone stares at you and commands you to speak wasn‚Äôt helpful.

Maybe letting the kid know that it‚Äôs not that there‚Äôs anything wrong with them. They‚Äôre learning how the world works and trying to figure out where they fit into it. Maybe it‚Äôs that there are things wrong with the world (there are!) and kid is trying to figure how to live with that.

Some sincere, credible assurance of as much confidentiality would be helpful. A kid who won‚Äôt talk to parents isn‚Äôt going to talk to a therapist who is just going to report back to the parents.

Not at all to suggest that this is this kid‚Äôs issue, but I‚Äôm trans. After decades of depression and feeling suicidal I finally dealt with that, blew my cover, changed my name and a few other things. It‚Äôs been more than 15 years since I last felt suicidal.

Carolyn Hax

Advice Columnist

I also don't see what's even remotely embarrassing about a food sensitivity or limitation. (I have firsthand experience, so.) But she can have her feelings.

That's all the makeup you owe; no need to lay burnt offerings at her feet for having the nerve to buy her a special dessert ffs.

Advice Columnist
ANSWER: So ... huh. Okay. First, small thing, I already agreed with you that the dogs/kids comparison didn't work. But I do want to add, now that you put it the way you do here, there are so many worries a pet-parent might have that a typical child-parent wouldn't. Like, getting loose in traffic or fear biting, or not being able to say what hurts, or knowing when it's time (or when you jumped too soon or took too long) to euthanize. Plus a dog will never learn to feed itself or let itself out. So I think we did better just not comparing.

Second, big thing: You left out the fact. Had I known "this relative is a conversational monopolizer," I would have been able to talk about a broken clue receptor. That's the issue. Has nothing to do with dogs, kids, hurt feelings or broken dreams or whatever else. He's not reading the room, he's just filling it -- so of course all kinds of dubious stuff comes out, Stop overthinking it; that just sends it on a journey through your own sensitivities.

My wife's therapist is driving us to divorce
Guest Oct 3, 2:08 p.m.
QUESTION: This is a weird question, but I could use some help in how to deal with my wife's reliance on her therapist.

We've been married eleven years and have always been able to work through our disagreements with candid communication, compromise and empathy. Last year, she lost her mother, and decided to start seeing a therapist to help her work through the trauma, which I supported.

But since then, she's started talking about our occasional marital disagreements with her therapist, and the therapist always seems to have a strong opinion about how it should be resolved -- almost always in her favor. So now, when we have our disagreements (which she usually brings up right after her sessions), and we start what used to be our period of communication/compromise, she says "But my therapist says...", and then she becomes totally inflexible, treating it like it's some sort of binding appellate court ruling.

I almost always disagree with the therapist, who's only getting a once-sided viewpoint and a small slice of the big picture. But my wife won't budge, so now, for the first time in our marriage, we've had a series of blow-ups due to her unwillingness to budge from whatever her therapist told her to do. (I'm sure she'd say I'm the stubborn one, unwilling to acquiesce to what her "neutral" therapist says.)

At one point I asked to talk to her therapist to at least hear my point of view before rendering her judgment, but the therapist says we'll need a different therapist if we want joint sessions, and recommended her friend. But I've resisted as these therapists are really expensive, and I don't feel like I should cede control of my marriage to them.

It reached a head where she wouldn't budge on something important to me (my hour of zoning out with a video game when I get home from work to decompress), and I asked to separate. She bawled and said we could work it out, but then the next day, after her therapy, she told me I was "gaslighting" her and that I should go if I can't "respect her boundaries," like she was reciting lines in a play.

I feel like I could save our marriage if I could free my wife from her therapist's clutches, but I also don't want to disrespect what my wife says, e.g. that the therapist is just helping her "find her voice." What do I do?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: "I've resisted [joint sessions] as these therapists are really expensive, and I don't feel like I should cede control of my marriage to them."

But you asked to separate. So, to me that says you will end your marriage rather than "cede control" of it, and pay for two homes full time rather than one hour per week of a "really expensive" professional's time.

Meanwhile, you don't know whether you "disagree with the therapist," because you are not in the room. You are hearing it from your wife afterward. You also don't know how your wife represents your arguments to her therapist. Her "one-sided viewpoint" could be pretty accurate. You're assuming she skews it to her advantage.

None of this adds up to a credible argument against therapy or this therapist. It's a lot of conjecture and nonworking math.

What it does add up to is this: You feel like your life was better, and your marriage was better for you, before your wife got therapy -- and you're angry. You want the changes reversed. And you really don't want to go to the place that you blame for breaking everything to try to fix it.

Fair assessment?

If so, then here's my response to it:

-If you want to hear how your wife represents you and your marriage in therapy;

-And hear what a trained therapist says about that;

-And to make sure your side of the story gets heard;

-And spend hourly dollars vs monthly rent;

-And try to "save our marriage" and "free my wife from her therapist's clutches" vs walk away from it;

... Then you have been offered that chance in joint sessions with another therapist. Find out for yourself what the conversation is about and join it, instead of railing about what you think they're saying about you.

It takes guts to get in there, open your mind and really listen. But if your wife wanted things to be the way they were before, then they would be. For whatever reason, what worked for you so well wasn't working as well for her, and that's not a therapist's fault. A significant death and grieving can be an eye-opening experience -- emotionally transformative. The therapist may be correlation, not causation.

Listening is the first step toward figuring out whether both of you can be happier on the other side of this.

avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Streisand Effect (google for the full description) is when the act of trying to suppress something is what makes it huge.

So, the application here = my hope that the relentless effort to dismantle the fed workforce will cause this largely invisible, unnoticed, underappreciated group of workers to achieve a kind of nerd folkloric heroism.

Wife's therapist
Guest Oct 3, 2:27 p.m.
QUESTION: Your wife's therapist is correct: She is an individual therapist, not a marriage counselor. If you want your voice to be heard within the context of your marriage in therapy, you need to go to a marriage counselor.

It is not at all uncommon for each person in the marriage to work with their own individual counselor and a marriage counselor in order to figure out how to support each individual within the marriage best.

If you don't like what's happening in her therapy, that doesn't mean the solution is to get rid of her therapist.

What's wring with being happy?
Guest Oct 3, 2:59 p.m.
QUESTION: Hi Carolyn! Lately I've noticed a lot of my friends are really down about the state of the world. I totally understand and don't judge. However, I think I'm pretty good at judging the size of my hands, and exactly how much of the world's pain I'm able to hold in them, and *spoiler alert* it's not much. Therefore, I do my best to be a good person, and accept the rest as not within my control. It works for me and I'm pretty happy.

However, this seems to really rub people the wrong way. There are quite a few friends and acquaintances I know who seem to think if you're not consumed with hatred and rage and fighting and screaming, that you're not a good person and you're not "doing enough."

I'm not really sure what, exactly, I'm expected to do to make our dear president start acting with some sense, but until I have that answer, I'm not going to make myself miserable over it. Also, hating anyone (even "fascists") is against my religion, and I don't think it helps anything, and I simply won't do it.

My question is how to talk about this? Sometimes my friends make comments that I'm "burying my head in the sand," or they'll say "you can't look at what's happening and not be upset." It feels like I'm failing some litmus test simply for taking care of my emotional wellbeing. I read the news. I'm perfectly aware of what's happening in the world. I just refuse to be consumed by it. Do you have any suggestions about how to handle the people who believe that misery is a moral imperative?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Reasons to be happy:

-It's an act of defiance I: "You can't break me"
-It's an act of defiance II: Say of your opponents, "Look at these clowns" (this entire concept via tuba). To sulk is to concede how big and powerful they are. Come on -- look at these clowns. Grab your tuba.
-It's an act of defiance III: You can't lose sight of what you're fighting to preserve.
-It's necessary. You can't be effective if you don't rest. Joy breaks are restorative.
-The happy warriors, merry band concept. You have to do the work anyway, might as well play good music and dance.
-No one will join your cause if you're miserable to be around.

That's it for now -- I have to run, this caught my eye as I was closing the window. Lmk if this moves any of your friends.
