In-laws made us feel unwelcome
Guest Sep 26, 11:03 a.m.
QUESTION: We just came back from a visit to my in-laws (FiL and StepMiL) and I don‚Äôt know what to make of it. If they were trying to make us feel unwelcome then mission accomplished. They relocated a year ago and have been bugging us to visit. Between one thing and another, we were only able to commit this month. It‚Äôs a 4-hour trip so we both took a half-day Friday and drove straight through since our 3 yo son slept most of the way. When we arrived on Friday at 6 pm they said they assumed we‚Äôd already eaten and hadn‚Äôt prepared anything. After we said we hadn‚Äôt had dinner and had rushed lunch they put out 3 slices of lunch meat, 6 slices of bread, some mustard, and a snack size pack of chips. They are not hard up for money and there was more lunch meat and bread in the packages as well as more bags of chips but that‚Äôs all they put out for all of us. I asked for some of the fruit I'd seen in the crisper and StepMiL said she was saving it for a cobbler on Sunday. For breakfast the next day, they said they usually have coffee and little sugar wafer cookies and that‚Äôs what they offered us. I asked about cereal since I‚Äôd seen some in the pantry but my FiL said they didn‚Äôt have ‚Äúenough milk‚Äù though there was a quart container in their fridge. My son was hungry, so I went out for take-out for both of us. My wife made do with cookies and coffee. Lunch was the same meager sandwiches. My wife offered to fix dinner since they had plenty of food in the fridge and freezer. They accepted the offer and that was the only full meal we had that weekend, other than us taking them out for lunch on Sunday. The cobbler never appeared.
I don‚Äôt think it‚Äôs an age thing since my parents, and my MiL and her husband practically shower us with food when we visit them and they‚Äôre all the same age (late 60s). I asked my wife why they acted like that and she said she didn‚Äôt know but we‚Äôre never visiting again. I can‚Äôt shake the feeling that they wanted us to feel unwelcome and unless I can get an explanation, I‚Äôm not going to have a good feeling towards them ever again. Is it okay to ask my FiL what was up with the lousy hosting?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I'd call that more than unwelcome -- that's downright weird.

A couple of things. First, maybe unrelated, you can't draw conclusions on "an age thing" just using a few others in the age cohort the way you did. There could be health-related problems in your in-laws' home that have an age component, and that could have been a contributor to your weird weekend (so many qualifiers there ...) -- and whether your parents or other in-laws feed you is immaterial. Likewise, you may think you know their $ situation but you do not know.

Second, weirdness to that degree is well worth a follow up, so I'm with you there -- but I would step back a bit from seeing it through such a personal lens, for now. Where you see, "They wanted us to feel unwelcome," try, "That was bizarre behavior that doesn't track at all with bugging us to visit." You have the facts of the severe food withholding and control. Wait until you have more information before you draw any conclusions about their motivations.

Third, your wife is with you on this. In most cases, the immediate family member is in a better position to communicate on weirdness. If she's willing, then she might get better answers. Plan the questions beforehand, using your past experience with them as a starting point: "I loved seeing you, but noticed things were very different on this visit," she can say. And then give specific examples -- one slice of lunch meat for dinner? Then, "I'm concerned."

This does seem to need eyes on it, I'm sorry. In a way I hope they were just mad at you (maybe for taking a whole year to get there?) and being shockingly childish about it, because then it's not an emergency; you can just peace out and not visit again.

two thoughts for today's husband who doesn't want to move back
Guest Sep 26, 11:26 a.m.
QUESTION: As somebody who lived this (my spouse and I alternated: best place for me, best place for her, then compromise city that wasn't perfect for either but above average for both and our young kid), two additions to Carolyn's advice.

While moving back and enabling her pursuit of her career is clearly the right answer, keep in mind the old home won't be like it was. You have a kid now, and so the questions of where to live and lifestyle and work/life balance, will have to consider the kid's needs. That means your partner's effort to resume her career is going to have more compromises (can't stay late in the lab because tonight is kid's talent show, etc.) There will likely be nights when she says "this isn't how I thought it would be" and your reaction better be, "OK, so how do we ensure you succeed?", and not "Oh, maybe we should go back to my happy place and you can just teach"!

To that, point consider solo counseling for you (if you prefer, a good job coach can work too) during the first year after the move, so you can work through your emotions and your loss (leaving a great job!). You're going to be balancing a job change (hard), trying to support your wife as she reboots her career (harder) and parenting (hardest?). You need to present to be a great parent, to support your partner in her tough moments, and to succeed in your new job, without carrying emotional baggage from the move/change. Oh yes -- and being a great parent is #1 -- if you and your partner don't have to worry about whether the kid is thriving, the edges of the other issues get less sharp.

Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Great points, thank you. It's not hard to see how this spirals into a, "Well it's what you wanted," situation, if they're not careful. [Read the original column here.]

The problem is when our daughter wants to know why she doesn't have a sibling or a dog. I've never told my daughter about my husband's bait and switch, and I don't think it's my responsibility to explain my husband's decisions. He's the one who pulled out the rug underneath me.

My husband thinks we need a united front on these two decisions, which is hilarious because he contradicts me in front of our child all the time. I say he made both of these decisions unilaterally so he needs to defend and explain it, not me. Why would I defend something I don't agree with for the sake of martial harmony just so he doesn't look like the Bad Guy?

What do we do?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: "We" -- you -- need to own your decision to stay with your husband once you learned about his (apparent) deception. Or you need to be true to yourself and make a different one.

I say apparent because people do change their minds, and they're allowed to. Conversations about x kids and y pets before marrying are essential, absolutely, for what they reveal about visions for the kind of life we want. I'm not minimizing that. People who say they want a stampede of life through the house will offer a different experience from the ones who say "... ehhhh no pets, maybe a child, but I have reservations about that and we need to talk about it." Both also are absolutely valid ways to go through life.

So what matters is the transparency -- and that means, as always, the main character here is character. More accurately, the integrity of the person you're weighing as a potential life partner. That tells you how much you can count on what they promise. It's not about the number of kids or pets, per se, because to treat that as a certainty is to deny how much of a say reality gets. All kinds of stuff can change, go wrong, pleasantly surprise you. (Hello, multiples.)

So -- back to your situation. You "quickly learned" his true preferences. Are you certain he did not come about that change of heart honestly? You say you were baited and switched -- which matters, because it is a towering plate of horse brownies that you "had to go along." Unless you live where divorce is illegal, you chose to go along.

If you chose to go along knowing your husband deceived you, then you -- and your dogless only child, unfortunately -- are lying in the beds you and your husband made. It's not all on him. He lied, but you stayed married to bad character, and you have nursed your resentment right up to posting this question. That is going to wear you down -- on top of the other predictable outcomes of eventually rotting out your marriage from the inside and deeply confusing your kid.

Please, get some counseling. Just you. Say out loud the foundational problem: "Shortly after we married, I discovered my husband lied to me about something huge -- and I thought I could get past it, so I stayed. But the anger is inside me every. day."

If I've called this wrong, then I'm sorry. But it's what I feel here throughout. And I don't see any answer to the, "What do I say to our kid?" question really mattering until you deal with the rage in the room.
Post has 0 replies and 716 Likes
716Press Enter to expand
Emotionally numb?
Guest Sep 26, 11:53 a.m.
QUESTION: Hi! Love your column. I've noticed myself feeling emotionally really numb to the array of things happening in the world. our nation, etc. Almost like my emotional center said, "nope, can't take any more." Is this something others are feeling? (or not-feeling?)
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Hi! Thank you.

I think somewhere between a squillion and a crap ton of people are feeling this, yes. Scientifically speaking.

It's confusing, too, because it's both protective and unnerving at the same time. Your emotional center is taking a moment to withdraw from the overwhelm, reasonably, but overall it feels weird and wrong.

[Here's the usual disclaimer that I am not a mental health professional of any kind, and that seeing one of the licensed kind can be a real benefit when things feel haywire. If you're asking, "Should I see someone?" then my answer is, why wouldn't you? It's not a 60-month contract.]

Butanyway. Something to try for the numbness -- or just the things-happening-in-the-world-ness, numb from it or not -- is beauty. Collectively experienced, if possible, like a concert or other performance, or a walk with someone at sunset, or an art exhibit. Photo albums of people you love can be healing, too -- total waterworks here if they hit me at the right moment. Or creating art. Movement helps so much; there is a proven link between physical activity and the ability to sort our feelings. What unlocks your feelings is such a personal thing, so I'll leave you to it.

I offer for your consideration, Ned's World.

808Press Enter to expand
How do I move on?
Guest Sep 26, 12:14 p.m.
QUESTION: I really need to dress more professionally, so when I came into a small inheritance this summer, I decided to treat myself to a pricy online stylist. I hated it and my wardrobe isn‚Äôt any better, plus now I feel worse about my body and clothes. I‚Äôd like to move on, but I find myself dwelling on it‚Äînot only the money, but also I feel so foolish to have gotten such high hopes about this experience. I‚Äôm angry at myself and now buying clothes just seems so much harder. I hate that I feel so powerless and bad at clothes, but I really do need better work clothing for my job (just got a promotion, btw, so I must be doing something right). How can I stop this negativity towards myself and put this behind me? It seems like such a stupid problem with everything else going on.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Whoa, whoa. Being mean to yourself is not a stupid problem because you live with you 24-7 -- and never feeling good in your body and clothes is not a stupid problem because you're in those 24-7. So breathe. Be kind. Let yourself be validly problemed.

You had the right idea with calling in reinforcements -- it just sounds as if you need a real in-person person helping you, not an online stylist. Not just anyone, either, but one you can trust on a bunch of levels. Mainly it needs to be someone you feel you can say no to, so you don't succumb to sales pressure and buy things that don't genuinely feel great. But you also need to trust this person's eye for style, and suitability to your body and environment.

I wish I knew where to send you for this unicorn, but it's not an off-the-shelf hiring situation. It depends on the stores in your area, whether they offer this service or whether you can hire someone independently as a consultant. Or maybe it's an acquaintance with fabulous taste, who knows. Even then, when you find someone, don't go all-in. Get a few things and see how right they are for you, then if you're pleased, go back for more.

Meanwhile, take the stuff you bought that you don't like and list it on resale sites (if you have the time and the give-a-damn) or heave it into a donation bin and be done with it. Purge the regrets from your closet.

In other words, on both fronts, put this behind you by giving yourself a 100 percent do-over. You have the money, and -- "with everything else going on" -- it's just clothes, right? So instead of using that truth to shame yourself, use it to give yourself a break.

Dogs=Kids?
Guest Sep 26, 1:04 p.m.
QUESTION: Carolyn, Thank you for your column and wisdom. I have several dogs and several children. Someone close to me has a dog but no children. They repeatedly claim that having dogs is "just like having kids". This seems so out of touch that it is not worth rebutting and yet it is driving me crazy. Indeed there is a responsibility for both as vulnerable beings but help me out here. What to say to them or how to place this so it doesn't keep rubbing me wrong? TIA.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I see the wisdom of letting it go as beneath serious notice -- the reasoning behind it is, at least. When you can crate children for six hours or board them for weeks when you travel, then maybe we can talk seriously about the comparison.

But someone who feels the need to assert this repeatedly is, you would think, working on some kind of knot inside emotionally. It's clearly front-of-mind. In a person close to you, that is worth sincere notice -- the thoughtful, non-defensive kind -- next time they make this claim. "That seems to be on your mind a lot." You can stop there to see how they fill the silence. Or serve it up: "Anything you want to talk about?"

Could be a few things -- weighing having kids, sad about not having kids, tired of someone's pressure campaign to have kids, tired of being treated as less-than for not having kids (never hurts to check yourself for ways you might unwittingly contribute).

Unfortunately, if it's nothing and this person is just super dog-smitten and a tad myopic, or this is their conversational tic, they're probably not going to recognize the secondary message, "OMG you will not shut up about this."

But if you've ruled out the possibility that you're reacting defensively to a person who's in pain, then I don't see why two people who are close can't have a real conversation about it.

Friend back with abuser
Guest Sep 26, 1:05 p.m.
QUESTION: My friend is back with her abuser (pretty unstable dude with an alcohol issue), and now she's cutting people off a LOT. She cut off her very best friend for telling her to leave the abuser, and now she's saying that she "might" cut me off "in a few days" over a trivial political disagreement. How do I resolve this with her, and, how do I stop feeling like s--- about it? Thanks.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: 1. You don't "resolve this with her." Your friend is pretty unstable too, it seems? So your role is not to be the savior or fixer, but instead the safe place for her to go when she decides she's ready to stabilize.

2. I don't know that you're supposed to feel okay when someone you care about is self-destructing. It's awful, scary, sad.

Maddening sometimes, too, because you look at her choices and think "Omg how can you possibly not see a disaster coming here? And why do I seem to care more about you than you do!?"

It's also hard to know when to get involved and when not to. It's easy from the keyboard to say, "She has to decide when she's ready to accept help," blah blah blah (yes, I'm looking at me), but when you're a witness, it's hard to convey just how many times you ask yourself whether you'll forever regret not doing X if Y happens. It's a kind of torture.

Still. The answer is, and can only be, to make sure your friend knows you will pick up your phone 24-7 or darn close to it, and you will get her, no questions asked. This tells her she is worthwhile, and you care, and she has a place to go for help.

Guest Sep 26, 1:14 p.m.
QUESTION: You don't even need the husband to have done a bait-and-switch to ask yourself if you're right together. I was married to a man who didn't want a second child (we had a second--a legit accident) or pets. I convinced myself it was okay because he was such a good person and we were compatible in other ways.

But it wasn't just about the kid and pets. It was about him wanting most of my attention. It was about him not tolerating a relaxed and sometimes chaotic family life. It was about his difficulty engaging with others. It was about his preference to spend money on his hobbies rather than on living things. I'm not saying those things are wrong. I'm saying they didn't, ultimately, fit my priorities.

So now I'm married to someone else, and we have a blended family and 5 rescue pets and are very involved with our community. The warning signal, way back when, was that I couldn't imagine a life without a pet and he couldn't tolerate the idea of a life with one. And it was a total tip-of-the-iceberg situation that took years to become apparent.

dogs and kids from someone who didn't end up having kids
Guest Sep 26, 1:45 p.m.
QUESTION: I never had kids, which was not what I imagined. I actually imagined having four kids! I did end up having step kids, who were a little older when I came along, and I have had dogs. I have totally had the impulse to relate to someone with kids about how I take care of my dog, and most of the time have never blurted it out loud. I think what could be in play is that you can feel so isolated from the world being someone who doesn't have kids. There is that whole, "You just don't understand -love - -commitment- - fear- etc" until you've had a child sentiment. And I know I don't understand. But I do kind of, as a feeling human being, and someone who has informally mentored lots of kids, and stepmothered two kids, and yes, raised puppies. I guess what I am saying is try to be generous that your friend may be wanting to feel included, that they are not some non-contributing, non-sacrificing member of society because they didn't have that experience.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Thanks for your message, it's on point and refreshing.

The one you cited really gets under my skin: "There is that whole, 'You just don't understand -love - -commitment- - fear- etc until you've had a child' sentiment." There is a huge difference between the whole having-dogs vs having-kids comparison, which is concrete, to the "you don't know love/commitment/fear" extensions of that reasoning, which are so abstract, individualized and totally unfounded.

To be clear, the whole comparison of experience thing is suspect to begin with. It's so rarely necessary to point out to people what they "just don't understand." If we're about to say that, we really really need to ask ourselves if this is about advancing the cause of greater understanding or puffing up ourselves.

But in the rare cases when someone does need to say something, the whole area of what someone feels is not a valid basis for comparison, because no one can possibly know that - vs. has actually done, facts only, which can be compared.

387Press Enter to expand
Fence guy update
Guest Sep 26, 1:59 p.m.
QUESTION: Hi, I'm the guy whose neighbor kids keep jumping our fence. First off, I don't know why so man y people thought my dog is the problem and not the trespassing. Our dog is a rescue who wants nothing more than to chill in her yard and doesn't even like to leave it for walks. She deserves her peaceful backyard so we're gong ahead with a 6 foot stockade fence. Thanks for taking my question
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: You're welcome, glad you wrote back with an update.

The dog vs. kids isn't a value judgment, it's a reality judgment. Kids shouldn't be in your yard. That's simple. I agree with you. Dog shouldn't be an issue. Also simple. I can even add the parenting layer: Parents should keep their kids out of your yard. Of course.

But then you factor reality into it. Kids don't have fully developed forebrains. Parents don't hover over their kids 24-7. The kids might even listen to their parents and stop jumping your fence -- yay! -- but then they have a friend over who doesn't know the rules, and jumps the fence. What I'm saying is, there are so many things you can't control about the neighbors, tweens, that side of the fence.

A dog, meanwhile, is ... well, if you've been here before, you know how I feel about dogs. But if anything goes wrong, ask cops, animal control, insurers -- there's a not-zero chance it'll be the dog's fault. Especially a big dog. Plus, you can't tell your dog the rules.

That's the long answer about my answer. The short answer is, "should" is a lousy planning strategy.

A good planning strategy is to look at what is likely to happen, then what you don't want to happen, then what forms of prevention are in your control.

Do you want the answer you want, or an answer that can work.

Moving on from fight
Guest Sep 26, 2:15 p.m.
QUESTION: My husband picked a stupid fight with me yesterday and I‚Äôm not sure how to move on. I‚Äôm 39 weeks pregnant and have had a cold all week, but I was making dinner for us when he got home late from a work happy hour. He started criticizing how I chop garlic, which he has brought up recently and I‚Äôve told him to drop it. He would not let it go last night until I lost my temper and screamed at him that he‚Äôd be sleeping somewhere else if he didn‚Äôt shut up. I said that unless it‚Äôs a safety issue (it‚Äôs not), he shouldn‚Äôt be telling another autonomous adult how to cook especially since he has no culinary training. He finally let it go after I yelled at him and made him go to the bedroom so I could cool off while I finished cooking. But we never fight like this so I‚Äôm left wondering what do I do now?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: You go back to him and say okay wt[h] was that? Because, whatever it was, it was not about garlic, and you and he need to figure that out here and now.

I'm not saying this happened here, but some people actually do pick fights when they're anxious -- it's a way of seeking release. It's also super immature, obviously. Like, find your own way to process instead of getting all up in my grill.

Now, you, presumably not one to scream? have your own pent up stress at the moment. It could just be a one-off for both of you.

But it's something to consider here. If this is in any way a pattern, then seeing it before anyone reacts can really help. Ideally the person will get some, ah, counseling to learn better stress management, but if you see the nitpicking for what it is, then you can say, "Nope what's really bugging you?" and sometimes get from A to B without the detour into the proxy argument.

Oh, and small fix: "I said that unless it‚Äôs a safety issue (it‚Äôs not), he shouldn‚Äôt be telling another autonomous adult how to cook especially since he is having dinner made for him by an enormously pregnant person while he was at happy hour."

how do people move on so fast?
Guest Sep 19, 11:05 a.m.
QUESTION: I recently found out that my ex-boyfriend has a new girlfriend. We were together for a year, which I know is not long in the grand scheme of things, but it was a really important, special, and impactful relationship to me. And to him, from everything he told me and what we shared while together (which I do not doubt that he was lying about or misrepresenting). It's only been a few months and I cannot imagine meeting or becoming involved with a new person. Most days I am still so sad. But it seems he met this new person a month or two after we broke up, and pictures of them together are already all over his social media. (A friend told me this because she thought I already knew, which I didn't -- I've removed and haven't looked at any of his social media since breaking up, because I knew it would make me too sad.) I know logically that him being with a new person can't make what our relationship was any less special to me, but it just hurts. How do people manage to move on so fast? We're in our mid/late 30s, in case it matters.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I'm sorry. People are just so, so different. About pretty much everything:

-How deeply they attach is different (you may be a 10 to his 5 in capacity).

-How any two people feel about each other is different (you may have been a 10 to his 5 in this particular relationship).

-How any two people break up is different. Some people do all the mental/emotional work of breaking up while they're still with someone, and the breakup just makes it official. Others start that clock upon breakup.

-How any two people recover from a breakup is different (maybe you were equals in capacity and attachment, but you recover through mourning and he through reattachment).

-How any two people present themselves is different. He could be more upset than you are, for all you know. It's social media, not truth serum.

-What any two people create in combination is different. What you had with this guy was impactful, special to you, that's important -- for some happy memories and for what it taught you about yourself and what you're looking for in a partner. Painful as it is, maybe this is a useful look now at some differences too big to reconcile.

Or it's just a setback as you're trying to get over him. Either way, he wasn't the guy. It's okay if you're not able to draw more from it all than that.

This is also great, we get along well and see them almost weekly for dinner, etc. A few times, mostly at our kids' birthdays, my parents and SIL's family have been together at our house. My parents interact great with SIL's two young kids (now 1 and 3). The 3 yo even calls them "Grammy and Grampy" because that's how our kids refer to them, which I think is adorable. But it's now causing an issue. SIL/my husband's parents live a few hours away, and don't travel much due to mobility issues. SIL's whole family has been no-contact with her husbands' parents for years (just for context... I agree with their reasons, not that my opinion matters). My SIL recently started talking about how nice it would be for her kids to have a routine grandparent connection and she would like for us to include her kids on outings my kids take with my parents. I feel... weird about this, like it's cutting into my kids' bond with my parents. But I asked my parents, and they said they had no interest in a closer relationship with SIL's kids. Unfortunately my SIL did not take this well. I think she's working through some grief about the lack of grandparents and now she's lashing out at me for "selfishly" keeping my parents "to myself." So... advice on handling this in a way that doesn't destroy a relationship I hope can be close again?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Wherrrrrrre is your husband in this? Where. Seriously.

This was up to your parents, and they said no. So there you go.

I do feel for your SIL. If I were in your place, I'm not sure how I'd feel -- because it would depend on all the variables, like my parents' natures and their health, my relationships with them, theirs with my kids, my kids' relationships with the other kids and the SIL and husband, mine with the SIL and the husband, husband with everyone, all that. If everyone got along, then I hope I'd be in a more-the-merrier frame of mind. But I could easily see being more circumspect.

That's why the SIL took responsibility for not flipping out if she didn't like the answer. That to me was the prerequisite to such a delicate request.

Because if the parents weren't game for any reason, then she was forcing you to be their bouncer, and that role was always going to suck. "No, your babies are not on the list to be their grandbabies. Step aside."

Which brings me to my opening query. I would want my husband to absorb any lashing-out from his sister on the family's behalf. It's the right thing to do.

Worried abut litigious neighbors
Guest Sep 19, 11:37 a.m.
QUESTION: We‚Äôre starting to have problems with our next-door neighbors. I don't want to be enemies, but I also don‚Äôt want to lose a bunch of money because they decided to sue us. My wife and I have lived here since last January and the neighbors on one side have two tween boys. Recently I saw the older boy jump our fence to retrieve his ball. I was worried because even though he was the one trespassing into our yard, the fence is rickety, we have a large dog and a lot of uneven spots (we did what we could this summer but the yard still needs a lot of work). The father of those boys is a lawyer and I know if one of them gets hurt, they‚Äôll sue us for every penny we‚Äôve got. I told the boy to stay out of our yard, to ring the bell and ask for the ball when that happens. He said he would but I‚Äôve caught him and his brother both in our yard since then. I went and talked to their father, but didn't get anywhere. He didn't take me seriously and told me that I was overreacting, that kids will jump fences and it‚Äôs no big deal. I felt like he was patronizing me so I snapped and told him that he better not let his kids trespass in my yard again otherwise I would call the police to start a paper trail. I‚Äôve put up no trespassing signs but we can‚Äôt afford a new fence and I‚Äôm losing sleep over this. My wife thinks I‚Äôm overacting but my parents got sued by a neighbor when I was young. Their kid was playing with my older brother, tripped over a ladder in our yard and hit his head on a retaining wall. My parents had to pay all of the hospital bills and their insurance went through the roof. So I know how badly these things can go. Should I try to talk to my neighbor again or just take out a loan and put up a bigger, jump-proof fence? My wife is against going into debt but says she‚Äôll go along with it if it‚Äôll give me some peace of mind.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Whoa, wait a minute. You "know" you'll get sued because your neighbor is a lawyer? You "snapped" just because you "felt like he was patronizing me"?

It sounds to me as if your next-door neighbors are starting to have problems with you -- based on your assumptions about them that you've cast into hardened certainties based on a bad experience you witnessed as a child. Whew.

I am not an insurance expert or a lawyer, obvy, and this brings in both areas of expertise. But I am comfortable calling it when I see someone creating a problem they're blaming on someone else.

Is it great that the tweens are in your yard, no. But the parents can't possibly supervise their kids 24-7. That is unrealistic. You, however, can recognize when you're letting past results cloud current judgment.

And you can also recognize when you're worried about the wrong problem. You have a large dog and a rickety fence. That tells me you -- not the boys -- are your own reason to borrow money for a big, sound new fence. And to approach the neighbors with a peace offering and an apology for starting off on such an aggressive note. And looking into an umbrella policy if you're going to lose sleep over stuff like this to the point you can't be civil.

Moving Away/Left Behind
Guest Sep 19, 11:46 a.m.
QUESTION: My bestie ‚Äî my daily ride or die! ‚Äî is working hard on moving their family to Europe. Like, selling property, getting background checks, hiring lawyers, seriously trying to leave. And while they're excited and want me to be excited for them, I'm really struggling. I'm, frankly, devastated. I can't leave for a billion reasons (primarily, kids and husband are not on board with exploring this). How do I navigate faking excitement for them while my heart is breaking at being left behind?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Have you told the truth yet about your devastation?

It happens all the time that friends' new relationships, promotions, moves, births -- obvious additions to their lives -- turn out to be subtractions from ours, of their time or presence or attention. At some point we have to find a way to be happy for them, or else we're just peevish and self-absorbed and not very good friends.

But it's hard to get to that point in a genuine, good-for-the-friendship way without some sort of honest reckoning with the loss. Like, be peevish for your moment. "I know I am supposed to be happy for you, but I need to say out loud first that I am devastated by this. I want you around every day/week like always and my heart is breaking at being left behind." You can even say right afterward that you appreciate the chance to say this, will be on message with your excitement and support from now on, and have some thoughts about the decor for their guest suite.

If you have already aired your grief to your friend, then openness about your struggle can still work and be a fair part of close friendship, as long as you're not expecting her to fix this for you. Something like: "I'm still excited for you, I'm just having some breakthrough grief today for me." Good friends have their own sense of the right mix for them, but in general too much dumping and too much faking both can be a strain.

So much hatred
Guest Sep 19, 12:36 p.m.
QUESTION: Carolyn I'm not sure how to cope anymore. My husband and I have been married 20 yrs with no kids. In the beginning our marriage was fine. About 10 yrs ago his mom died and mine died 9 yrs ago. It was a hard time but we got through it. We've since relocated to be near family and life got better. His attitude started to improve. He came off his depression meds.

Lately though he has so much hatred for everything and everyone. He gets so angry for little things now and things he can't control (the firing of Kimmel for example). He's become lazy at home - never helping out with chores and never picking up after himself. For the past 11 months we have not slept in the same bed (he always has an excuse - not tired, needs to listen to podcast, dog took up the bed etc). And don't get me started on the romance - it isn't there.

He's clearly going through depression issues but I don't know how to help. I've suggested counseling and he doesn't follow through, I've suggested a therapist and he doesn't follow through. I've even tried to get him to see just a primary care but nope.

What, if anything, can I do at this point? Last night I put on a netflix show but he keeps his head in the phone and when I asked him a question he literally told me to "F off". Obviously I wouldn't take it so I left the room. He'll try and talk to me today when he comes home from work but never apologizes for his words which quite honestly hurt.

I am seeing a therapist but how can I help someone who is refusing to get help?

I'm feeling lonely in my own marriage.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: You don't have to live like this.

Don't just believe it -- know it. It is your baseline.

It does sound as if his depression is back with a vengeance, yes, and so medical intervention will be the answer. It also sounds as if you will need to be more forceful in your "suggest[ing]" that he get professional help. This is still going to fall short of so-called making him get treatment, which we all know isn't a thing.

But you, in the meantime, don't have to live in this reaction place to his misery and mistreatment.

You can make any one of a series of decisions on a scale of disengagement from his misery. Just to use the sleeping apart example: You cite 11 months of his excuses. That means you keep trying, right? You could instead choose to formalize the new sleeping arrangement and make your room the retreat you want it to be.

This may hit you all wrong, and I can understand that. But it's an example of using your agency and working within your current reality to stabilize your well-being from one day to the next. Instead of dragging that nightly what-if into a 12th month, you settle it, and sleep well in your nest, and decide to focus on higher-percentage issues like urging him back into care.

You can go bigger or smaller than this. Smaller would be a sit-down with him on making appointments, for example. And/or, trying on a decision to switch from reacting to acting, and then going through the next few days noticing what things would change when you do that, and how -- then starting gradually to introduce those changes.

Bigger, separation. "I love you. Too much to let this keep spiraling. You told me to F off. You can make appointments to get back into treatment for your depression, or you can pack."

However big you go, though, do take the reins of your life back. Not only will it be healthier for you, but it will actually make you more effective in dealing with your marriage, however you choose to respond.

Nights to Ourselves
Guest Sep 19, 12:38 p.m.
QUESTION: My wife is an amateur singer. She is in a barbershop quartet. They practice one evening per week and go to regional retreats/competitions a few weekends a year. She's been doing this for many years. She loves singing, though she has told me on numerous occasions about various frustrations with her group; it often seems like she is not having much fun. Still, I support her activity because it is what she chooses to do.

We now have two kids, currently four and two. Since they arrived, I've always taken care of them on rehearsal nights. I go with her on the weekends away, bring the kids to her for nursing, and otherwise find ways to keep busy with them. It isn't easy, but it's fine.

Having two young kids in general is hard work for both of us. A year or so ago, I said that since she was taking one night a week off for herself, I should get one too. She agreed, and now I go out to do some activities I enjoy (gym climbing and poker) one evening per week.

This arrangement is fine with me. I thought it was fine with her. Recently, though, I noticed some body-language resentment. When I asked, she said that she kind of felt it was unfair that I got to have "personal time" each week to do things just for myself when she didn't. I was confused, and said I thought of her rehearsals as her personal time. She said that they did not really count as personal time because she had made a commitment to her group and, although she did not say this part, lately has not been enjoying it so much.

I think that if she chooses to spend her time with her quartet, that's great, that's her choice, that's her personal time. If she doesn't want to do it anymore, she can and should quit; other members have quit in the past, there is nothing forcing her to continue. We've agreed to disagree about this.

Her position seems absurd to me. What am I missing?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I suppose I could say, "Her quartet is her personal time," blam, and be done with it -- I want a curly white wig -- but if she needs some relaxation time, then she does. That makes sense, doesn't it? You can understand how her hobby isn't relaxing?

And maybe she isn't ready to let the quartet go -- those two things don't seem mutually exclusive to me, loving to sing and not loving the organizational headaches.

So why not two blocks of discretionary time each. Doable? Maybe get paid care for one of them, so you both get a break and it's not always paid for with the other's energy reserves. It doesn't always have to be expensive. We used community-center drop-in care for a while, for example. We had to stay on site but there was a fitness room and a pool, good enough.

Anyway -- I love the phrase "body-language resentment." And good job picking up on it and talking about it. But don't get so caught up in keeping perfect score that you don't prioritize finding ways to give each other what you need.

Re: nights to ourselves
Guest Sep 19, 1:24 p.m.
QUESTION: I think one thing that‚Äôs missed in Carolyn‚Äôs response is: the parents should discuss and agree on how many nights they want to spend together as a family, and how many are ok with just kids + mom or dad or childcare. Maybe she does the weekly rehearsals and then one me-night every other week, or whatever else works. The biggest issue is that when you‚Äôve got young kids, there never ever feels like there‚Äôs enough time to do all of the things you wish you could, so you have to prioritize.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: It doesn't have to be nights. Weekend mornings, for example, were a natural break time Back in the Day. Sign the kids up for X, and one parent takes them there and the other one has ahhh time. If the family schedule is packed, then the parents trade off that Saturday breather. Saw this all the time.

There was something else we used to talk about and I put it in the column too, though it's been a while? Each parent in a co-parent situation feels like they're doing 75 percent or more -- it never feels like just half. That's when you know you're pulling your weight.

Point being, it's important to remain mindful of the value and exhaustion in what the other is doing, too. If you start getting possessive of time and accusatory with each other, danger.

Marriage
Guest Sep 19, 2:08 p.m.
QUESTION: If I'm making sure that we have sex weekly, because partner wants sex weekly, can I do anything about not wanting it weekly? He says it feels like I'm checking something off my to-do list. I mean I am but I'm just trying to be a good wife. Our kids are 7 and 9 and I'm doing my best to be there for everybody and my boss. But I'm just tired.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Oh this super bugs me.

The correct answer to your attention to something he finds important is gratitude. Or just not freaking crabbing about it to you after you do this for him.

When he crabs that it's not good enough, think of the message -- you have to look like you aren't tired, depleted, erased? It's not good enough that you're doing it for him, you have to pretend you're doing it for you? OHHH, it's terrible you have to pretend? You should be doing it for you? Okay!! So basically you can't win. You can't have an honest feeling. You can't be tired. You can't fake it, either. You have to give him the thing but not look like you're giving him the thing.

This is really crappy and not okay, and all I can do is hope that if he thought about it for a moment, he would agree that it's a crappy and not okay thing to do to you.

Yes, I'm sure you wish, too, that you had the energy to want it. It's not like this is your first choice of feelings.

Now -- I have sympathy for him and for anyone who doesn't think their partner is attracted to them, or with a strong sex drive whose partner's drive isn't there. Difficult stuff. But think of the limited menu that hands them: They can be told they're outta luck!! That right there. Or they can talk about opening the relationship, they can cheat? A lot of not great options. or ...

Their partner can make an effort to push past the low sex drive, understanding that's a part of a long-term monogamous relationship. That it's a mutual effort to meet each other's needs, with love and respect.

That's what you're doing. I wish I could beam thoughts into your husband's head that complaining about "good" because it isn't perfect is not a great look. Everyone is taking something from you, and he's whining that he's not getting enough.

That's all rant, no advice.

Condensed version is the advice: Tell him you would love to feel like your freer self. Currently, you are this self -- offering herself to him out of love. To be criticized for that is not setting a mood.

Even if none of you ever says a word about it and the deal is just to allow you to live in peace.

Update and new question from LW with abusive stepdad
Guest Sep 12, 11:02 a.m.
QUESTION: I wrote to you over 3 years ago with a question about how to tell my daughter about her father (who was my abusive stepfather). I took your advice and let my therapist guide me in how best to let my daughter know about her father. What she's been told for now is that he‚Äôs not a part of her life because he wasn‚Äôt nice to me. We are doing great. Last year we moved into our own apartment. We're scraping by, eating a lot of beans, lentils and peanut butter but making the best of it. I converted a walk-in closet to a tiny bedroom for my daughter and made a fabric canopy for her little bed and together we put glow in the dark stars all over the walls and ceiling. Our little home makes both of us so happy. She's a great kid - cheerful, healthy, smart. She‚Äôs in second grade now and the joy of my life.
Now my question. Her father died about 6 months ago in a car accident. He was driving drunk but thankfully no one else was severely injured. I‚Äôm so happy he‚Äôs dead. If that makes me a terrible person, I don‚Äôt care. I feel great knowing he can never hurt us or bother us in any way. My mother started contacting me after his death. She claims that she was as much a victim of him as I was and she wants to be part of my life and to get to know my daughter. I guess she could be telling the truth but I only remember the terrible things she said to me and the ugly names she called me when I got pregnant. I never saw him abuse her or even get angry with her. I don‚Äôt know. My daughter and I are doing so well and I want to enjoy our stability and not invite trauma and pain back into our lives and that‚Äôs what my mom means to me even if she‚Äôs telling the truth about my stepfather. My daughter and I finished with therapists just last year and this would start that up again since a relationship with my mom might mean that my daughter has to learn more about her dad, especially that he's dead now. Is it okay if I tell my mom we‚Äôre not up to dealing with this right now, check back in a couple of years?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Of course it is, yes -- and would be so without the extent of the trauma. Because you decide whom to have in your life and on what terms.

That's the bedrock here. All other issues -- including what is fair or reasonable or true or healthy, etc. -- are secondary. Everything starts with the fact of you as your own gatekeeper for your time and your heart and your self.*

Now, some thoughts about "even if she‚Äôs telling the truth." Knowing only what you've written in your two questions, I believe your mother was also a victim of your stepfather's abuse. Or at least, I find it credible. It's an uncomfortable topic, to put it mildly, but there is room for grey in the reality of abusive relationships, and people who are caught in an abusive cycle with someone like your stepfather can certainly do harmful things themselves.

Sometimes it's to themselves, or to their abusers, but too often it's to others. (Or all three.) Relevantly: We've all seen it over and over in this space, in fact, in the form of either turning a blind eye to the other parent's abuse of the children, or even siding with the abusive parent against the children. Nothing excuses this -- but it's cited so often by people who grew up with an abusive parent that I suspect it's a survival response in the partner. A maladaptive one, obviously. When humans get feral it tends not to be good.

Anyway -- none of that justifies her claim that "she was as much a victim of him as I was." If that is indeed how she phrased it. She was the adult, you were the child, you were raped, and she remained partnered with your rapist till his death. So, absolutely freaking not. There is not enough "not."

And further, "she wants to be part of my life and to get to know my daughter" is only about her. She's coming to you, imagine that, to help her heal? The framing a healthy person would know to offer, as you may have spotted instantly from your own good work in therapy, is, "I understand it was unforgivable, but I at least want to apologize for my utter failure to protect you." In other words, only about you. Only. Paired with an independent effort to seek support and heal on her own time and dime.

You can't want wellness for her. But you can respond to her that you're sorry to hear she also suffered your stepfather's abuse -- and hope she will consider therapy, given how empowering it was for you. (Your own phrasing, of course.)

(expand this to see the rest)

Losing Half My Kid's Life
Guest Sep 12, 11:14 a.m.
QUESTION: Carolyn, My partner and I are splitting up after some terrible, grueling years. I know I cannot live like this another second - but the idea of missing so much of my (one) child's life is unbearable. Do you have any words for how to bear up with this? Tell me it's going to be ok?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: It's going to be okay.

I'm sorry it has been so bad for so long. That means, though, that you have already been missing some of your child's life to that. So in a way this is your chance to reclaim some happier time.

Plus, the reality of co-parenting under one roof includes more tag-teaming, handing off, you-here/me-there, than you may realize. And, there's another relationship to conduct (an offshoot of the above point). Even when you get along with your co-parent, you are managing that bond and the one with your kid, and the mechanics of the whole unit together. When you're solo parent, it's just you -- you're on.

So yes, it's less time, specifically fewer days, but those days are more. Meanwhile, their time to themselves is something many newly solo parents come to appreciate and learn to use to their and their kids' benefit.

Talk to some parents who have made this transition -- not all the stories will be happy ones, but I think in aggregate you will find them reassuring. Definitely useful.

Permission to leave
Guest Sep 12, 11:36 a.m.
QUESTION: Some time ago, I discovered that my spouse had never been faithful to me since our relationship began, well over a decade ago. We have an elementary-aged child together, and I think we get along pretty well on a day-to-day basis, considering that I cannot imagine trusting my spouse with my emotional well-being again. We have both done individual and couples' therapy, and my spouse continues to withhold information about social interactions despite promising transparency.

There are obvious practical concerns, including wanting to retain as much stability for our child as possible, and the costs of separate households in a high cost-of-living area. How do I know that divorce is an okay thing to do when it would upend our child's world? What reasons are good enough?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: That your spouse is fundamentally, serially dishonest seems like one of the better ones to me.

Divorce isn't a picnic for kids but in-house instability isn't, either, so you do your best.

I mean, have you said outright to Spouse, "I know you will never be faithful to me. I am done trying for that. I want you to be honest with me"? I'm not suggesting you have an open marriage, or divorce and live apart, or divorce and cohabit as roommates, or whatever other configuration might arise from this tell-me-the-truth phase of your lives. I'm merely asking whether you've removed this last remaining incentive your spouse might have to hide the infidelity.

If you've done that and the lying persists? Then that tells you something. Spinning off your own household, especially if you have primary custody, could be a lot healthier for your kid. But that's super-speculative, probably (sorry) better for a session or two with the pros.

I'm sorry.

331Press Enter to expand
Perceived cruelty
Guest Sep 12, 11:36 a.m.
QUESTION: If one feels they have been spoken to cruelly, is it better for one to confront that, or just cease communications completely?

Thinking of Angelou's poem: "When someone shows you who they are, believe them."

But unsure how best to proceed.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I'd prefer more context, but without it I'll say generally that it's best to respond to people directly. "What a cruel comment. I hope you didn't mean that."

When you "just cease communications completely," you don't give people a chance even to know what they did to upset you, much less fix their mistakes. Certainly there are times when people know exactly what they did and have no intention of fixing it -- but severing ties treats that as a 100 percent certainty. Meaning, you're leaving zero room for the possibility that anyone ever misspeaks, or tells a joke that falls flat, or means it but has instant regrets.

That's just not realistic, is it? I believe Maya Angelou is a beacon, but also that you're misapplying her words here. I don't think she's saying, "The minute someone says the wrong thing, they should be dead to you." Instead, it's wise guidance on character, and on trusting yourself. You do know poor character when you see it, and it's okay not to keep granting second and third and nth chances for people of poor character to keep harm you.

That leaves plenty of room for you to tell someone the effect their words had on you, and to weigh their response. Their words, your objection to them and their immediate and longer-term response to that are all, to my mind, under the umbrella of "the first time," which I believe is the omitted part of the quote/thought.

He was hurt by my reaction and said that many people have this problem and I shouldn‚Äôt shame him about it. Yes, many people have this issue, but we‚Äôre 55 years old, not 85!

Turns out I wasn't the gf, I was the other woman
Guest Sep 12, 12:48 p.m.
QUESTION: I was the other woman for months and had no idea. I met my boyfriend through a dating app and immediately felt an incredible connection. It was my first serious relationship. He was a little older than me, a widower just getting back into the dating scene. That‚Äôs what he told me. We said we loved each other after three months, he met my family, I even met his brother when he was in town for a week. The only drawback to the whole relationship was that his job required him to work every weekend. Two weeks ago, I decided to treat myself to a Saturday spa day at the best salon in our city. After I was done, I planned to stop at a caf√© that I‚Äôd been to once with my boyfriend. It was within walking distance of his apartment. We never spent much time at his apartment since he said it held a lot of sad memories for him and a lot of his late wife‚Äôs stuff was still there. When I got to the caf√©, I was surprised to see my boyfriend sitting at an outside table with a woman who looked like his late wife‚Äôs picture. I was immediately suspicious so I lurked around across the street until they paid the bill and then followed them back to his apartment. I knocked on the door and when the woman answered, I asked if she was his wife and she said yes. My boyfriend appeared and his face told the whole story. I felt faint but managed to tell his wife that we'd been dating for six months. As I left, I could hear him apologizing to her and saying I meant nothing to him. Later the wife called me and demanded I leave her husband alone. He must have put the whole blame on me. I learned that she works in another state and comes home on the weekends. I don‚Äôt know how I‚Äôm ever going to get over this. I feel stupid and ashamed and crushed. We were making plans for the future, I thought we would get married. What am I going to tell people, especially my family? How will I ever get over this? I can barely make it to work every day and then I come home and cry all night.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Why? Why are you being so hard on yourself, when you did absolutely nothing wrong?

I get that you feel embarrassed in a reflexive way -- the "stupid and ashamed and crushed" is the natural trio of reactions most people would have if they choose Column A reactions (with Column B being the outward-facing set, the anger family). But after reflex comes thought, and I would hope with reflection you'd see there's nothing here that made you stupid or wrong. In fact, a good deal of relief is ready and waiting whenever you're ready to swap it in for "crushed."

This "boyfriend" is very bad news. Of what exact variety, I can't know and don't care. There's no real reason for you to care, either. Maybe eventually when you're ready to review and revisit and learn, but not now.

And the idea that he "put the whole blame on me"? Like, you hunted him down and made him cheat on his wife while she was away? There's nothing he could have told her that would erase his responsibility. Nothing.

There was only a decision on her part to buy his lie. That's another thing we can't know -- what her motivation is to keep lying to herself to this degree. But 100 percent, her call to you was her committing to the bit. She can demand you stay away and this and that and blah blah blah and it was never and will never be you. Okay? It's her husband and she has to know that.

Anyway. You got lied to but good. It's a scary, creepy, destabilizing feeling. That part I get. Especially in a first serious relationship, that can make you question your ability to trust others and yourself, so talking to someone about it might be well worth it. And just that you can't stop crying -- that's a get-help scenario. If your work has an EAP, that's the most accessible care. Right now, even, thehotline.org is appropriate because it's emotional abuse, what the guy did, easily.

But as far as feeling shame, again, please don't. Tell people/family you broke up, he wasn't the person you thought he was. That's all you need -- anything more is totally at your discretion, to whom and when/if ever you're ready. To learn someone "wasn't the person I thought" is basically every breakup ever, truly. Take care.

Frustrated by wife's lack of interest in building a social circle
Guest Sep 12, 1:00 p.m.
QUESTION: My wife and I moved to our neighborhood four years ago, and have trouble making friends to varying degrees. For some background, we have a 5 year old and 1 year old. We lived in a large city, but moved out to the suburbs when our daughter was 1. My wife had a core group of three other friends, but by the time we moved, the other friends were in the process of moving elsewhere in the area/ out of the region all together (that's not why we moved; just happened to occur at the same time). This was particularly hard as her best friend, who was the glue of this group, moved to the other side of the country.

When we moved to the suburbs, we aimed to have a strong group of friends like we had before. To that end, we specifically choose a neighborhood that we knew had a lot of young families, even though it was more expensive.

In the four years that we‚Äôve lived here, we‚Äôve had mixed results with making friends. I‚Äôve been able to make friends with some of the Dads in the neighborhood/our daughter‚Äôs school. However, things have been a bit harder for my wife. She‚Äôll get invites from people to have play dates, but rarely reciprocates. We‚Äôre both on a few group chat threads, and whenever someone says ‚Äúlet‚Äôs meet up at a playground,‚Äù my wife‚Äôs response is ‚Äúyou should go, I don‚Äôt feel like being social‚Äù. Whenever I mention trying to get together with some of the other Moms we know, her response is ‚Äúeh, i don't really want to‚Äù or ‚Äúthey won‚Äôt want to get together with me, they already have friends.‚Äù

On one hand, I empathize with her; I‚Äôm an introvert too, and I recognize that socializing doesn‚Äôt always come easy. On the other hand, it‚Äôs frustrating to hear her complain about how she wishes she had friends, when it feels like if she got out of her shell a bit, her social life would be better, and maybe she shouldn‚Äôt be looking for this unicorn of a best friend. It just feels like she thinks that a friendship is going to appear out of nowhere, and that‚Äôs not the case, she does have to work at it.

Am I off base in feeling frustrated about this?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: No, because it's super frustrating to hear someone complain, then pass up chances to fix the problem, then complain.

However: An introvert with a 1- and 5-year-old (and maybe a touch of PPD? ‚Äúthey won‚Äôt want to get together with me") is a low, low percentage bet to agree to the playground meetup to start from scratch to make new friends.

So maybe you, OP, but also you, both of you, would benefit from some realism and patience. She will need friends, yes, and is lonely -- but this is going to take time. Time for her to recover physically and hormonally, time for kids to grow a bit, time for better sleep, time to pep-talk herself into wanting to #$@&! start over.

Your wanting her to get on with it won't help. You mean well but I'm guessing she picks up the vibe. Sorry for the pun, but baby steps. These years are just hard. And moving on top of that? Ugh.

College transition
Guest Aug 1, 11:09 a.m.
QUESTION: My 18 year-old starts college in 2.5 weeks, but has been gone all summer at a low paying, extremely demanding, exhausting, somewhat risky, but rewarding (to him) live-in job far away. He left the day after graduation and has only come home once for 24 hours. His college is 1,500 miles away‚Ä¶. Now the summer job wants him to stay for an extra week, so he said he‚Äôll only come home (reluctantly) for about 24 hours before his flight to college. ‚Ä¶.He has a laundry list of things he needs to do: rest up from the job, see the doctor (for a possible medical issue), visit the dentist for a routine cleaning, haircut, buy clothes/shoes, buy things for the dorm room‚Ä¶ He thinks staying the extra days post-contract make him a shoe-in for a ‚Äúpromotion‚Äù at this summer job next year. The fact is that there are guarantees and the employer probably just wants more cheap laborers to exploit ‚Ä¶ I said if he doesn‚Äôt come home one week before the flight, I‚Äôm not paying for college. My question: Is this overstepping? A mistake on my part? Yes he‚Äôs technically an adult but I see this as a really dumb decision. College transition is a huge deal and the out of state college is jaw-droppingly expensive. He refused to attend the in-person orientation so he could stay at this job. I don‚Äôt want to pay SO MUCH when he‚Äôs showing almost no interest. PS back story is he didn‚Äôt get into the Ivy League type schools that were his first picks.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: UPDATE: I posted this answer Aug. 1, when I announced the chat was going on hiatus -- I saw it in the queue and hoped the parent would see the answer and not threaten to pull the kid's tuition.

[I'm supposed to be off, but I can't help myself.]

An 18-year-old working this hard and owning it this much can't be the worst thing.

He sees the doctor in the 24 hours. Yes.

The dentist too if possible, but that can be at school. Check your insurance. So can the haircut and all the shopping. None of it really matters, certainly not the way it used to. He can click-click-click it all from where he is for delivery at his campus, or you can do it for him if you're both okay with that. A list and a budget and shipping instructions from the school are all he needs.

What neither of you needs is a power struggle right now. That is madness and it is not going to improve his college uptake prospects.

If anything, imagine the job as a relationship you don't approve of -- resisting it will only push them together.

But again, this is work! He's working and caring. I don't know how to beg you hard enough to pick your battles more carefully. Caring is good. Hard work is good.

Okay now I will be resting.

Family (?)
Guest Sep 5, 11:16 a.m.
QUESTION: My ex-husband and I divorced when I was still pregnant with our third and our other two were still in diapers. We became a great co-parenting team and when we each remarried, we welcomed our spouses and their children. We vacationed together, had holidays together. When my ex became ill with cancer, I was part of the inner circle taking him to doctor's visits when his wife couldn't do it, making dinners, being the taxi driver so ex's youngest kids continued with their sports, and supporting our older kids any way I could. My husband did laundry, shoveled sidewalks, and picked up the slack in our home when I was helping. When he died, his wife wrote a heartfelt message on social media and published a formal obituary and never once mentioned me. The larger family my ex and I created was a source of great pride for him. He even did public speaking about it - encouraging families to redefine who is family for the betterment of the kids and everyone. These announcements detailed his life skipping right over our marriage, co-parenting etc. They talked about the great care team and supporting and loving their two sets of kids (older and younger) - neighbors were named and thanked, aunts/uncles, ILs - and my husband and I were not mentioned at all. Our kids noticed it. I'm confused about my relationship with his wife. I thought we were family. Not friends, exactly, but family. Now I don't even know how to be in a relationship with her any more. Bringing up that I wasn't anywhere in any announcement sounds petty but my ex meant a lot to me. Our history meant a lot but maybe now that he's gone his wife doesn't want to be in a relationship with me any more?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Well that's weird and awful, I'm sorry, and I'm sorry for your loss.

As hard as it is to override the impulse, though, I don't think it's helpful to assume she wrote you out because you don't matter to her or she doesn't see you as family or wants to sever your relationship.

I mean -- they're all possible, sure. But it's bizarre to tell you that by erasing you; it's angry. And I'm not seeing anywhere that you were reading anger on her (though grief does mess with people).

She could also, say, have some weird hangup about his having an ex-wife -- like mentioning you in these carefully curated propaganda pieces for public consumption was inconceivable to her. There is still a whole cultural subset that treats a second marriage as a courthouse walk of shame. It's bad form to deny kids but erasing an ex? Pff.

That's one hypothesis. Another is an offshoot of the first -- that she whitewashed the official stuff because she didn't want to hear it from an uptight branch of her family.

Maybe both are a reach. The highest percentage explanation? I suppose it's probably in your own words: "we were family. Not friends, exactly, but family." Do I detect a mutual not-feeling-it? Without your ex to justify being friends, maybe this was her way of ... being done.

Regardless: As for "Bringing up that I wasn't anywhere in any announcement sounds petty," I agree, if it's for its own sake. But bringing it up as an "I couldn't help but notice ..." is an opening to talk about where things stand now. If you would like to remain a blended family of sorts, if way less involved day-to-day for obvious reasons, then float the idea with her. But say that if the announcements were a hint, then you hope she will tell you straight.

Glory Days Gang
Guest Sep 5, 11:17 a.m.
QUESTION: My boyfriend still regularly hangs out with some of his friends from college (several of whom also went to the same high school). My boyfriend himself has accomplished a lot in the decade since graduating, but some of these friends are stuck in old patterns and conversations about things that happened in school. When we hang out with them, my boyfriend regresses; he will never mention things he's doing now in his public health work or with the friends he and I have made together. Instead he retells the same stories about stuff that happened at a school I didn't attend, before he was finished reaching adulthood. It gets really boring and old, and it only happens around this particular group of people. His feelings get hurt when I don't want to be present for those gatherings. What is my obligation to continue hanging out with the Glory Days gang?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Zero.

That's the obligation. His being "hurt" doesn't take away your control of your social time. Plus, while he feels what he feels etc. and all that, the hurt is misplaced. It's not personal; it's just boring. You're not included in these conversations, so you're just an audience, and the stories are all retellings, so you're an audience to the same show on repeat. He'd be bored too if you switched roles.

Those are just the basics, though. There are other things to consider: Snobbery is one. Yours isn't even veiled, as far as I can tell. So that may be getting to him. You definitely look down on this branch of his friends.

There's also the element, if you're both willing, of trying to come up with a third option. Option 1 is that you go and get bored to tears, and Option 2, he sees these friends without you. Right? Option 3, then, is where you agree to go if he agrees to make an actual effort to have actual conversation instead of high school/college story hour.

That's his challenge: Does he want you there badly enough to do the work socially to make it inclusive? If not, then he and his feelings can zip it when you decide to stay home.

How do I begin?
Guest Sep 5, 11:34 a.m.
QUESTION: I have had an ongoing disagreement with one of my siblings which has resulted in not communicating for months. I was really hurt by their actions and I think I did my share of hurting too. What‚Äôs the best way to start off a way back? I feel really vulnerable.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: The way back is to admit your part of the hurting, and apologize for it sincerely.

But before you do that, prepare for the possibility that your sibling doesn't do the same, and just accepts your apology as the end of it without admitting any fault. It's a tough one, because the apology is only genuine if it's not intended as the opening of a quid pro quo. But at the same time, I expect you won't feel good about their walking away with the idea they did nothing wrong. It takes courage and a real commitment to owning your mistakes to be willing to do that.

It also may mean you choose not to reconcile for that reason, which obviously is a bad outcome in a lot of ways. But taking responsibility for your mistakes is the honorable outcome that's entirely within your control, so you do that. And you hope for and work toward the best on the rest.

Re: College Transition
Guest Sep 5, 11:47 a.m.
QUESTION: I am a 20-year college professor, and I spent the majority of this career teaching first-year students. Telling your student you will not pay their tuition is equivalent to telling your spouse you want a divorce: you are telling them you are ending their current work/living situation and their future life plans as they know them. Threatening to do this so they behave as you want is just as manipulative as when a spouse threatens to call the divorce lawyer "unless." I have had many, many students sit in my office, weeping, trying frantically to figure out how they will stay in school because their parents have threatened to stop paying for school unless they change their major to something they hate but that the parent prefers, go back in the closet, stop dating their partner, etc. Often the students ask for reassurance that, when they start lying to their parents, no one at the university will reveal the truth (because of FERPA, the student privacy law, as long as the student is a legal adult, the answer is almost always that we cannot reveal any information about them. Students, please think very carefully before signing FERPA waivers--and know that you can revoke them at any time). Alternatively, if parents make this threat too often, students stop caring, or they act first, rejecting their whole relationship with their parents along with the funding. I strongly encourage this parent to find other ways of resolving their disagreements with their adult child. Please make sure discussions about providing financial aid are calm, accurate, and only about your family's financial situation, rather than a leash to control your adult child's behavior.

Was my daughter a great guest or a bad stepdaughter that week?
Guest Sep 5, 12:11 p.m.
QUESTION: My wife, our 2 children (5 and 7), and my mother-in-law spent two weeks at a beach house in August. My older daughter (Tess, 22) from my first marriage joined us for a week. Tess is very fair with blue eyes so is extremely careful about sun exposure. She got up early every morning to run on the beach and swim. I joined her several times. She would then head back to the house for a shower and nap and was back in bed when the rest of the family was eating breakfast. By the time she was awake again, we were all at the beach or out sightseeing. We‚Äôd return home at 4 or 5, just when Tess was ready for more beach time so we didn‚Äôt overlap during the day. Tess would fix dinner for herself (groceries she‚Äôd had delivered and paid for) about an hour or so after we were finished (we ate dinner early because of the kids). The kitchen was open to the living area so while she ate and cleaned up we were all able to chat. Tess would then head back to her bedroom to Facetime/text with friends or stream non-family friendly shows. She joined us for dinner out once and was always in bed by 11. She kept her bedroom and bathroom spotless. I was proud that she was so clean, considerate, and quiet so was shocked when I found out my wife was upset that Tess spent so little time with the rest of the family. I think it‚Äôs asking too much of a young adult on vacation from a demanding job to keep the same hours/activities as a family with small children. A lot of people her age would have been partying, chasing guys, leaving a mess so I thought she was a great guest. My wife and I can‚Äôt seem to reach an agreement here so decided to ask you which of us is being unreasonable.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: So if your wife wins the Who's Right contest, then she wins the right to be mad?

I hope she takes a minute to think about that before pursuing this any further.

There is a chance here to think the best of someone vs thinking the worst, in an area (blended families) that is notoriously fraught with conflict and misunderstandings -- and to do so using the well-established channel of the benefit of the doubt. "Thanks, Pookie, for framing it that way for me. Now that I think about it, she was incredibly considerate of our space and our schedule." That's an example of what it looks like to make an effort to like someone, especially someone whose ways are different from one's own. Or just when there's a huge incentive to keep things warm and functional.

I could cast it in a totally practical light, too: You raised this daughter -- so it makes total sense that she'd be following your model of what makes a courteous guest under these conditions. Your wife, a different creature from I'm guessing a different set of habits, givens and customs, obviously works from a different model.

Now that your daughter's visit has exposed this difference, it makes sense to get rid of the whole "who's right" construct (yay) and have a happy "aha" moment where you realize this is what happened. Your daughter got an A+ on your test because that's the one she thought she was taking and you taught her to take. You and she didn't know, and it probably never occurred to your wife to say, that your wife was taught completely different things and so all along your daughter was getting a C- on your wife's test.

No harm intended or done. Right? (Right?) The whole point of being a great guest and stepdaughter is to contribute to the greater good of the family unit (which, in turn, promotes the health of the individuals in it -- it's cyclical). So it still makes sense to give benefits of doubts liberally and celebrate the good.

And if your wife would like your daughter to interact more next time, then she needs to learn from this to make that expectation clear to you, and you and she need to discuss ahead of time what that might look like while being respectful of her 22/23-year-oldness and thoroughly valid lack of interest in sunburns and elementary age field trips. Then you can talk to your daughter to float some ideas.

crumbling republic
Guest Sep 5, 12:12 p.m.
QUESTION: i am a married 41 year old trans guy, working at a very stable job at a family business. money is good, work is soul-crushing and boring, white collar industry that tends to lean to the right.

my wife and i are planning a potential move overseas (grad school for her, presumably new job for me) but i'm struggling in the interim. any tricks for 'making' myself more productive without believing in either the work or the future?

for what it's worth, i've been to a couple of marches, and have volunteered on campaigns in years prior. i plan on doing the same for the midterms but i feel like i'm part of the problem by not being a bigger part of the solution.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: You are not part of the problem. You have been relentlessly targeted, singled out and bullied by the problem.

That means, among other things, that if you need other people to be the solution for you right now, then that's absolutely fine. That's how compassionate people and civilized societies work. They recognize we can't (and shouldn't have to!) do everything all the time all by ourselves, and step up for each other when needed. That's the social contract.

To help you with your struggle in the meantime, I don't recommend "tricks." You're too smart for that. You know what just working and killing time are costing you. So I suggest instead that you think in terms of what can reconnect you with life and beauty. Think as small as you need to -- playing more music or going to more museums and galleries, for example. More walks. Gloriously trashy fiction or tv. If you have the time, then go bigger -- maybe a pet, a container garden, a new craft or hobby, a volunteer gig where you have regular interaction with the people you're helping. I'm giving a range because we're all so different, and what gives one life meaning can be a demoralizing drain on another.

And of course these things are always on advice-columnists' lists in do-something columns. But it's not what's on the list so much as the nature of it that I'm trying to convey -- get out of the "productivity" and "marches" mind set. Go to the "beauty of being human" well. That's the one I think will help.
