343Press Enter to expand
How to treat kids fairly when it‚Äôs just not in my heart?
Guest Sep 5, 12:45 p.m.
QUESTION: My spouse and I are about to take on the care of our 14-year old nephew. His single adoptive parent is elderly and very ill.

We have a 14-year old daughter and an 11-year old son. They are wonderful kids and have always had the stability of a two-parent loving home.

The cousins do get along well.

I can already feel the tension of knowing that our nephew deserves our full efforts and love AND that I just do not love him the same way as I do my own children. Do I just fake it? The kids are clearly old enough to know, see, and feel the difference.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I'm sure your nephew doesn't love you the way he loves his parent, either.

Why should he?

Things like that take time, and it's unrealistic to expect it to happen the moment life puts you together, just because. But that doesn't mean you won't ever form as loving a bond as any parent and child -- and that's where you can be the person your nephew needs. Just by opening your home, heart and family to him as another child. That means in standing, access, privileges, care.

It may seem cold to define it as a class of treatment, but that is what you can do honestly -- just not treat him as a second-class kid to the first-class daughter and son. To fake that you love him like your own child seems like it would be insulting his intelligence. And uncomfortable, no? Might he not resent that, even -- having anyone presume to be instant parent, especially as he deals with what must be scary feelings about his sick parent?

It's great that you're doing this, so please don't be so tough on yourself. Don't ask the impossible and then shame yourself for falling short. Be attentive and fair, be mindful of his comfort and need for space as he makes this adjustment, and, again, be open to getting to know him better and to having him as part of the family. Give it time.

panic when the work matters to me
Guest Sep 5, 1:14 p.m.
QUESTION: I am finally in a science ph.d. program, and the work fascinates me. I got through the grueling first year and am trying to write my thesis proposal, just a one-pager to start. I know what it is I want to say but an old panic is gripping me as I do it, imagining that everything rides on this. I can't seem to grasp the idea of allowing a draft and moving forward. Does this mean I'm not cut out for this? Is there a way to do this better?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I'm just an opinionated stranger so I don't know what it means. But you say it's an old panic, and when it came out of your past and tracked you down, it found you in a science PhD program -- so I'm inclined to think it means you have an obstacle you've worked around in the past, which suggests you can probably work around it again.

Since dealing with old panics over and over again is annoying, and since proposals (grant vs thesis, presumably) are probably here to stay now, this would be a good time to workshop some permanent fixes.

Some ideas: There's the psychology-centered route, where you talk to someone who specifically addresses performance. There's the writing-centered route, where you shake up your techniques -- instead of typing, for example, you can talk your words into a file, just to introduce a small disruption in the pattern. There's the vagus-nerve centered route, where you walk, breathe, meditate, do yoga before your writing sessions. There's the logic-centered route, where you look back at your career to remind yourself of your ability to manage -- to quiet the doubting voice.

You get the idea.

Most writer's block is a matter of not knowing what you want to say, so if you really do have your points all teed up and ready to go, then you're in better shape than you realize.

Trying to be Switzerland
Guest Sep 5, 1:43 p.m.
QUESTION: My husband has had a strained relationship with his dad since husband's mom passed away seven years ago -- and his dad started dating (and having women stay at his house) literally four weeks later. Husband still loves his dad and sees him once or twice a year and they talk on the phone occasionally. The relationship is just not as close as it could be or once was. My SIL, on the other hand, has gone no contact with her dad.

About five years ago, FIL moved in with a woman (not the one he was dating so quickly after MIL's death) who is trying to repair/reinforce the relationship between dad and his children. And she's trying to enlist my help. I told her I thought the reasons for the estrangement were valid -- my husband's dad was really insensitive to his children's grief -- and I didn't think it was her or my role to broker any situation between my FIL and his children.

I told my mom this and she was appalled at my response, so now I'm second-guessing myself. Should I try to reconnect the family? Even if I wanted to, can I?

The other issue is that -- independent of everything else -- my husband does NOT like his dad's girlfriend...and that is rare for my husband. So any aversion my husband has to spending time with his dad is only worsened if it means the girlfriend is going to be involved.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: If your husband and sister-in-law learned from the experience of their mother's death and its aftermath that they really just don't like their father or think he's a good person, then so be it. Their prerogative and they've known him all their lives. This could be a simple actions-have-consequences culmination.

But if the sole basis of these estrangements is their judging him for being with women "too soon," then I'm seeing boundary issues here. His kids don't get to tell him how to grieve or when it is or isn't okay to be with another woman. They can have their opinions, sure, but it's not about them. Just as their parents' marriage wasn't about them. They grieve their ways, he grieves his way.

There's also no one conclusion to be drawn from his behavior. Some widow(er)s are so bereft without their spouses they kind of lose it, and grab the first person who walks by. Or, other extreme, it can be a sign the marriage was oppressive to them and they hung in there and hung in there like they thought they were supposed to ... and oops kind of go a little overboard a few weeks after the funeral.

Just two possibilities. Are they admirable? Hardly. But does "messy" justify seven years of not- or barely speaking as punishment for not handling his kids' feelings cleanly?

Or the other person was on the scene before the death -- which can be anything from a total betrayal to totally out in the open all along, because lives are incredibly complicated.

I'm 50 percent with you on your response to your FIL's new person. "I didn't think it was her or my role to broker any situation between my FIL and his children" is concise and sound -- but it was all you needed. There was no need to tell her what you thought of the reasons for the estrangement, and not just because I happen to think they got up in their dad's business ... disproportionately. It's not your place to be the broker whether their reasons were valid or not.

The upshot is, your second-guessing was founded and worthwhile, imo, but takes you to the same place: no to making it your job to reconnect the family. But if you ever want to broach the topic of anyone's standing to judge another's response to a death, then that might be a fascinating conversation :)

QUESTION: As a practicing faculty member in science, pretty much all scientists get writer's block sometimes. And pretty much everything we write gets revised a lot. See if you can say it a little better. Add an idea. Just start any old how, then refine a little bit at a time. After awhile, it'll be in enough shape to show it to someone else for some feedback.

1308Press Enter to expand
grandparents I never knew trying to give me money I don't want
Guest Jul 25, 11:37 a.m.
QUESTION: A few years ago I went to a lot of trouble to find and meet my biological father. Knowing that he left my mom pregnant and threatened her and my grandparents with physical harm if they tried to find him, I prepared myself for the worst. I‚Äôm not sure why I even wanted to meet him. With the help of my grandparents, my mom turned her life around, gave me a great childhood and an awesome stepdad. I guess I was looking for some clarity on where I came from. It was so not worth it. There was a reason he dipped, and it didn‚Äôt have anything to do with me or my mom. He‚Äôs a terrible person who chose himself over his child and everyone else his whole life. Even after I prepared myself for the worst, the awful things he said to me still managed to hurt me. I wish the worst person I‚Äôve ever met didn‚Äôt happen to contribute 50% of my DNA and I‚Äôve tried my best to forget all about him. Recently his sister contacted me saying her parents (technically my grandparents though I never met them) made a lot of money from the sale of their farm (they're retiring and moving away) and want to give me some of it to compensate for what their son did because they were always ashamed of his actions. My aunt seems nice and they all seem to have good intentions but I put that man mentally in a locked box and taking this money will open that box back up. My aunt admitted that he knows about this money I‚Äôd be getting. I haven‚Äôt even been able to tell my mom and dad (that‚Äôs what I call my stepdad since he‚Äôs the only father I‚Äôve ever known) any of this. Can I refuse this money or is that a slap in the face to two people (three if you count my aunt) who seem like they're trying to do the right thing? It‚Äôs a nice sum that would help pay off my student loans but that‚Äôs not worth possibly inviting my bio dad back into my life.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I 100 percent support a decision to keep your bio-dad out of the zone of peace you have created in your mind. If it means face-slapping 2 to 3 lovely people, then that is what you have to do.

However. I am not as certain as you are that is what you have to do.

If this couple were, for example, to put the money in a trust for you (or your heirs), and if you (or your heirs) were not to receive it until your bio-dad shuffled off this mortal coil -- or until you decided you were good and ready to use it -- then could the money sidestep him entirely on its way to serving the emotional needs of the decent?

Or they could give it to a cause meaningful to you. Or a person.

These are just on-the-fly examples. But it seems worth some thought, at least, to try to connect the well-intentioned people. Call it a rebuke to the person who did harm.

Or call it off, the moment the effort feels like it's costing to much. You can, like I said, refuse the money, and say why, and say it's not meant as a slap in their faces because they seem lovely. You are not beholden to them.

It's your call, but talking to your mom and dad might really help.

For today's single mom

QUESTION: You're doing AMAZING - a full time job, 10-year old, maintaining a home, I'm impressed! I did it too, I was a single mom and I know how hard that ish is - and how much I felt like I just wasn't getting it all done.

When I met my now husband though, and explained to him that therapy was just too much and I couldn't do that and then immediately jump into handling the bedtime routine - do you know what he did? He offered to do school pick up to bedtime on those nights so that I could do my appointments and then take myself out for an hour (or read on a park bench) to decompress after. When I struggled during COVID and difficult work schedules? He offered to take turns doing at home school with my child (week on, week off style). He worked to make my life easier, more doable, more manageable - he even did dishes some nights at my house, tidied up when he was "babysitting" (although he would hate to hear me call it that).

In turn, my bandwidth expanded, and I help with stocking his parents freezer when we do elder care visits. I run errands for his parents, helped find them a caregiver, etc. Partners HELP each other. They work TOGETHER to make life manageable and to take on all of the things life throws their way because they're in it together. Someone is out there who will work with you on life, until then you're better off single. Best of luck, you're still doing absolutely amazing at all the things.

X is controlling our special-needs adult child.
Guest Jul 25, 12:04 p.m.
QUESTION: Hi Carolyn, I read your column every morning :-)
Trying to be brief, I have a lovely daughter who is almost 30 and has mild intellectual disability, autism and anxiety. We struggle trying to find things for her to do, and her father (we are divorced for many years) gets angry about things that she wants to do if they are independent ‚Äì he is paranoid about safety and will not let her use WheelTrans, he also gave her a big problem about taking vaccinations because he‚Äôs into right wing crap- and generally, I feel that he‚Äôs holding her back. We both have trouble with his anger, as he will shout on the phone and sometimes stop talking to her when she does something he doesn‚Äôt agree with, which is just awful -but because of the hassle he gives her for every new thing she tries to do, it makes life difficult and she has a lot of trouble standing up and saying what she needs and wants to him. When she tries to do so he talks over her and does not listen, and he thinks that I‚Äôm controlling her. I‚Äôve been trying to prepare her for fighting these battles with him herself, but it‚Äôs hard for her, and I‚Äôm wondering if I should still be inserting myself to help her with him- but I feel that that is undermining my point that he should learn that she has her own mind and feelings, and he needs to start respecting that.
Also would love some tips on how to deal with his bullying behaviour on the phone towards both of us when he feels that we‚Äôre doing the wrong thing. Honestly, I don‚Äôt know why I let him get under my skin, but that shouting and abusive stuff affects me a lot as well. I feel so ashamed that I also still feel controlled by this man, who doesn‚Äôt do any of the hard work for our daughter anyway, and has no real vision for her other than keeping her in a little safe box. If I can‚Äôt stand him and hang the phone up on him when he gets difficult, how can I expect my daughter to?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Your daughter needs to hang up the phone, too, when her father is yelling and talking over her. "We can talk when you're calm." Tap.

But I'm getting ahead of myself. Please go to a few sessions of counseling -- just you -- to learn some basic strategies for dealing with a bully, and to talk through the merits of advocating for your daughter vs allowing her to advocate for herself. Ideally you want a therapist who has relevant training and experience (the American Psychological Association refers to this accreditation) but that's not going to include a lot of clinicians. Maybe you have someone already?

Also, while you're there, talk about ways you can teach your daughter these strategies through role play, or adapt them for her.

You have no cause to feel ashamed. But it is time to get formal instruction on how to stand up for your daughter's needs against someone who is actively working against them. Take care.
Home - The NADD

Proud Dad
Guest Jul 25, 12:25 p.m.
QUESTION: My parents were just 17yo high school seniors when I was born. My mom and both sets of grandparents raised me. My dad was around for taking me to the rare movie or driving me to the library (b/c I always have loved books), but the man never made me dinner or picked me up at school or visited my teacher or took me to the doctor. I played competitive soccer and went to state for track and he never, ever attended one of my events. Not once. Whenever I post something on social media or whenever I am in my dad's presence and something positive comes up about me, he says, "Proud Dad" or "That's MY girl - all the hard work of parenting has paid off!" In restaurants, he'll stop the waiter to tell her that I'm in college and my GPA and she is a chip off the old man block. I have a hard enough time being around my dad but when he takes credit for something he had zero part in, it makes me want to punch him in the face. But, at the same time, saying anything to him would crush his fragile ego, which seems just mean. Help.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: It depends on what you say and how you say it, though, doesn't it?

"Dad, stop taking credit for something you had zero part in."

Ouf.

"Dad -- you're here now. You got off to a tough start but now you're here. That story has its own kind of courage, don't you think?"

Invite him to join you in this line of thinking as an alternative to getting defensive.

If he accepts, then: "We both know you missed a lot. Talking as if you didn't can't be making you feel better. It feels uncomfortable to me. I'd rather we build on what's true -- that I'm your kid, that you're here now, that we're both doing great. That's what I'm proud of."

If his ego can't take that, then that's on him. Plus, it's yours to say. You aren't comfortable. And it's your best chance to have a relationship going forward. Staying silently angry means you just keep tolerating him at best while building up resentment.

Boyfriend needs a project manager

ANSWER: If there is only one man on earth, and if you want to live with a man, then this is something you're going to have to learn to live with.

Otherwise, I hope you never hear yourself thinking that thought again about anything of which there are about 4 billion alternatives.

I am seriously serious. The first step-thing here is to take much better care of yourself. Don't ever throw your agency away so lightly. Over potato-peeling instructions.

The second step is for you to read this Emma comic (scroll past the purchase info for the book version -- it's all there).

Then ask him to read this Emma comic. Then have a conversation to find out whether he now understands the problem with "he just shrugs and says it‚Äôs up to me." If he doesn't, or if he says he understands but then doesn't apply even a minimal economy of arse* toward figuring out how to function, then you have some thinking to do.

This?: "he does do some chores without direction like taking care of the cat, watering the plants, and doing the vacuuming. The cat and plants are his and he says those are things he feels confident in." Is an 8-year-old.

Sisters not mending
Guest Jul 25, 12:41 p.m.
QUESTION: I have two daughters that lost their father literally 2 days before one got married. The sister now married did not want to attend an elaborate funeral service the next day for her father. This, I might add was not something he wanted. He specifically wanted a small service in another state. Not only that but a relative got involved in a lengthy estate settlement teaming up with one sister against the other. Fast forward, i now have grandchildren in two states with siblings not wanting to have family get togethers and excluding each other from family events. Seriously can't we get over this?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Apparently not.

Plenty of families can't ever gather all at once because of geography; it's still not great that they can't, but presumably they dwell on it less, just because the reason is less emotionally fraught than a grudge. Somehow a grudge feels more fixable than geography.

But it's not. Not here, not for you. You have what life handed you. So I suggest you make the best of it and alternate seeing your kids and grandkids in the two different states, and remind yourself as needed not to waste any of this time on the futility of wishing things were otherwise. Because no doubt you've already lost enough time and joy to this feud.

Boyfriend needs a project manager

QUESTION: I had the boyfriend (now husband) who didn't do things around the house without direction. It was frustrating. We had several task-related conversations, that, like the letter writer, didn't produce much change. Re: Boyfriend needs a project manager
Guest Jul 25, 1:01 p.m.
QUESTION: While understanding the frustration and not disagreeing with Carolyn's points, there could be a couple of things going on with the passive boyfriend.

Does he have controlling parents? And, since he's a man behaving this way with a woman, does he especially have a controlling mother who is the uber-boss of everything in the home? This could be a lifetime of training for him that isn't going to be undone in just 14 months.

If he feels confident with the cat and the plants, he might be better off taking full ownership of a couple of new household tasks at a time, rather than pitching in with a lot of stuff that he still feels a need to get instructions on.

Is it possible he has an executive function issue? My husband and son are both brilliant in general and in their jobs, but they both have diagnosed executive function issues (one b/c of ADHD and one b/c of autism). They lack confidence in new tasks in which I have expertise--not because of ego, but because of fear of doing it wrong. People with that issue can develop incredibly strong functionality in some areas and struggle mightily in others. Practice and consistency helps, which is why I (with behavioural science training) say the boyfriend needs to work on mastering fewer things before moving on to others.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Thank you for laying these possibilities out in such neat summaries.

wanting to post a too-long poem as an offering to the woman who was tempted to say "no" to grandpar
Guest Jul 25, 1:03 p.m.
QUESTION: wanting to offer a too-long poem as wisdom and support to the woman who wanted to say "no" to a gift from her grandparents. The poem speaks beautifully to holding on to all of our ancestors, not just one, not just her awful father.
A HOUSE CALLED TOMORROW

You are not fifteen, or twelve, or seventeen‚Äî
You are a hundred wild centuries
And fifteen, bringing with you
In every breath and in every step
Everyone who has come before you,
All the yous that you have been,
The mothers of your mother,
The fathers of your father.
If someone in your family tree was trouble,
A hundred were not:
The bad do not win‚Äînot finally,
No matter how loud they are.
We simply would not be here
If that were so.
You are made, fundamentally, from the good.
With this knowledge, you never march alone.
You are the breaking news of the century.
You are the good who has come forward
Through it all, even if so many days
Feel otherwise. But think:
When you as a child learned to speak,
It‚Äôs not that you didn‚Äôt know words‚Äî
It‚Äôs that, from the centuries, you knew so many,
And it‚Äôs hard to choose the words that will be your own.
From those centuries we human beings bring with us
The simple solutions and songs,
The river bridges and star charts and song harmonies
All in service to a simple idea:
That we can make a house called tomorrow.
What we bring, finally, into the new day, every day,
Is ourselves. And that‚Äôs all we need
To start. That‚Äôs everything we require to keep going.
Look back only for as long as you must,
Then go forward into the history you will make.
Be good, then better. Write books. Cure disease.
Make us proud. Make yourself proud.
And those who came before you? When you hear thunder,
Hear it as their applause.

Alberto R√≠os ¬© 2018

Alberto R√≠os was born in 1952, Alberto R√≠os is the inaugural state poet laureate of Arizona and the author of many poetry collections, including A Small Story about the Sky (Copper Canyon Press, 2015). In 1981, he received the Walt Whitman Award for his collection Whispering to Fool the Wind (Sheep Meadow Press, 1982). He currently serves as a Chancellor of the Academy of American Poets.

Thank you.

Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: So escalating to a license-appointment no-show, a blame rant and a month's suspension was okay, though?

ü§¶‚Äç‚ôÄÔ∏è

Both of you are emotional flame-throwers, standing on the earth you scorched and believing you're in the right.

Both of you also, for that reason, might want to take a moment to sit in gladness and sweet relief that you didn't get married. Not until your natural (or at least after a beat and a deep breath) response to conflict in your relationship is to regulate your emotions or pause until you can.

This is not to say you were wrong to end things with your boyfriend -- "if he didn‚Äôt want to marry me after 4 years, he was never going to marry me and I needed to move on" seems like the only call here to me. But then you brought out Harvey Keitel and his bleach bottles to remove all traces of now-ex-love-of-my-life DNA, and I got a little scared.

I can't talk to him (ex-BF, not Harvey), but for you all I want to advise is a big picture look at this whole thing. It is hard when relationships end, yes, it hurts, but this is just So Much Escalation. And I would absolutely tell him the same thing if I could, and nudge both of you to counseling (individual!) to work on communication. Or read about nonviolent communication and its practice, if the time/money/access is an issue for therapy.

In a basic sense, one of you really needed to talk to the other, plainly, long before it erupted. I think it's clear his contents were under more pressure, but it was a both of you thing, not just on him.

As for now, having the sister come to you is not cool. Responding rewards that. Terrible spot she put you in.

At some point, though, I think it would be appropriate to unblock him, when you are ready to talk. Not forever or at length, just when you're ready to stop this he's-not-talking, then you're-not-talking war.

Still seeking justice from the man who killed my husband
Guest Jul 18, 11:03 a.m.
QUESTION: Two days after I told my husband he was going to be a father, he was hit and killed by a distracted driver. Because the driver was 19, he got off with probation and community service. I read an impact statement, so did my in-laws, and several friends of my husband but none of it mattered. All that mattered to the judge was that driver was young and had his whole life in front of him so he got off with a slap on the wrist. I sued for wrongful death but it was settled out of court and the insurance company paid the money so that guy will never really pay in any way for what he did. He never even said he was sorry to us. He gets to live his life and my son grows up without a father. I know life is unfair but not usually this heartbreakingly unfair. I wish I could stop thinking about how much I hate that driver but I can‚Äôt. Only my in-laws understand since they feel the same way. Everyone keeps telling us over and over: ‚Äúit was an accident‚Äô, ‚Äúhis brain isn‚Äôt even fully mature‚Äù, ‚Äúlet it go because nothing will bring Don back‚Äù and so on. I posted on a grief forum how I'll never recover until we get some justice for my husband and they said I should smear the driver's name all over social media and the internet so everyone knows what he did, so it follows him forever, when he tries to get a job or date or anything. Maybe that‚Äôs mean but it will make me and my in-laws feel so much better. Is there any reason not to do that?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: You say "I'll never recover until we get some justice for my husband."

What would you consider "justice"? How would you define "recover"?

Because all see here is vengeance. And that's neither recovery nor justice. Your solution is just taking the anger you have inside and spreading it as far as the internet can take it -- which we all know is a force you no longer control the moment you unleash it.

And all you're doing is throwing to strangers, like raw meat to wolves, what has always been the proper responsibility of both the justice system and this man's conscience. You're doing this simply because you don't think these two corrective bodies gave you enough. But for one thing, there was never going to be enough, because there never is in the case of an accidental loss of innocent life. It's impossible. There is no eye for this eye -- which itself isn't justice anyway. And, another thing, his conscience may be hard at work on this every day if his life. You don't know that it isn't.

I see every detail of your side of the story, I do, and it's awful -- I hear your grief and sympathize while still adamantly opposing what these grief forum sirens are luring you into. It's terrible and it's as far from healing as I can conceive of at this point.

Life actually is this heartbreakingly unfair. Every damn day.

You may recall former First Lady Laura Bush also did what this 19-year-old driver did, when she was 17. She ran a stop sign and the accident she caused killed a 17-year-old. She said herself she did not reach out to the family at the time to apologize, and was wracked by guilt for years. Her reckoning is one you might want to read about -- when you're ready, but before you go smearing anyone out of certainty of what is just.

My friend Ann Gerhart wrote about her in "The Perfect Wife" in 2005, and Bush herself wrote about it in I think a 2010 memoir.

I am very sorry for your loss. I hope you will -- beg you to -- address the grief, keep going, vs. succumb to the rage.

Non-Beach Blues
Guest Jul 18, 11:13 a.m.
QUESTION: Hi Carolyn,

Next week is my family's annual beach vacation. It is A Thing - my family has been doing this since I was a toddler and I'm in my early 40s now. Not only will my immediate family be there, but so will all of my relatives since they live in the beach town.

I stopped going on this vacation a couple of years ago because my chaotic, alcoholic brother often made it so difficult. Intellectually, I know I made the right choice for my mental health. Emotionally, it's really hard and I spend the week sadly thinking about everything I'm missing. Any advice on how to get through this week every year?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Counterprogramming, right? I mean -- you aren't sitting home alone, flipping through old beach week albums, are you?

Actually, there is something to this. You kind of have to go all in on distraction, or all in on your inability to be distracted. So if you have some great other thing you can be doing, so great that it can get you to stop thinking about what you're missing, then book that annually in this timeslot.

If instead your hard feelings about missing the trip will ruin whatever else you plan for the time, then make this your annual Chores of Misery Week -- attic, basement, old files, DMV, photo purge, weeding, bring your worst. Just, suffer. (Good food and music, though.)

I'm a nurse so when I work my 12hr shifts, I just cannot get to her on those days and I don't think the animals are being properly cared for. Her animals are her only joy and quite honestly have always been her primary joy. My husband and adult kids simply are not animal people and these pets need a lot of care. Also, what happens to all these animals when she passes? I don't want to hurt my mom and I think removing animals might actually kill my mom. Feeling really stuck.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Can you/she hire someone come in? If $ is an issue, would someone from the rescue group volunteer to come in 1 or 2x a day? Her continuing to work for the rescue could be valuable enough to the organization to make the tradeoff worth it, as long as a quick check-in is all the animals need to receive proper care. Another option is to talk to the techs at her vet, to see if any are taking on side work, or know someone who is. They may be connected to reputable petsitters.

Meanwhile, the unsteady walking and the dogs could be a hazard. Does your mom have a way to alert you? A life-alert-type button, or a smart watch?

Can't say I love you
Guest Jul 18, 11:40 a.m.
QUESTION: I come from a really dysfunctional family. My parents are at the end of their lives (early 90s; mom has early dementia, dad does not but he has some physical health issues). Im in my late 50s with 4 other siblings. I live nearby my parents, who live in an active retirement community.

My parents were never touchy-feely. And I mean no touching, no hugging, no "I love yous" with me or my siblings. Ever. Honestly, if they ever said it to me, I cannot recall it or it would have been before I was in grade school. They do not touch or hug me to this day (although their method of discipline back in the day was physical hitting up until I was in high school, same for siblings - it could be considered abusive by today's standards). Likewise, I don't touch/hug them. When I see them (pretty often), hellos and goodbyes are simply stated.

Obviously, this has impacted me through adulthood. I'm single, no kids, and have had only a few short relationships.

What do i say/do when the end comes? It would so forced to say I love you. Should I find a therapist now or after they die? So much emotional baggage. Where do i even start?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Oh. Okay. I would take this slowly.

Especially since you don't have to decide anything now about when the end comes for your parents.

And it may simply not come up at all -- the opportunity may not happen, or may slip by. The Hollywood death scene where people gather and say their goodbyes is less of a thing than you may think. If the end does come in deathbed form, then it also often comes with spotty consciousness. I'm spelling all this out mostly to ease your mind, so you don't have to fret about a big farewell pronouncement that your life with your parents hasn't prepared you for.

That said, I hope you will tend to the questions and discomfort you're dealing with for your own reasons. If you're thinking a therapist would be a good idea at some point, then the best point is now! Sure. Air some of the stuff that has bothered you. Set down some of the baggage. If you don't feel like unpacking it all, that's okay -- just set it down for now. One thing at a time.

I also wish I weren't answering this live, because your situation strikes me as one that is so good for fiction -- meaning, I have wisps of memories of novels that really get at where you are, but I can't ... get .. them. And I think the beauty of a novel is the ability to inhabit someone else's life, to achieve comprehension and feelings from an armchair.

I know there are serious readers out there. Can anyone recommend a novel or three that takes a character from an emotionally remote and inexpressive family into connection and awakening? Thank you in advance.

Anyway. These are places to start. Another place to start, quite basically, is aesthetic or therapeutic touch, like a manicure or haircut. It may seem incongruous, but what you describe sounds like fairly extreme detachment from human contact. Maintaining regular appointments is a small thing you can do.

Re: Justice vs Healing
Guest Jul 18, 11:44 a.m.
QUESTION: As a former criminal prosecutor turned civil attorney, I want to assure you that ‚Äújustice‚Äù was served here, however unsatisfying it may feel to you and your family. The state pursued the case and rendered judgement and punishment according to the law. The civil case provided compensatory damages. As a former criminal prosecutor. This is justice. Carolyn is right that what you are describing is not justice, but vengeance. And neither the criminal justice system nor the civil legal system is designed to deliver vengeance or retribution.

Having worked with many families of victims, including of horrific crimes, I have tremendous sympathy for your plight. But Carolyn is right that no amount of suffering that you or others inflict on this young man will ever make you feel whole. And that‚Äôs because your feelings exist entirely in your mind and body. The healing you seek - and that you and your family truly need - will not come from the outside. Healing comes from the inside, from processing your own emotions and grief, from learning to cope and move forward with your life as best you can.

I hope you will find your way to a skilled grief counselor or program to begin this work of healing as soon as possible. Consider individual therapy for yourself and also group grief counseling so you can travel this journey with others and feel less alone in the world.

Do this for yourself. But also do this for your son. He has lost his father. I hope he will not also lose all or part of his mother to the grief over this tragedy.

UPDATE:
Since I didn't see a follow up with reasons the OP can't leave, I will try to answer without it.
First, always, I will urge you to get away from abuse. To interrogate your reasons you "can't." To work with your therapist to plan your way out.
There are interior approaches too:
-Know your abuser and avoid them as much as possible. Except nothing but abuse from them.
-Expect no help from your family.
-Limit your contact with all of them to what is absolutely necessary for business.
-Keep great records. Receipts for everything.
-Assume even this won‚Äôt protect you. Don‚Äôt see documentation as saving yourself, see it as doing what you can.
-Know that this is a "them" problem ‚Äì because good people don‚Äôt do this.
-Start rebuilding your friendships.

I'm inclined to stay out of the rest of it because it's really between them, but since Mom doesn't communicate directly with someone when there's a problem, it's very unlikely she'll talk to him about it. So, I'm left to listen to her complaints, despite repeating, "Talk to Brother!" and leaving the room (she turned to my bf and continued the rant).

What can I do? For the record, I've become very close with the gf and enjoy having her on these trips.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: You can do all kinds of things.

You can stop traveling with Mom.

You can stop responding when she complains.

You can say, "I've become very close with the gf and enjoy having her on these trips."

You can say, "Mom, if you complain to me about GF, then I won't respond. If you keep going, then I will leave. If you continue your rant with bf, then he will leave." (Make sure bf leaves -- full empowerment.)

You can start singing "99 Bottles of Beer on the Wall" every time she starts in.

So the question is, what are you prepared to do?

I feel like Sean Connery in the church in "The Untouchables."

Babies in the beach house
Guest Jul 18, 12:57 p.m.
QUESTION: Hi Carolyn - I've rented a beach house with the same five friends for 15 years, and the plans have evolved over time to incorporate partners, pets, work schedules and food/alcohol needs. We added my daughter two summers ago, and this summer another friend and I will both have baby boys. When I sent our traditional "30 days to go" planning email this week, one person responded that having babies in the house "really changes the vibe." She said the child free group had been talking and proposed the families take the house the first half of the week, then the child free group the second half, and we can all hang together on the arrival/departure day. I was really surprised, hurt and angry (the week went alcohol free to support her sobriety, which also "changed the vibe," but were genuinely happy to support her). The other parent friend and I want to respond that we're staying all week because this is our planned and highly anticipated time away, and they're welcome to skip or leave early. My husband thinks the trip will be tense now regardless and we should just agree. What would you do here? The rental is in my name, my family paid extra for a room for the kids, and we're past the cancellation window.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Babies in the beach house totally wreck the vibe. "Change" was being nice. And maybe I can say this only because I was in the no-babies camp before I was in the babies camp myself, in a beach house gathering with the same group annually, as it happens (on point life experience for this one, I must say): but I'm going to ask you to re-think the "really surprised, hurt and angry." All three.

There is no surprise here -- little kids absolutely demolish a "same five friends for 15 years" beach house experience. I love babies, they're magical nubbins. But they cry, need gear, nap on day-trip-killing schedules, get up at the crack of piss, and either go to bed while it's still daylight -- or, worse, they don't. I'm sorry, they're just anathema to a relaxed group hang unless you have a rare super chill one. Toddlers? Forget about it. Both take their parents and parents' attention away constantly, because they're supposed to. And your friends are losing that time with you. So where is the surprise in their noticing the loss and not being super thrilled with paying big bucks for your distraction?

And the hurt and anger -- let's talk about those. Your friends don't hate your kids. This isn't personal. It's just ... it changed, not for the better for them, and it sucks. And this may be one of their few vacation weeks this year, and now they're working it around nap time? Don't hate on them or -- this is so important -- take it personally. They're just telling the truth and saying out loud they don't want one of their few precious weeks off to accommodate poop and crying.

If they need you to return the favor years from now when your kids are grown and theirs are small, it might irk you, too, but maybe less.

You have some of a point with the sobriety, but not all.

If you want to have a constructive convo about this, then drop the surprise/hurt/anger, channel the experience of being babyless on a baby-centered beach week, and own up: "I get it. It is a changed vibe. All I did was add a room and assume that was that, vs think how the group was affected.

"It's way too late for big changes this summer, but we should talk about what would work better for everyone."

I suggest you ask as part of this, "Do you see our tradition continuing for another 15 years, another 30?" Because life is change. Kids are just part of it. And doing the work through these "hard" years can really pay off, but it takes commitment and communication. (And fewer defenses.)
Post has 0 replies and 662 Likes
662Press Enter to expand
Mother won‚Äôt stop asking to include relatives in my wedding ceremony
Guest Jul 18, 1:06 p.m.
QUESTION: Hi, my wedding is coming up this October, and while my parents have been wonderfully supportive and loving in so many ways of me and my fianc√© ‚ÄúDavid‚Äù over the past year in the lead up, every few months my Mom will occasionally drop into chats that she hopes I can have her sister, my Aunt ‚ÄúRachel‚Äù as a reader during the ceremony, because Rachel is my Godmother, and Rachel‚Äôs son excluded her from his wedding (various reasons, but partly because due to a nasty divorce between Rachel and her now ex-husband) several years ago and Rachel was very hurt by it. At first, my Mom started a year ago with ‚ÄúOh just a thought‚Äù suggestions about what I could add, and eventually it‚Äôs escalated to ‚ÄúI want you to have your Aunt read a passage at the wedding, it would mean so much to her and she is your Godmother.‚Äù Our wedding is three months away, and I know David and I are going to meet with our officiant in a month or two to discuss what we want our ceremony to look like. Earlier in the planning it was easier to brush it off with saying it‚Äôs far away and we haven‚Äôt figured out details yet, but that‚Äôs going to get harder to do now. While I am keeping my mother on as much of an information diet as possible, I‚Äôm dreading the inevitability of her bringing up yet another ‚ÄúHave you found a reading for your Aunt yet‚Äù guilt trip, and her freaking out when I say no or if she gets me to waffle.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: K, coupla things.

1. It's not "relatives in my wedding," it's Aunt Rachel.

2. If it's no, then say no! I read the Q once and skimmed it a second time and I'm seeing brush offs and info diets and dread, but I'm not seeing, "Mom, I do not want Aunt Rachel to do a reading." A year ago.

3. If she takes it badly, then you can explain if you want, but you don't have to. It's your mom, though, and weddings are big feelings in tents, and you've run away from her for months. So: "I understand this is really important to you for a bunch of reasons having to do with AR's divorce and her son's wedding. But I hope you can understand my discomfort with the use of my wedding as the vehicle for her makeup ceremony? It just seems off in so many ways. Please let me have this without mixing in other stuff -- even my beloved godmother's stuff." A year ago would have been nice, but now will do.

4. You could also have Aunt Rachel do a reading. Do I disagree with what your mom is doing? 100 percent, with a marzipan dove on top. But it's a wedding, and sometimes a reading can be really nice. It's not like she's asking you to fit in a Sousaphone solo.

5. Either way, just Own It Already. Or don't get married. This is not a typo or brain spasm.

Because being part of a married couple is so hard on a person's identity. You have to remain yourself while making decisions as a unit and often thinking as one. You can become overbearing or vanish without even knowing how you got there. And so your ability to say "no" to Mom about Aunt Rachel is one of the many useful drills and test runs you've been handed leading up to your marriage. You've got an F so far, but there's still plenty of time to pass it. Get to it.

Those who don't like the kid-centric trip seem to appreciate having a place to retreat, and those of us with kids have liked being in a house where people are of course trying to be considerate, but our housemates are more accustomed to and understanding of early wakeups and bedtimes, middle-of-the-night challenges, conversational discretion in the hot tub, etc.

Adult Friend Makin
Guest Jul 18, 2:03 p.m.
QUESTION: Totally random. When adults talk about being lonely everyone suggests volunteering. I have always thought it was a stupid idea. I would love to hear some other points of view in the comments.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: How is this random? It's what we do here.

Here's the part that is not stupid. The basic, known, as-proven-as-can-be recipe for adult-friend-makin is this:

1. Interact with a blend of new people.

2. Do so on a regular basis with some frequency. (Once a year will probably not help you much.)

3. Have some purpose that brings you together. This can serve as conversation prompt (icebreaker for the shy especially) and common-interest filter.

This is optional but ideal:

4. Have some project that requires you to work together. This allows you to be parallel and not talk, or talk about what you're doing, or go for weeks observing how you contribute to a community vs. going blah-blah-blah about whatever.

So. These 3-4 elements can fit into a lot of different scenarios. ONE of those scenarios is volunteer work. But it's hardly the only one. That's why it's included so much. But any time this topic comes up, people usually throw out a whole list of ideas because it has to be something you enjoy. You, personally, would be stupid to try to make friends volunteering. I feel pretty good about that.

Also, these things are not going to do all the social work for us -- at some point, the people trying to make friends will have to take a leap and say, "Hey, do you want to go to dinner sometime?" or otherwise take a chance of face-planting. It's just how it is.

My grandmother‚Äôs first husband was killed in a highway collision when my aunt was a newborn. My grandmother was about 20, had no job, and had an infant to care for in the wake of the loss. My mother was born about 10 years after the accident, and her childhood was marked by an extremely unstable home life. I‚Äôm sure you know that vengeance won‚Äôt bring him back, but it won‚Äôt set things right either. I don‚Äôt say this to be cruel, I only say this because in the midst of all this heartbreak is your son. Your son is a part of your husband that is going to live on. Yes, that 19 year old has his whole life ahead of him, but so does your son. Which of those lives you give your attention to is going to affect your son more than it will ever affect that 19 year old kid.
I don‚Äôt know the name of my aunt‚Äôs late father, I don‚Äôt know how the case was resolved in courts or in the court of public opinion. All I know is the generational impact this tragedy has passed onto me. I know that my aunt grew up with a despondent, emotionally unavailable mother. I know that my mother grew up in the explosive conflict between my aunt and my grandmother when the system reached its limits.
Your son doesn‚Äôt need vengeance, he needs you. Your in-laws may share your rage, but they don‚Äôt share your responsibility. They lost their son, but you haven‚Äôt lost yours.

I CANNOT forgive my father-in-law for wrecking my gardens
Guest Jul 11, 11:09 a.m.
QUESTION: I don‚Äôt know how I‚Äôm ever going to forgive my father-in-law for wrecking my gardens. When she knew she was dying, she dug them up and brought them over and we planted them together. They looked bad last year due to transplant shock but this spring they were rebounding.
We went on vacation in early June and my FiL took the opportunity to have a landscaper rip out my pollinator and herb gardens, putting in non-native plants, mulch and edging. He says he spent a fortune and expects me to be grateful. I am devastated and let him know it so he thinks I‚Äôm awful. I don‚Äôt care. I can‚Äôt stand to look at him. Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Whoo. This is the thing: "even though his dad screwed up badly he meant well." His dad did not mean well in the slightest. Obviously you two had talked about it and you had held your "no," so he went behind your back with purpose and forethought.

Your husband, now, he means well for sure -- he is just not there, to the point where he can see his father for who he is. (You are right; profound control issues, rooted in cowardice.) Let's give it a "yet," though. Your husband is not there yet. Because he is supporting you, not his dad, and that's so important.

In fact, my advice is to support his support: Accept your husband's offer to replant the garden with your pollenators. For yourself. for your marriage. I am so sorry. I feel sickened on your behalf.

2392Press Enter to expand
Mom talks with food in mouth‚Äîick
Guest Jul 11, 11:25 a.m.
QUESTION: My mom is almost 90 and, thankfully, in great health. Over the past few years, she‚Äôs developed a habit of frequently speaking at meals with food in her mouth. It‚Äôs gross.

Our family discusses this habit with each other. But we‚Äôre stuck about how to discuss it with her. Our dad, also a healthy 90, seems not to notice. He‚Äôs not mentioned it to us.

Next month we‚Äôre taking a family trip. For a week, we‚Äôll have most meals together. That means lots of opportunities for chewing and talking. How does a conflict-adverse family discuss this with Mom beforehand?

Thank you.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: She's (almost) 90! You can't look off to the side? Take deep, centering breaths? Focus on her active participation?

Seriously. Before you decide something must be said, do some mental role playing. The business of being 90, even in "great health," is not for wusses. Senses and reaction times simply aren't what they are for everyone a generation younger -- or more if there are grands and great-grands involved. Let's just assume a mind of undiminished sharpness, no ageism here. Eyes probably hardened into the correction zone somewhere between age 40-60 (90-ish percent chance), so we're now plus-decades on that. There is a near certainty of at least some hearing loss. Just these two in combination affect the ability of a person to stay in a conversation with younger people -- unless there's attention to different abilities. There will be muscle atrophy, too, no doubt, and tooth decay and/or dental work, which affect eating and swallowing.

So your repartee with Ma while she's eating taxes her abilities at 90 a lot more than it does yours, assuming you all fall within typical ranges for your age. Needing to focus on things she used to take for granted, meanwhile, would understandably take her focus off things she used to be careful about, such as not talking with a mouthful. Waiting could mean she misses her opportunities to jump in and says nothing at all.

So a little leeway, okay?

Approaching 30 and feeling dread
Guest Jul 11, 12:06 p.m.
QUESTION: I turn 30 this year and I have a constant feeling of dread. On paper, there is so much I should be happy about - great friends, family, super intense but fulfilling job in the best city in the world. For now, I've reached my academic and professional goals. I have a very full life. But I just feel so lonely. My work is social in that I have great work friends and a lot of my job is spent talking with people. I have plans with friends several nights a week. I visit my parents (3 hours away) 1/month. But I dread going back to my empty apartment in the evening and I dread waking up in the morning to just myself.

I recently broke up with my ex and I miss him so much it hurts. I also just miss having someone there and I miss being there for him. But I know that regardless of having/not having a relationship, I don't want to dread being alone. Everyone says "go to therapy!" but I'm trying that and it's not helping. It's mostly just me blabbing for the whole session. I feel guilty for feeling so blah when it feels like I have everything going for me. How do I reset?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I don't know how "recently" you broke up, but feeling lonely in your home as you recover from that sounds right. Healthy. Normal. It's not going to feel good, but sometimes it's healthy and normal to feel bad. It's part of the healing process.

So having social friends and good work friends and parents you can and want to see often, and a fulfilling job, and a city you love? Plus therapy as a safety net as you heal? That all sounds like good stuff to have in place as you go through the emotional hellscape of post-breakup recovery.

If I'm right that that's what this is, then there's no shortcut "reset" option. You kind of just have to feel it until it doesn't hurt so much.

Except the guilt -- that's crazy talk. There's no "everything going for you" exception when you're hurting and you miss someone. Breakups and other losses come for us all. I'm sorry. Make sure your closer friends know you're struggling so they can be there for you extra.

(If I'm wrong ... or even if I'm not, then I'm thinking more truth-telling in therapy is warranted. Admit the guilt and the "It's mostly just me blabbing.")

2338Press Enter to expand
Walking on eggshells
Guest Jul 11, 12:22 p.m.
QUESTION: Dear Carolyn,
I‚Äôm married to a man with two teenage children; we also have two young kids together. (It‚Äôs a second marriage for both of us.) He has a high-pressure job and carries a lot of stress, which often sets the tone for our entire household. We find ourselves walking on eggshells, and his OCD tendencies‚Äîlikely a way of coping‚Äîare making home life more difficult for everyone. I‚Äôve raised this with him gently and directly several times, but little has changed. I also feel like a single parent to our young children, as he‚Äôs often mentally elsewhere, absorbed by work or stress. How can I support our family and create a more stable, peaceful and engaging home without constantly confronting him or waiting for him to change?

- Overwhelmed
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: This sounds like a couples-counseling situation, not so much in a let's-dig-into-our-feelings way but specifically for the purpose of setting up practical adaptations to get the household working better.

Think about it: He's stressed from work pressure, then he comes home and stresses out you and the kids -- and that's when he's theoretically supposed to be decompressing from work. And then on top of that, he's going to do the mental/emotional labor of figuring out how to change his coping methods into something more family-friendly -- while also stepping up to his fair share of the household labor and having more meaningful engagement with his kids.

You're not wrong (by implication) that your having to function as de facto single parent is unfair, that it's tough on the kids, that he's the one whose approach needs to change. But being right and having sympathy for his predicament are not mutually exclusive.

Getting objective, trained eyes on this can help you two figure this out. Maybe how to give your husband some room to decompress when he gets home, for example -- built into a routine -- so that he's able to be present for all of you, instead of limping along basically incapacitated. How to add hired care strategically to ease pressure points. Etc.

Making it an appointment with you and a trained pro means it's something you work on together, in a dedicated time and space, sharing the workload, instead of asking him to figure it out by himself mid-stream.

Trying to solve stress while stressed is setting yourselves up for failure, with all of the best intentions. Stepping back and treating it as a design problem, then hiring a designer, sounds more like it. Certainly less charged.

Husband's friends are a bad influence
Guest Jul 11, 12:29 p.m.
QUESTION: Hi Carolyn,

My husband and I were struggling in our 15 year marriage. We tried a 6 month separation period. We both worked on ourselves and missed each other during this time. We are both in a place where we'd like to try again.

While separated, he became part of a new social group, linked to a casual friend of both of ours. He seems to have spent some time venting about me and the marriage to these friends. The casual friend, it turns out, felt this was a time to take sides, and he took my husband's side. My husband was ok with that (ugh).

I can't see us reconnecting if these friends stay in the picture. I understand that he needed a social life during a difficult time, but he agrees that they aren't really fans of me or supportive of our marriage. However, he is very unwilling to give them up. He has even lied to me about them, pretending he has cut ties.
What do you advise??
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Of course they sided against you -- your husband was venting about you and the marriage. They got the full dump of everything your husband wasn't liking about you at the time. (You didn't have a friend you confided in?) If you were to have heard an anonymized version of the information he was dumping, then you might not have liked you, either.

I can see (but don't agree with) your "We can't move forward with them in the picture" argument. Somewhat like, newly sober person can't move forward with drinking buddies still around. But you've hit the asked-and-answered point already. You drew the line for your husband and he said nah, friends stay.

So that leaves you with three choices: quit on the reconciliation, find a way to make peace with the friends, or convert this to an unwinnable recurring fight.

There are arguments for 1 or 2. My bias, there's never an argument for 3 except a delight in perpetual suffering.

Argument for 1, the lying! Omg. What is he, 15 and sneaking out on his parents?

Argument for 2, your ask was an overreach. Telling partners they can't see someone is a this-already-isn't-working first step. Either they get it without being told or you trust them without having to control their friendships.

In this case, a viable option for you was to say to husband, "Hey, this was your dump-out friend who heard the worst about me. Not going to be an issue?" And you trust his answer. Just as he would trust you on your staying close to the person who heard your worst about him.

A workable household is not "an additional demand on all the other demands."

Again -- it's to lessen demands.

So I'm seeing stress, eggshell-walking, stubbornness, mention of OCD. Are you ever concerned he will react explosively? Because then you have a different thing, which I hope you are talking about with your therapist, if true. That would nudge this toward a safety issue for you and the kids.

Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Good for you for getting yourself and your life where you want them to be.

That is what matters. Not how others respond to your sobriety, and not where other family members are in their relationship with alcohol. I understand they're family and you care, but their opinions of you aren't your business, and their substance use isn't, either.

What you choose to tell them, though, is. So I hope you will be forthcoming, if that's what you want to do -- because you want to be honest about you, not because you want to fix them. I suggest you line up support before you do, though, so you have someone to talk to about their reactions. In case there are some difficult ones.

Hold Your Horses
Guest Jun 27, 11:03 a.m.
QUESTION: My 16yo daughter has her first boyfriend. He is a nice kid. The boyfriend's mom has taken to inviting my daughter to everything and saying that my daughter needs to go "because you are family." The mom wants my daughter to go to church with them every week and was very insistent that she attend their older child's college graduation. Daughter's boyfriend thinks right now - age 16 - that he's interested in joining the military and the mom is telling my daughter that she can live with them - if needed - while he's at basic training. Why on earth would she need to live with them? My daughter says no to many events - she has a job and other friends and us, her family - and my daughter says that she likes hanging out with the boyfriend. My daughter feels like the mom doesn't take no for an answer. The boyfriend just seems use avoidance has his best tactic with his mom. I'm very uncomfortable with his mom's behavior. They are just 16 and have only been dating for 2 months. Is it appropriate for me to talk to this mom? Is that a big no-no and I'm suppose to work through my daughter? I've met this kid's mom over the years and she has a very big, pushy personality.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: That's completely bonkers.

It's most appropriate for you to talk to your daughter -- or I should say to listen, a lot, and save your talk for when it really really matters because you want her coming to you.

With all the eye-popping lunacy going on in your letter, the biggest thing is deceptively ordinary: "My daughter feels like the mom doesn't take no for an answer." This means your daughter is talking to you, trusting you (huge) and she is not okay with the mother's lunacy. It is so, so importanrt that you keep these three things intact.

Nothing you consider doing to "stop" (ha) the overbearing mother is worth risking those three pillars of your daughter's mental health: She is talking to you, trusting you and she is not okay with intrusions on her own agency.

This is not to say that the mother is completely off limits. There may be a time where you need to let her know in no uncertain terms where her family business leaves off and yours begins. But that too is a save-it-for-when-you-have-to thing. The biggest threat to your daughter's trust and emerging sense of agency right now isn't this mother, necessarily; dragging everyone into an ill-advised power struggle is the threat. That's why your position, for now, is to focus on building your daughter's strength to remain true to herself through this pressure.

That's another reason "My daughter feels like the mom doesn't take no for an answer" is so pivotal. Your daughter is identifying the problem and opening the door to your help with it. Much of that help, again, will appropriately be in the form of listening. But it is okay to say to her, unequivocally:

"There is no such thing as 'won't take no for an answer.' I am serious: Your no is final. People of character respect that. She may not want to accept your no and feel entitled to push for what she wants -- lots of people do that, this isn't just about her. But that's always their problem, not yours. I hope you understand and trust this." [pause for absorption and acknowledgment.]

Ideally it leads to further conversation. Either way, on the way out, be sure to remind her: "If you ever need me to back your 'no,' I will. And you can talk to me any time."

Acceptance
Guest Jun 27, 11:22 a.m.
QUESTION: Late 30s - I think I‚Äôve finally accepted that if I won‚Äôt be enmeshed and feeding approval supply to my mother, that she does not like or even love me.

She was abusive but I was trying to make things work with gray rocking and sticking to pleasant subjects.

Now she doesn‚Äôt reach out, she doesn‚Äôt say ‚Äúlove you‚Äù back any more. She seems to be living her best life. While I want that for her, I still feel some grief and any time I sit still long enough my eyes just start leaking water. But I do accept it, and honestly I think overall I‚Äôm much happier than when I stuck around and engaged with her cruelty.

I guess I just would like any suggestions to speed along the process of truly getting over this. Thanks!
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I am so sorry.

Think about someone who would be cruel and abusive to their own child. Who would go through the expected demostrations of love only in return for getting what they want -- as the back half of a transaction.

Does this sound like someone who has the capacity for "love" or "like," at least as you have come to understand these things? Meaning ... let's say, based on meaningful shared history, experience, connection, time, acts of giving, the joy of seeing thing through each other's eyes, comfort, familiarity.

To me, these things are anathema to the transactional dysfunction you describe. (And of course it's not just your mom, it's a pretty familiar form of emotional sickness.)

So I think the kindest thing you can do is finish the last few stages of the acceptance process and recognize that the mechanism in her that would produce the connection you crave is just ... not there.

It is, I agree, horribly sad that you got a mother who apparently does not have the capacity to love. It is, however, no reflection on you, nothing you've done, not a sign in any way that you aren't lovable.

In my experience, the combination of allowing yourself to grieve the first part, and then focusing on the second part, is healing.

My sister's pain.
Guest Jun 27, 11:43 a.m.
QUESTION: Hi Carolyn. It would really be great if you could help me. Right as I type, my sister is typing me about our father, who is deceased. He is all that she wants to talk about these days. It started with a psychic who told her that her depression was caused by our dad sexually abusing her. She was like, "Oh yeah! He did!" No specific memories, but just a "creepy" feeling that she's always had around him. She says she went to a therapist who agrees that 100% she was sexually abused by dad. I ALWAYS want to believe the woman, but I DON'T BELIEVE HER. I feel terrible that she feels this way. But I don't know how to talk to her! HELP!
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Oh my. Even if we were given an instruction manual for life, I don't think there'd be anything about how a sibling responds to this.

What may help you here is that you don't have to have a response, per se. You don't have to believe (or disbelieve) her. You don't know what happened and presumably never will, and your father is gone, so life is not reasonably asking you to have an opinion or take a side.

Your sister, meanwhile, does seem to want to tell you about this, but you are not the appropriate person, necessarily, to help her heal. You are/were too close to it yourself -- so you're someone to be updated, if that's what she wants, and to make sympathetic sounds in response to what she tells you. But you are not the right person to bring into her reckoning process. (Check out Ring Theory. As part of the nuclear family, I see you as defensibly innermost to this issue and therefore not a dumping-in place.)

These do simplify your role considerably. You can clarify your role to your sister, too, and put boundaries around it without coming off as an uncaring or disbelieving jerk of a sibling. You can say something to the effect of being glad she trusted you with this, and wanting her to have the support she needs. Then you can say that you feel too close to it as her sib and your father's child to be the place she goes to work through it-- just too confusing and complicated. And you can assure her you aren't shutting down the topic, you just want her to know what you can and can't do right now.

Should I ask or not?
Guest Jun 27, 12:10 p.m.
QUESTION: My partner (P) asked me to fetch them their passport from their travel wallet in our safe. It was not in their wallet where it usually is so I had to look around to try find it. Just before I called P to say I could not find it, I came across 8 pictures in the wallet, 6 were special pictures of P and their close family and best friend. 2 were of P and a particular person of the opposite sex (A), we are heterosexual. When I saw those pictures my body and mind reacted, as if I was not in control of them. I tried to override all the negative thoughts that raced through my mind. Telling myself it means nothing, my mind is just overreacting on the negative side. But this was to no avail, if not making things worse. About 9 years ago before we got married and while we were in early innings of dating, long distance, P told me that someone had asked them if their partner (me) was not worried about P spending so much time with A and sleeping over at A‚Äôs house sometimes. At the time I said, no, I was not concerned as I considered A to be a close friend who had previously dated P‚Äôs then best friend (B). And that was that. But the pictures of P and A triggered that thought from the archives. Plus I guess how P talks about A more than any of their other friends and has become close to A more than with B and other close friends. All this probably contributed to my reaction, but was never an issue (at least conscious one) until the pictures. I do not think anything physical has ever happened between P and A, they have not seen each other except maybe once or twice in last 3 years or so. Although 6 years ago P went to visit A for a week or so. A was married then and living with their family and kids. So if anything, I guess it would be emotional affair at the worst or just close friends at best I guess. However it‚Äôs gnawing at me. Should I ask P about the pictures or should I just keep trying to ignore my mind and it‚Äôs normal for one to keep pictures of a close friend of opposite sex in a wallet in the safe among other special family pictures? If you recommend I ask, how do you recommend I ask? I have never had to have such a conversation in my life before and not sure how to approach it or if I should even approach it.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: These are great questions. I suggest turning them over in your mind before you say anything, because they can really help clarify what you'd be asking and why.

I believe, for example, that you already know his having pix of A isn't "normal," or that his connection to A is ... different. That's all I will commit to, because there can be so many different iterations of this. They could be having an active affair; she could be the one who got away, with (a) some embers still or (b) nothing left to it now; he could have a thing for her that she never ever reciprocated and he never got over -- but he just had to get on with his life. Which could be something he's very glad he did! His state of mind now and when he put the pix in there could be different, who knows. And so on.

Anyway. This is not to torture you with new possibilities, but to help you map out your own mind, heart, values. You may have someone from your past who fits this "I had to give up and move on" description, only you didn't stay in touch or save pictures -- in which case incorporating it into your happy reality isn't beyond comprehension. Or you may suddenly see this is the end of your marriage. There's so much potential range.

As you sort out possibilities and what you might or might not be able to accept, also work on your knowledge -- including what you already know but maybe haven't let yourself believe/dwell on. This is usually a lot. Then think about what more you need to know.

There's also much potential range in this category -- what we choose/want/need to know. It has always fascinated me, really, how different people are about this. Some people Must Know and some mindfully choose not to, and I long ago stopped having an opinion on the rightness of any one position. Too personal.

When you know you, then I think you'll have a lot better idea whether you want to ask, and if so, what you'll ask, how, when and why. But the opening is, "I found these." Then space for his response. Few words then much silence make a powerful combo for getting an answer.

re: should I ask
Guest Jun 27, 12:28 p.m.
QUESTION: It is my belief that having a relationship where you fear asking and being vulnerable to a problematic answer so you try to pretend not to know what you know is self-defeating. Ask. listen. Feel. Reply. Ask more. Listen more.

Risk openness.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: yes, yes, agree. Two qualifiers (not really the right word). One is that the pre-thinking process can really help with the state of mind we bring to the conversation. Coming in hot and fearful can have a truth-suppressant effect, even when we're talking to someone honest or willing to be honest. When possible, getting into a calm and open frame of mind -- even scared -- can really help.

Two, I think the "I choose not to ask" option is for when you already know except for the actual knowing, and you've decided the answer does not change anything. I don't recommend it for "the truth is too scary." "I can't handle the truth!!"

Value of non-productive time
Guest Jun 27, 12:53 p.m.
QUESTION: You said something in today's column - "You have value for existing ‚Äî not solely for what you contribute."
Sure, in theory. I think where I get hung up is that I believe everyone should contribute what they can, and receive what they need. (Yeah, I'm a dirty pinko.)
Since I am a person who can contribute, I must. I have no limiting factors to my productivity other than the time I need to spend eating, sleeping, and like, brushing my teeth. I know many people are not as fortunate, and so the inherent value of *their* lives, even though they are not able to be as productive, means that I and people like me must work a little more to keep the whole boat afloat. Does that make sense?
Burnout is always around the corner for me. Can you help me reframe it and really, truly convince myself that it's ok to NOT?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I'm not sure you need a reframe as much as a closer look at your own picture:

"I know many people are not as fortunate, and so the inherent value of *their* lives ... means that I and people like me must work a little more to keep the whole boat afloat."

You don't define "more." So, more than what? What if only 15 hours a week, say, covered the share any given person needed to contribute, and you satisfied the "a little more" requirement by adding only an hour to that?

By that math, all your burnout hours would be for some vague idea that you had to even though there's no actual need for it. In fact, maybe deciding not to work those extra hours would free up a work opportunity for someone who needs the work.

You're hung up on your own construct. I suggest a construct where you get evenings, weekends and at least four weeks a year off.

avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I can't explain the college career office, odd, and this is definitely not my lane, but this has become (gradually then suddenly) such an issue for recent grads that I want to get something out on the record to acknowledge it.

I also think your son is right not to panic into "graduate classes" without a clear plan. This is the time for him to think of a career path that has openings and is even growing, as others are drying up. If he needs to train for it, then so be it, but specific training/apprenticeship, on the consensus recommendation of people in the field. Very targeted education.

As for career counseling, that's a yes -- first step -- and he can check here at the National Career Development Association to start looking for someone.
Career Help
