Secondhand Dog Embarrassment
Guest Jun 27, 1:36 p.m.
QUESTION: Dear Carolyn,

I take daily 6 am walks with my neighbor, "Sarah." She is not a close friend, but we are in similar stages of life with the same constraints on our schedules. (If the walk doesn't happen before the kids wake up, then it doesn't happen at all.) Sarah almost always brings along her dog, "Sparky." Sparky is very big and very curious. He has a tendency to run right up to strangers and explore them. I am not a dog expert and can't say whether Sparky is appropriately trained, but I know that this makes a lot of people very uncomfortable, and some even get quite upset (especially those out walking with their own dogs or small kids).

Sarah does not leash Sparky on these walks (as far as I know it is not required). I have been with her for multiple encounters that turn hostile because other people don't appreciate Sparky's friendliness. To strangers, Sarah and I are both responsible. One woman yelled at us about not leashing the dog. Sarah yelled back, and I didn't know what to do other than to stay quiet. I think Sarah was offended that I did not take her side.

What is my role here? I am not a dog person and honestly I agree with the people who don't care for being approached by a big dog that way. Sparky is used to me and doesn't invade my space anymore, but I would find the walks very unpleasant if he did. Since we're in the space of being close acquaintances but not necessarily deep friends, I am unsure of how to handle this. My husband is worried Sparky will attack someone and that I will get sued as a co-defendant!
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: This question is like one of those I Spy photo-riddles where you get to pick out all the hidden objects. Except in this case it's Sarah's horrible antisocial behaviors tucked into friendly neighbors' little morning dog walk.

1. Sparky is *not* appropriately trained, ugh.

2. Not leashing a dog on walks, especially a large spelunker and kid-rusher that upsets people to the point of anger on a regular basis, is so incredibly selfish. And irresponsible. Hello. I would be surprised if there were no leash laws -- I suggest you look it up if you feel like completing the I Spy -- but at this point no one with a clue would need a law to tell them the right thing to do.

3. Okay, you just "think" Sarah was offended you didn't back her, you don't know it. So I'll go light on that sign of entitled behavior.

4. But, wait -- no apology? She and her dog force you to witness a yelling match on your morning constitutional, and she doesn't think to say, "Yikes, sorry about that"?

Your role here is to figure out what your boundaries (limits) are, say them and hold them. So if you don't want to walk with Sarah anymore, then don't. If you don't want to deal with the entitlement-off-its-leash show, then don't. And if you want to be the person who signs up to writhe in discomfort through antisocial behavior while doing nothing about it, then keep doing what you're doing every day at 6.

How you phrase the second option is a challenge because the truth is uncomfortable. But, even if it's Sarah's dog, these walks still involve your time and your comfort, which matter. So: "I want to keep up our daily walks, I enjoy your company. But all the negative reactions to Sparky off-leash are stressing me out. Are you willing to leash him for our walks?" If she refuses, then you say you're sorry but you have to opt out. Not good for your nerves. If she gets defensive and turns on you, then -- well, I hope she doesn't, but it seems like it would be on brand.
Post has 0 replies and 567 Likes
567Press Enter to expand

Re: Second hand dog embarassment
Guest Jun 27, 2:21 p.m.
QUESTION: I'll bet Sarah doesn't want to leash Sparky because she cannot handle him on a leash. I had a cocker spaniel that could pull my arm out of the socket when he decided to go after something while leashed. I knew a couple of women who were badly hurt when the big dogs they were handling bolted afteer something.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Um. That means no public mingling with the dog, ever. Dogs we can't control stay home and apart from the public. Full stop.

And a dog that's too big to manage on leash like that goes back to dog school or private work with a trainer. Or gets a pull-deterrent setup like a head halter -- plus the proper training, for dog and handler.

This is dog-owner malpractice. They are pets and awesome and wuzzy and our "fur babies" and all, but they are animals and can do harm if not responsibly handled. People who do not know what they're doing have a duty to learn it asap. It's for the dogs' sake as much as anyone else's.

Monster
Guest Jun 27, 2:59 p.m.
QUESTION: My husband and I tried for 15+yrs to have a child and it never happened. We missed out on a lot of life trying to conceive and grief. I felt like I was just finding a new normal when my husband received a message from a 26yo young woman, Ellen, who was his daughter from a one-night stand when he was early in college. My husband had no idea. Ellen had been put up for adoption. Ellen is a great person. Her adoptive family kind and welcoming. My husband has developed a good relationship with Ellen. I've kept my distance. I'm happy for them but feel intense jealousy that my husband has a daughter and I do not. Ellen is now pregnant and asking if we want to be bonus grandparents. My husband is enthusiastic and I am not. Ellen says she is excited for her child to have so much love, so much family and I see that but I don't feel that. I know my jealousy and my anger is not healthy or normal. I fear I'm pushing my husband a way. I know I need therapy but I'm afraid the therapist will judge me. avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I went into the queue just to post some reactions, but I want to respond to this quickly -- just go to therapy. Please.

First, most seasoned counselors have seen so much. It's hard to surprise them.

Second, they are in this to help you, not judge you.

Third, what anyone thinks of you is irrelevant, especially your therapist. I hope you see this -- right now your focus needs to be you and getting some peace. Stop with the self-name-calling and go.

To be human is to have some dark thoughts.

living on a golf course
Guest Jun 13, 11:05 a.m.
QUESTION: We recently purchased our last (we hope) home in a lovely, large gated community with an 18-hole golf course. We do not play. One of the fairways is directly behind us, with a handful of trees forming a barrier of sorts. There was some golf ball damage noted on the back of the house upon inspection, but it was minor and we proceeded with the purchase because the house checks every other box for us. Surprised and unhappy to learn, however, that any damage caused by a golf ball is NOT the responsibility of the golf course, nor of the player. It is strictly on the homeowner per the HOA! So when I should be enjoying our screened-in porch, sipping iced tea and watching the birds, instead I find myself shooting death stares at anyone whose golf cart makes a beeline toward our back yard to retrieve their balls, and some even rooting around in our yard. This is messing with my serenity and my sanity. When (not if) the damage happens, given that I have no legal right to pursue these folks, how do you suggest I approach them?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: As a homeowner who didn't do sufficient homework, and has no right to death-stare golfers as if that's their fault. I'm sorry.

When I read your subject line, my brain filled in the popcorn sound of golf balls hitting your house. It also brought back memories of a lot of years of organized baseball with my kids, with everyone's foul balls hitting the decks, siding, cars of neighbors whose properties abutted the schools and parks. At least the cars with Massachusetts policies, IIRC, got their windshields replaced with $0 deductible :)

I suggest you have a sit-down with your reality, serenity, sanity, available funds and on-call contractor list, and decide which you prefer: to accept the inevitable, or move one more time to your really-this-is-the-last-one last house.

Because sitting out there in your yard thinking you should be having a golfball-free experience next to a golf course is a recipe for daily fury and stress.

Social Media Etiquette
Guest Jun 13, 11:37 a.m.
QUESTION: Are there rules to this? I belong to an online forum of a few hundred users, and have ignored/blocked some others and there's a big kerfuffle going on about how blocking is rude, but is it? If a lot of people are blocking you such that you feel ignored, isn't that a you problem not a them problem? The biggest griper just writes rambling tl;dr screeds too often, I don't even disagree or dislike her it's just too much. Anyway, tl;dr is blocking rude?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I think there's a Golden Rule, don't bring behaviors to a forum that make everyone instantly tired.

If there are rulebook rules. then the ones being broken here are, in order of importance, no rambling screeds; no public griping about other people's screed appreciation habits; no conflating of "block" and "ignore."

"Ignore" is for people you generally like but are wearing you out. "Block" is the dead-to-me button to enough people that it's best reserved for that.

I agree with you on the principle, though -- that no kerfuffle was ever necessary and the fact of one makes the case that it's entirely on the person griping. If I take this forum correctly, then your posts are all basically the publishing arms of your selves, so if one of you wants to pause your subscriptions to some members for whatever reason, then so be it. Nothing personal.

Wait -- did I kerfuffle by answering this? Did you by asking?
Post has undefined replies and 159 Likes
159Press Enter to expand
opting out of college
Guest Jun 13, 11:45 a.m.
QUESTION: My just-graduated son and my one-year behind daughter have said they are not attending traditional college. My son was never academically inclined, so choosing trade school (plumbing) is disappointing but not a shock, But my daughter is a star student, They both believe that AI will soon eliminate entry level college graduate jobs unless physical presence is part of the job. My daughter does not want to teach or gp into anything medical, so her plan is to enter a car mechanic program where she can make use of her computer and technical ability. I'm not sure what I should do - I'm hoping to convince her to apply to college in the fall to keep her options open, But I'm also not sure that she's wrong. And advice?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Whoa wait a minute. Why is your "never academically inclined" son's decison to choose trade school for plumbing "disappointing"?

I know it's not your question, but I tripped over it so hard that I think it's germane to your question. To me your son's decision looks like an unqualified win. Practical, smart, suits him -- right? So if you have a bias that prevents you from seeing that, then you'll want to take that into account and dismantle it before you make any decisions about your approach to your daughter. Certainly before you say anything to her.

And that's even before you deal with the obvious flaw in speaking up now: that you admit openly that you don't even know enough to make a reasoned counterargument.

Your kids are right that many sectors of the job landscape look like an animated Dali painting. (Then there's the economy, depending on its given day in court, holy cats.) But then, is she right about cars? You will have to do some basic temperature-taking before you presume to "convince" anyone of anything.

More important, though, I suggest you draw your daughter out on the idea of: her interests, her idea of what she wants her life to look like (i.e., what is work for, on the "enough money to live" vs "purpose" scale), where she might want to be, whether she sees herself as doing one thing over a lifetime in various stages or multiple different things across a lifetime, maybe even completely different things.

If you can get your single KOLLEGE idea out of the way of your ear passages, this could be a fascinating series of talks. (Listens, really.)

Great for your son too IF he's not already set with his choice and IF broaching the topic wouldn't come across as disappointment in his choice. If there's any risk of that, then leave it be. It really does sound like something you want to back 100 percent with 100 percent sincerity.

IMPORTANT re: why are people so secretive about cancer
Guest Jun 13, 12:39 p.m.
QUESTION: Can you continue this important discussion from the 5/16 chat about why people may not go public with the ir serious illness? As a cancer survivor, I‚Äôd like to add to the response to the person who was having a hard time understanding why people are still so ‚Äúsecretive‚Äù about cancer. The people, who made me feel loved and supported during my cancer treatment, understood that I was protecting myself by not making my diagnosis common knowledge. It isn‚Äôt secrecy and hiding; it‚Äôs privacy and boundaries. When you have cancer, sometimes it seems like you are being asked to provide support to other people who make your cancer diagnosis all about themselves and what they want and how they think things ‚Äúshould‚Äù be handled. People can treat you differently once they know. Some people have lost their job when word gets out. Also, google ‚Äúcancer ghosting‚Äù. It shouldn‚Äôt happen, but sadly it happens to many people and even family members may ghost a cancer patient. Poor reactions and comments are much harder to take when you already feel so incredibly ill from treatment, and you have no emotional reserve for dealing with extra things like you do when healthy. You may not be able to see from the outside all the consequences for someone else in not granting privacy that they‚Äôve specifically requested around their diagnosis. When I got cancer, I followed the lead of a dear friend who decided not to tell many about her cancer recurrence and terminal illness because of the upsetting things people did the first time around. Even though I shared only with my inner trusted circle, I still had some hurtful reactions after confiding, like being called ‚Äúsecretive‚Äù to my face by a friend. She made me feel judged, disrespected, and unsupported during a terrible time in my life. More power to people who want to let everyone know. That's great if it‚Äôs what the person with cancer chooses. But, love for you can be present even when someone didn‚Äôt confide. Don‚Äôt wait for illness to tell people that you care for and love them. I‚Äôm not advocating AT ALL for withholding a diagnosis from one‚Äôs inner-most circle and yes, those in the inner circle may need a trusted outlet too. But, it‚Äôs sad when people are not respectful about others‚Äô privacy and their carefully thought out life choices. I am almost 2 years cancer free and hope to help cultivate more understanding toward
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Thank you for bringing this back up. Going through the outtakes was really frustrating that day, because the queue was filled with "privacy isn't secrecy" assertions -- which of course is true. People put so much work and feeling into their accounts of maintaining their privacy during an awful time in their lives -- but I was the one asking, and I was asking about secrecy. To use your words, "withholding a diagnosis from one‚Äôs innermost circle." As you may recall, this thread started because the spouse of the OP insisted on withholding his cancer news from everyone but OP. Which is more than privacy.

So I got to see it from the OP‚Äôs side and have heard from others in that position, who were sworn to secrecy. I have heard from many who did not know of a loved one‚Äôs cancer until they got news of their death. But it‚Äôs rare to hear from the people insisting on this choice. That's why I asked.

The scant response I have gotten is that people wanted to be treated as themselves, not as Sick. I do sympathize. (I would still urge allowing some support outlet for the lone person who knows, assuming there is one. There typically is, given the necessities of care.) I would still like to hear a response to my question.

So, to reiterate, I understand the decision to maintain privacy. People's repetitive, rude, nosy, me-centric, unsolicited Ring Theory violations are known to anyone here regularly, since, "How do I stop people from pushing their judgments/feelings/treatment advice on me," is a chat staple.

Also, no question, this is the ailing person's decision. Even if it's not one I'd want my loved one to make. I am not looking to argue otherwise.

I am sorry you got a big dose of people's stupid when you were in treatment -- but yay to the almost two years.

Thank you again for the invitation to come back to this. It also allows me to mention The Post‚Äôs recent reporting on the spike in cancer in young people . Tl;dr: Get checked when you feel weird, and don‚Äôt take ‚Äúyou‚Äôre too young‚Äù for an answer.

My partner and I still, for now, knock on wood, have jobs that others have lost, but especially with our financial situation being as precarious as it is, I can‚Äôt risk those by protesting.

Things just keep getting more and more terrible. I am trying to keep my head above water, and although I don‚Äôt feel like the vicious and cruel will necessarily win at the end, the cost of preventing them from doing so is already high, and will only climb higher. I don‚Äôt feel hopeless, but I do feel helpless.

What can I do? I engage in hobbies to the extent that our budget will allow me. We are all in therapy, although that is another blow to the budget (and to all the therapists out there who can and are willing to work on a sliding scale ‚Äî bless you!). avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I am going to disagree with you, but sympathize 100 percent first. Yes it is hard to get through the contempt for the Constitution and all that entails.

Where i disagree is with the "can‚Äôt do anything." Just because there has been no response to what you have done so far (written and called representative and senators and attended town halls) doesn't mean it was or is futile and it doesn't mean you stop. It just means it's not a one-bucket fire.

Or, different metaphor, one I think I borrowed earlier -- Notre Dame? The people who labored their whole lives on that cathedral did so knowing they would never see it to completion. It took almost two centuries to build. So, you do your part, of whatever size, because it matters to the whole -- that is the point. Any results you are able to see are a bonus.

Take care. I am also going to post an amazing thing by Stephen Colbert (Anderson Cooper interview) to address your "permanently misshapen" observation. If I can find it quickly enough. I'm already taking forever.

College vs trade
Guest Jun 13, 1:20 p.m.
QUESTION: I realize I have a stake in this discussion as a uni professor but can I represent a different view? It saddens me for college to get treated as working papers. In its ideal form, it's a chance -- in total distinction to the vast majority of high schools-- to develop real critical thinking skills, to learn how to learn, to follow curiosity and to grow as thinking people. Everyone should have that chance. And, sorry, but an educated citizenry is something we all have an interest in sustaining. That's why financial aid and the GI Bill and state schools and all that are important. Learning for its own sake isn't elites, I don't accept that.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Yes, agreed. But stop with the "sorry"!

I do hate how this has all gotten muddled. When I was in wee school, the trades were falling out of favor -- "voke" -- for "Everyone should have that chance." It should never have been either-or, of course, that was the mistake. Stop applying "favor." But the idea was right to broaden access. Then people noticed the emphasis on college was skewed too heavily on college, and there were a bunch of students there just because they were expected to be, and lo not enough people were entering the trades. Add to that, US has its "Greed is good" 1980s and never looks back, and suddenly it's all comp sci, business degrees and ROI. Ugh.

I get it, it's a lot of time and money and people want to know what they're getting, but it does seem like two basics keep getting swamped: 1. In grade school, give kids options based on their strengths and interests, not their socioeconomic status or educational fashion; 2. In college, think broadening of experience and critical thinking skills first, not career.

Fair?

Commenting on privacy vs secrecy
Guest Jun 13, 1:34 p.m.
QUESTION: I had cancer twice in less than 5 years, forms that were curable by surgery but that could still come back. Now when I hear of people that have a cancer scare I always suggest maintaining privacy (bordering on secrecy). The first time I had cancer, I was open about it. People (eg, mother and several sisters) asked me how I was doing. A LOT. And long after things were resolved. Talking about it was painful, reminding of the bad times and the outcome, that I could not bear a child due to the required hysterectomy. Reminders come up often (eg, at doctor‚Äôs visits, or even sometimes at the pharmacy) anyway. Minimizing information sharing (and thus questions/reminders) is better. The second time I had cancer I was private. Even secret. It was better.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: This makes complete sense to me, thank you.

Cancer secrecy
Guest Jun 13, 2:03 p.m.
QUESTION: Whew is this a raw topic. I just had a very close loved one pass - I was her health care POA- and didn't know they had cancer till I got a call saying life support had been pulled, asking if I wanted to say goodbye.

The amount of people who showed right after just devastated and feeling betrayed was earth shattering. They had told any who knew - a boss, neighbors- a few different stories. They were so loved, and people were hurt. I could only do a few disclosures a day. Would let them have a moment and try to gently explain that I hadn't known myself.

So while I completely do respect that it is the best wishes of the person with the ailment and their autonomy... it is truly hard for those loved ones. Would try to aim for a middle ground. Ring theory, privacy, boundaries.

Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Thanks for this perspective, and I am so sorry.

I hope the takeaway for all is to be extremely respectful of someone who lets you know of their illness. When you find out -- from them directly or from a third party -- ask, "How do you/does X want me to handle this?"

Plan A, Plan B
Guest Jun 13, 2:28 p.m.
QUESTION: Dear Carolyn,

My husband is 39 and I'm 36, and we have been married for two years. We are both in total agreement that we want children, but we have been in active disagreement about the timing since our wedding day. My husband has an older sister who went through a divorce in her 30s, remarried an amazing guy at 40, and naturally had two children in her early forties. Breezy pregnancies and everything. This is my husband's gold standard for what's possible, and he has decided that all the fertility/age fearmongering that goes on is just conservative propaganda. I do think there is plenty of propaganda around this topic, but I am 100% sure that trying to get pregnant in one's forties is NOT anyone's Plan A, though it might be a solid Plan B. So how do I get us on the same page about this‚Äîdo I drag him into my gyno appointments? (Not ideal!)
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: So that's it, that's what he's going with -- it's possible because his sister did it? And anything else is "conservative propaganda."

Dude needs to read. Or, ah, listen to his wife?

I don't know what to tell you, but you've described a degree of resistance to information that is not promising.

If he is willing to consider a source that is neither his sister nor conservative propaganda, and doesn't involve his attendance at your GYN appointments, then I'll be happy to tell him that fertility windows vary from woman to woman -- which means some women struggle to get pregnant after age 30 or 35, some have babies over 50, and some can't conceive at any age. So if he wants you to conceive his child without medical intervention, then now's the actual best time to start trying.

And if he simply does not want you to get pregnant right now, then that is something he needs to own, knowing "not now" might well mean "not ever" -- and not deflect by saying "eh we shall do this no-worries-like when you're down to your last egg."

Also? Pregnancy punishes a body, more so as a woman ages, though not equally each prenancy and each woman. Some get through with relatively little effect and some get walloped and it's not entirely up to them. But, if you are reading, my good dude -- put an 8-ish pound roast in there, then rip it out, then lmk.

(Sorry again, everyone.)

So now she wants to be friends?
Guest Jun 6, 11:05 a.m.
QUESTION: Years and years ago, my friend Evie called and said that her long-term boyfriend had just broken up with her and moved out. She was distraught. I told her I'd put the kids to bed and be right over. I didn't make it over there for 36hrs because my husband had a freak accident that ended up in the ER and the next 24hrs were touch and go - there was a chance I'd be raising my kids solo. Half-way through the ordeal, I called and left a message explaining what had happened. A day and a half later, once I was home and kids settled, I called her and she said she saw no reason for us to be friends. I had failed her. We had been close friends for over a decade. I was sad to lose the friendship but I had just been through hell - I didn't intentionally fail her! We are now in our early 60's. Her daughter found me on social media and sent me a message that her mom's health isn't great and she'd like if it I stopped by. The daughter says that her mom wants to see me but won't reach out herself because she is too ashamed. I don't know what to do with this information. If Evie really wanted to see me, wouldn't she reach out directly? And if Evie is so ashamed that she can't reach out herself, then how is it going to go sitting face to face? I am a kind person. If seeing me will help Evie in some way, I'll go but I don't want to be in a position where she expects me to apologize. Am I thinking about this all wrong?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: For someone to have been as wrong and stupid as Evie was years ago doesn't come out of nowhere. You knew the context from the decade of close friendship, so I'm guessing you weren't completely blindsided when she did that to you?

So, it makes total sense to me at least that that Evie would be ashamed for years over her own behavior, and would not be able to reach out to you herself, even on her deathbed (if that in fact is her health situation). That seems to be the level of stuntedness Evie demonstrated all those years ago.

What you do with this info is up to you. I'm just saying, Evie was showily dysfunctional then and apparently isn't a whole lot more functional now. If you want to see her, then see her, but I'm not sure it's productive to think of Evie as having ulterior motives. You're in the position of strength, if you want to look at it that way -- if for no other reason than your ability to deal with people directly as a matter of course.

1104Press Enter to expand
Am I entitled?
Guest Jun 6, 11:25 a.m.
QUESTION: I'm pretty sure my mom would have chosen to be child-free, if that concept existed in the 1970s when she had me. Instead, as a school teacher, she faced a lot of social and professional pressure to be a mom herself. Now that I am the ages she was when she raised me, I understand my childhood a lot better. She hated every part of raising me. She gave up a lot of personal freedom, financial security, and much-needed introvert time (another concept that didn't exist back then) to raise me, and as someone who became child-free myself (no surprise why), I actually get it and feel a lot of sympathy for her.

I already temper so many of my emotional expectations of her because of her circumstances. She doesn't tell me she loves me, she doesn't express any sort of parental pride, she doesn't talk nostalgically about our departed family members or family memories, she doesn't have any photos on display at home, etc.

My husband and I buy her birthday and Christmas presents, pay for brunch on Mother's Day, invite her over for weekly dinners and a movie, help her with her taxes and repairs around her house, but she doesn't reciprocate any of this. She doesn't even call me on my birthday, though I usually call her that week and she works a Happy Birthday in then.

But there was one thing she was doing for us that mattered a lot to us: she would watch our pets while we went out of town on vacation or to visit my husband's family, about four times per year. Now she is refusing to help anymore, because she says it's too disruptive to her life. It's not a matter of age - she's in extraordinary health. I think at its core she thinks our trips out of town are frivolous, and our pets don't bring her any personal pleasure so she can't see the point in doing it anymore. I've offered countless compromises to see if she'd keep helping us, all of which directly addressed her descriptions of the inconvenience it was causing, but she just wants to stop outright.

We can totally afford to board our animals. That's definitely what we'll do now. But this was the ONE THING she was doing that seemed....generous? Caring? Above and beyond what acquaintances do for each other? I have to be honest - I am devastated by this. I already let her off the hook for so many things. I want to pull away, stop the presents and dinners, and just give up on calling this a mother-daughter relationship entirely. Am I wrong to make this the final straw?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: If you're going to frame it that way, then, yes. Because, as written, you've created the impression that this isn't so much a dialogue with your mother as a monologue where you're performing your part and your mom's. And so you're kind of getting mad at yourself and leaving.

But please let me answer a bigger question? I think giving up on "calling this a mother-daughter relationship entirely" is a great idea for both of you, dead on, so don't limit its tone and emotional possibilities with a connection to the petsitting.

I hope you see what I mean -- I feel like I'm not explaining it well.

If you say, "You stopped petsitting for us, so I'm punitively withdrawing the last few gestures that acknowledge you as my mother," then it's a tit-for-tat door-slam. I think the emotional complexity of this situation and the incredible generosity you have shown your mother deserve better. I think you and your mom both deserve better than pinning this to an artificially adopted symbol.

Free yourself of the societal-role expectations just because. Because they're merely smaller versions of what entrapped your mom in the 1970s, because they're no less arbitrary, because they're not working for either of you. (Well, maybe your mom likes the tax help and movie night, I shouldn't assume. But her big stand against petsitting looks like her way of saying she wants out of these rituals, too.)

The way I'm thinking about it, all the stuff you do for your mom gets another look, this time from forest perspective vs. tree. Do you buy her holiday gifts and do the Mother's Day dance, etc., because she wants and expects it, or have you been doing what you think you're supposed to? Or doing what you think you need, to tend to the injury of your childhood circumstances?

You make no mention of therapy, so I'll do it. You're dealing with a lot here. You've let more of it go, forgiven more, than I'm guessing most people would have been able to in your position, so I hope you don't take any of this as criticism. Anything I suggest is to help you along on the process you started.

I liken "temper so many of my emotional expectations" to anger. It's laudable progress for people to be able to feel their anger come on and then choose not to act on it. But it's a whole other step to heal the source of the anger so they simply get angry less often. Your next step is past tempering, and releasing yourself and your mom of mom-expectations entirely.

Then? You decide.

The kids always come first, right?
Guest Jun 6, 11:53 a.m.
QUESTION: We‚Äôve been together 5 years and I moved in last summer. He has 2 kids from a previous marriage and we have them 3 weekends a month. On a typical weekend he makes plans for the 3 of them that don‚Äôt include me but we will usually have a meal or watch a movie together at some point. I do my own thing or make plans with friends. I have invited them to join me many times but the kids usually aren‚Äôt interested in what I‚Äôm doing even when it‚Äôs kid friendly (e.g. theme parks) or my boyfriend doesn‚Äôt want to change their plans.
Once or twice a year I ask him to switch weekends with his ex so he can attend certain events with me. Otherwise, I do most things without him because he‚Äôs with the kids. Recently, I asked him in Feb if he‚Äôd switch for an event in May and he agreed, happily even. I reminded him every few weeks to talk to his ex about the switch. When the time came he‚Äôd never talked to her and said he‚Äôd go with me ‚Äúif‚Äù he didn‚Äôt have the kids. He made the arrangements only because I got upset (not the first time something like this has happened) despite my telling him not to at that point. As we drove by his ex‚Äôs neighborhood on the way to the event he said, ‚ÄúThat's where my kids are.‚Äù Clearly he just wanted to be with them.
He genuinely seems to want to include me in the bigger things but once there, I‚Äôm all but ignored. On a yearly trip with his family I decided to include myself by joining him and the kids in the pool. I tried joining in and asked if they wanted to play any pool games, etc. and was flat out ignored. When we got home I told him how hurt I was by that and he had no idea I‚Äôd even gotten in the pool. His brother and SIL paid more attention to me on that trip than he did. We also go on a yearly trip with my family and he decided not to bring the kids this year. He said, ‚ÄúI‚Äôll spend time with you this year since my kids won‚Äôt be there.‚Äù
We‚Äôve been doing date night once a week and we have our one weekend a month. I guess that‚Äôs enough for him but we could have continued that without me moving in. Maybe I kind of thought moving in would automatically make us a family but the 3 of them are still in their bubble. I know it must be hard not to see your kids every day and I‚Äôm sympathetic to that. But I‚Äôm not asking him to spend less time with them, I just want to be included more. Is this just how it is when one partner has kids? Am I asking too much?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: "Is this just how it is when one partner has kids?" There's no universal "how it is." This is how it is in *your* relationship. That is what matters, and whether you're okay with it.

You're clearly not, so it's time to accept that and decide your next move.

The next move includes your stating to him clearly, "I just want to be included more," because you apparently "kind of thought" it would happen "automatically," which I do not recommend.

I almost didn't bother recommending this step because it seems like you're saying it many different ways, especially by inviting everyone to join you, but that's the thing -- not saying it explicitly leaves all kinds of room for misunderstanding. He may actually think you want to do your own thing, and that you were only hurt that one time because he ignored you, not because you were left out of the bubble. You need to be clear, clear, clear about what you want.

Because that's the only way to find out whether he's willing to do the work to include you. Of course the kids will do their own thing around you, if that's what they're used to. He is going to have to incorporate you now, gradually and thoughtfully, to let the kids get to know you and get comfortable.

That is, assuming he wants you involved. If he likes it this way because it's easier for him, then you have a decision to make whether that's acceptable to you.

Again -- you have to communicate what you want so you can get to this point where you can see whether getting it is or isn't possible. It can't be asking too much if you haven't expressly asked.

You can, of course, read the whole situation as-is and decide you've seen enough; you're being ignored to a degree I wouldn't encourage anyone to rationalize. But you also seem like two people who don't communicate, so this is a chance for you, at least, to work on that.

A former Evie
Guest Jun 6, 12:26 p.m.
QUESTION: I used to avoid people like the plague when I made a mistake - especially a big one. I grew up in a house of both emotional neglect and perfectionism. This meant, if I made any kind of mistake, especially a big one, I could not emotionally handle what I did, and I couldn't face hearing a high level of disdain for another person.

While I've done a lot of work on myself over the years and I am MUCH better since I have the tools to manage these situations, there are some situations that I'm not proud of - people have every right to have a negative view of me due to past actions.

As a former Evie, I'll say this: She was in the wrong 100%. She should have reached out to you earlier to apologize. Much earlier. There are so many reasons she may not have that don't have anything to do with you. I wish that during those times, I had the hard conversation with the other person, even if I was scared to have it. I could have salvaged a few of those connections.

With that being said, you also don't owe her anything. If you think that you will benefit from seeing her in some way, then go for it. If it all feels like a big disruption, you can send a note wishing her well, or respond to the daughter saying that you will not be able to see her. People don't have their epiphanies on their deathbed, they talk about them more since the opportunity for long-term consequences are done.

Setting boundaries
Guest Jun 6, 12:30 p.m.
QUESTION: Hi! I was diagnosed with breast cancer earlier this year and am still undergoing treatment. FYI if you have dense breast tissue that mammograms can‚Äôt see through ask your doctor if you qualify for extra screening methods! It might save you from having a much bigger problem (like me). Okay, so it‚Äôs been a roller coaster for months and I kept my circle small. Just my family and close friends but in the process I came to the realization that my MIL isn‚Äôt in ‚Äúmy corner‚Äù and never will be. After 26 years of relationship that has been at times really challenging and hurtful (went to therapy for it 15 years ago), I really feel so much better with essentially keeping her away from me. Cancer helped me set boundaries with her. My question, and I‚Äôm embarrassed I still don‚Äôt know how to do it, how can I keep a friendly but somewhat distanced space from her? I am a people pleaser so this is really hard. She keeps trying to connect - she misses my attention.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I am so sorry you are going through this, a really taxing experience without the emotional weight of a difficult relationship. There is absolutely no need to throw embarrassment on the pile. Managing a difficult relationship is not a one-size-fits-all thing we are all supposed to know how to do by x time or y age.

Anyway. Your spouse really needs to step in here and tell your MIL that you are taking more time to rest/for yourself, or you are communicating less, or however you two decide to phrase it, and so your spouse is to be her primary contact hereafter. Period. And your spouse eats the fallout for this. Period.

That is the path to the distanced space while you undergo treatment and heal.

If you and/or spouse are unwilling to take a step this drastic, then I am going to be a noodge and ask you to think about that for a bit. Because you deserve and need this space and support. So who is not giving it to you and why? Who is treating your needs as less important than hers?

If that is too big to take on right now, then, fair. I will back off. And suggest this:

Pick a form or topic of communication with her that is easy, and live there. Errands, recipes, your spouse, a TV show, a team, books, her garden/dog/job -- by text only, or one call a week, or one narrowly defined help request per week, etc. Set it and forget it.

I know this is emotional work just to figure out, which is why it is my second option. But once it's in place, it can be magic for containment.

Take care.

Grief
Guest Jun 6, 12:49 p.m.
QUESTION: Sibling and I just past the 2nd anniversary of our mom‚Äôs death, and are quickly approaching the two year mark on the incredibly difficult job of clearing out and selling her house. Sibling and I have had an up and down relationship over the past 30 or so years.

Thanks
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Best advice I have is to relieve yourself of the burden of having the right kind of grief. You can miss one version of Mom and not the other in the same thought. Have all the complicated thoughts and feelings and don't bother feeling guilty.

The classic saying -- if you don't like New England weather, wait a few minutes -- works pretty well for grief like you describe, especially when you're digging through box after box of memory triggers. Try just letting it all roll by. See if that helps.

The thing is, I‚Äôm from a southern-ish area and am used to a certain kind of considerate treatment from friends etc, and my husband is also quite considerate. However, she‚Äôs NYC born and isn‚Äôt mean, just rarely thinks to say things like please and thank you, and is the only person in my life that would ever just tell me what to do vs asking. I just feel disrespected, especially when she‚Äôs anxious and it comes out in talking to me in a way I would never tolerate from anyone else. But I don‚Äôt think it‚Äôs anything she is even aware of, and I‚Äôm rather conflict averse. Is the problem me, and I should learn to be less sensitive and more compassionate? Or is there any way to address the dynamic that won‚Äôt likely do more harm than good?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Thanks for the kind words.

I'm sorry, too, that you're the designated driver for exactly what torments you in a person. Very "No Exit," if you're up for a little Sartre.

But you have gone 99 percent of the way to solving your own problem, so I hope you will take me up on the easier suggestion to push the last 1 percent. You are not being "disrespected," any more than you would be if your MIL spoke English as a second language and used an insulting word inadvertently as a consequence.

The formula for disrespect includes intent. From your description, your MIL is not only deeply imprinted in her mannerisms, but also the same with you as with anyone, yes? -- though let's take away the geographic markers; they're not relevant. (They may dictate manner and accent, but to suggest region determines thoughtfulness vs. self-absorption is a nonstarter.)

So there's nothing to internalize or address. You're carrying a passenger who doesn't speak your language, and you don't understand hers. The fact that she goes deeper into "her" language when anxious or upset really tracks.

The thing for you to learn, then, is more like retraining, to see your differences as stemming from communication vs. intent. Nothing hurtful there.

Couples therapy
Guest Jun 6, 1:29 p.m.
QUESTION: My wife is has been seriously depressed for a couple months, more so than in the 25 years we've been together. In the past few couples therapy sessions, she declined to explain why when I (at the therapist's suggestion, though I had asked before too) invited her to do so. She then said part of the reason she couldn't is that I would then get upset, which would shift the focus from her to me. The therapist supported her position.

In other words, it is my fault that she won't explain why she is upset to me. (Narrator: He knows why she is depressed. It is because she doesn't like sex, he is hurting as a result, she knows she will never change but is unwilling to say so clearly, and we both realize there is no good solution. But his saying so would be speculating about her internal mental state which he's learned is a no-no in couples therapy; it is important only to talk about one's own feelings.)

Is this rational? Is this abuse? Is this therapist playing favorites?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: What do those questions have to do with anything.

Your wife doesn't like sex. Are you ready to accept this and move on to whatever is next for you -- as a couple or not as a couple anymore?

Or is it your intention to try to talk about this to some better outcome for you, when you seem to be saying you already know there isn't one?

If it's the former, then you say what upsets you is limbo and not knowing the truth, and you are prepared to accept your reality, even one you don't want, because then you at least know where you stand. Because it's not about blame it's about living honestly. (Isn't it?)

If it's the latter, then you don't belong in couples counseling, because your wife is not safe to speak freely -- because you may try to use her honesty to get something you want from her.

How do I get over myself?
Guest Jun 6, 2:03 p.m.
QUESTION: I got laid off and recently have been hired by former colleagues as a consultant, but I've never been a consultant before and I'm really struggling to get started. I helped develop the scope of my work, so I actually do know what is needed, but I seem to be in a paralysis and can't seem to get started. Huge amounts of imposter syndrome happening here. How can I get over this so I don't lose this opportunity?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Can you pick something? You developed the scope of your work, you say -- so what's the first thing? Type it and send it in. And if you want, also say why you aren't doing it, because there's a reason you aren't doing it.

If it's a good reason, then do the second thing.

Imposter syndrome is real, I know that, but it's also BS and here's why: It affects regular healthy people who know what they don't know, while all the "I alone can fix it!!" Dunning-Krugerite yahoos rush in and screw it all up because they don't know what they don't know.

So just do a thing. Once you start, you'll have it.

Re: How do I get over myself
Guest Jun 6, 2:13 p.m.
QUESTION: I worked on a consultant basis for decades. At first I always felt like you do: "What am I doing here? Why did they even hire me? I don't know what I'm doing! I'm going to screw this up and never be able to work again ... " Etc., etc. After a couple of experiences like this, I realized that I ALWAYS felt like this for the first week or two on a contract -- and it wasn't because I was ignorant, it was because I genuinely did not know at first what the problems were and what I was going to do to solve them. I was being a good listener and figuring out what the client actually needed, rather than coming in with a canned solution that might not fit their situation at all. So I learned to relax and be a sponge for the first couple of weeks, maybe (as CH suggests) produce a bit of work to show the client that I was listening ... and then kick into problem-solving mode once I felt surer what the problem was. Your reaction is perfectly normal, so don't worry about it.

1229Press Enter to expandPost comments expanded
Ex insists my gf is toxic because she wrote a book about cheaters
Guest May 16, 11:04 a.m.
QUESTION: My marriage ended 3 years ago because my wife cheated on me and left me for that guy. The whole experience was devastating but after wallowing for months, I spent time in therapy, worked on myself and carried on. My ex and I have reached a good place and co-parent our son well. We are not friends but we are friendly.
Now, I am in love with a beautiful, smart, funny woman who I‚Äôve been dating for 6 months. Because of her I am happier than I‚Äôve been since before my marriage ended. She was also cheated on and we bonded over that experience. She wrote and published a book about it. My ex Googled her name and read the book and told me she‚Äôs not happy about the relationship. The book is very brutal about cheaters. My ex says she‚Äôs worried about what my girlfriend will eventually say to our son about cheaters and about her. I promised I won‚Äôt let that happen but my ex says my girlfriend is bitter and toxic and I should steer clear. I read the book for myself and it‚Äôs very funny but she doesn‚Äôt pull any punches about her hatred and disdain for her ex and all cheaters. Her ex was mean and insulting all through the divorce so it's understandable. They don't have kids so she's free to hate him all she wants. Could that book be a sign of toxicity or is it a great method of coping?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Guess what. Your ex wife doesn't get to decide whom you date. She doesn't even get an opinion, not even as the amicable co-parent of your children.

If someone you are dating does or says something that either causes harm to your kids, or presents a credible risk of imminent harm or long-term damage (I think that covers it), then your ex does have standing to speak up. Arguably that's what she did, but you answered her concern so her proper next move was to drop it, not drop a "should" on you.

Cuz, sorry, ex's complaint that she's uncomfortable with your GF for authoring a book about cheaters because ex is a cheater herself does not pass the laugh test. Unless "laugh test" is literally that it makes a tired advice columnist laugh out loud at her keyboard, in which case it passes with flying colors so now I'm confusing myself.

Of course she's not happy. You can say this to her. "Of course you're not happy after reading the book. I wouldn't expect you to be."

But then you can say: "I am not dating her, though, as some kind of hired assassin to kill your standing with our kids."

Because you're not, right? You're dating her because she is her own person and you like her a lot and you like yourself with her.

So: "I am dating her because I like her. She and her ex are not the two of us. You and I are our own people and have our own history and have made our own peace."

Then: "If she can't respect that, then of course we won't stay together. But I decide that, not you."

End of conversation, no negotiating.

As for whether the book is toxicity or coping: It could be either. Or both. Her book could be a snapshot in time, maybe since superseded or outgrown, and possibly even part performance.

You have chosen the high road as your kids' co-parent, so if you and your GF are going to stay together, then first you're going to have to find out for yourself whether she is willing and able to support that choice 100 percent. No oopses.

Again -- YOU find this out. You don't preemptively walk away because your ex thinks she can exercise veto power. You do not want to grant her that.

Your ex could choose to blow up your detente over this, because that takes only one person to do. She can do more harm than any anti-cheating zealot you date. I hope she doesn't force the issue so you have to articulate things like this.

Fun fun, the need for trust never stops and keeps running both ways.

Unless there's a not-zero risk you'd hate it.

Living with a cheater‚Ä¶ strategy or insanity?
Guest May 16, 11:26 a.m.
QUESTION: Carolyn, I‚Äôve been serially cheated on by my wife. I have not directly confronted her. We have settled into routine and can enjoy a day without issue if I compartmentalize my knowledge of her deceit, which now comes more easily. My heart is not in it, my kids are my life, my work supports us and fulfills me, along with hobbies.

If I can take what pieces of enjoyment I can from my weak relationship, live my life finding love and fulfilment outside of my supposed partner, and let the kids have a ‚Äúnormal‚Äù upbringing in the midst of background secrecy, is that fantastic adaptation and coping in a tough situation, or am I just a masochistic time bomb waiting to have a breakdown?

In the thick of it I‚Äôm not sure if this is strategy or insanity.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Huh. I see your predicament -- this could be one honest conversation away from being a functional(ish) open marriage. Or, boom.

We know you're lying to your wife (about not knowing how epically she's lying to you). The question is whether you're lying to yourself about how okay you are emotionally with the arrangement.

Two suggestions -- well, one, to spend some good honest you time with that question.

But the first version of it is, on your own, to imagine a life where you tell your wife the truth and then live with configurations of that idea. Same "open" (loveless) marriage but honest; divorce; shared home but separate rooms and social lives; and earnest reckoning with the marriage and an attempt at least at repair and monogamy. At various points of your days, think where you'd be at that moment if you chose option x. Better, worse, too weird to know? Not that you'd be 100 percent in control of the outcome, you're just pushing your mind out of the "This is fine" meme you're living.

The second version is to put yourself in solo counseling to figure out what you want and feel and where your lines are between healthy tradeoffs and self-erasure.

I vote version 2 -- but getting therapy set up takes time, so try on version 1 while you wait for your appointment. (Unless your work has an EAP. Then you can usually talk to someone in a matter of days.)

At a loss for words
Guest May 16, 11:43 a.m.

Advice Columnist

Now can I throw things around the kitchen in frustration at people for not letting things go that aren't their business?

Ugh.

Here's all you ever need to say to people who are asking for more info than you are willing or able to give: "Ah, long story."

This one, you can cap off with, "But I'll be back as soon as I can."

Then you change the subject. Have a pivot topic ready.

Also? Unsolicited, sorry: Your husband is asking a lot of you. In my book, which is irrelevant, he's asking too much. The caregiver needs care too.

If you agree with that, then ask him for license to confide. Not everywhere, just your chosen place of unfailing discretion.

Boundary-setting with possible special circumstances
Guest May 16, 11:53 a.m.
QUESTION: I've learned a lot about setting appropriate boundaries in relationships over the past few years. I recently had a conversation with a close friend which led me to look at a specific situation differently though. I have sibling with whom I have a complicated relationship. He can be verbally explosive and insulting, including cursing, name-calling, etc. Sometimes this is followed by an apology, or an "I was just angry" explanation. I told him that this type of communication is unacceptable to me. My friend's point was along the lines of "what if this is early dementia or mental illness, and he truly can't control it?" If that's the case (we're both seniors), I realize that I still have a choice, but it makes the choice more complicated, doesn't it? I'm interested in your thoughts about this.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Whether someone can or can't control their "bad" behavior matters, sure -- I'd let my wailing infant smack me in the face without consequences, right? But my healthy 8-year-old, not so much.

The boundary for a sibling who is "verbally explosive and insulting" but who has dementia or mental illness would be that I don't engage with the insults at all, or leave the room or end the call or whatever -- whatever it takes to go non-responsive. I also wouldn't take it personally. Not about me.

Also, that behavior never gets "rewarded" in any sense. That can mean the threat of it never factors into my decisionmaking; I do as I intend for my reasons and if the reaction happens, then it happens. There's no tiptoeing around.

I'm answering generically here. You don't say whether your sib's behavior has deteriorated, to suggest onset of dementia, say. "I was just angry," absent context, doesn't fly. We all get angry. Not all of us take that as permission to lash out and inflict harm.

The amount of their control factors into the amount you limit contact with the person, as part of your boundary. That's how it works for me at least. And of course it's just our perception of how much control they have, because who knows. The closest we get are formal diagnoses.

Dealing with inquiries of ambiguous loss
Guest May 16, 12:08 p.m.
QUESTION: Hi Carolyn,

I'm so grateful for your column and am an avid reader. I'm sad to be writing you, but I hope you can help. A friend of mine has been missing for a month now. She is an adult, but it is under suspicious circumstances that she disappeared (she left behind a cat that she loved deeply and daily medications), the police are involved and every day I help with the amount of spoons I can muster. I do hang quite a few posters and talk to a lot of people at my job (I get a lot of people from many areas of Denver passing through) about my missing friend to try to create awareness and possibly recognition to generate leads for the tip line. My question is that sometimes the overwhelm of people asking follow up questions about things we (her collective of friends and warriors trying to find her) have covered 100xs gets to me and I need a nice way to say that I don't have anymore answers. I realize I'm instigating these conversations, and people want to be helpful, and I point them to the tip line and the Facebook page her mom has going to try to organize everyone. I'm also undergoing therapy, to help manage my own loss here. I really miss her. Thanks for your help.

Sincerely,
Bring Jax Home
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Oh oh oh. This is awful.

Your question is very much like the one people have when they are seriously ill and everyone they talk to says, "Have you tried ...?" It comes from this place of real caring, and then it's over and over and over, and you. just. can't.

I have a few suggestions. One is that you release as much of this worry as you can. You are lovely to worry about how people feel when they talk to you, but I can't imagine any of them don't see your distress plainly (unless they're just not capable). So you're good. really.

Another is basic and mechanical. If you can carry a stack of little printed business-size cards with the Facebook info and the tip line and some FAQs or whatever else, then, "Thank you -- this is all of it," is a natural transaction closer.

And, you can also say: "I am so grateful you're asking. I can't be the answers though. I hope you understand."

A huge hug through the ether to you.

Feeling weirdly guilty after my weight loss
Guest May 16, 12:33 p.m.
QUESTION: Carolyn, I'm glad you answered today's question to open a discussion about weight loss drugs and judgment. I have a different but related one. I've lost 70 pounds taking meds, I'm healthier (was pre-diabetic), but now I'm having an unexpected response. I really like seeing myself thin. I like shopping for clothes, something that's been torture for years. But in this era of more body positivity, I'm feeling strangely guilty and kind of ashamed that I had to make myself so much smaller to embrace my body. I have friends of all sizes, of course, and I love them for who they are. But sometimes I can hear that judgy voice in my own head (never out loud) saying I'm glad I look the way I do now. And I hate that. Is this just another way that diet culture has messed with me? What should I do with these contradictory feelings?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Yeah dramatic intentional weight loss definitely brings up some interesting new ways to feel bad -- when all "body positivity" was supposed to mean was not linking body size and shape and food to punishing measures of personal worth.

I wouldn't call your experience "unexpected" though. I mean, you were pre-diabetic -- your body wasn't in a happy place. Now it's happy, or happier. where it is, and it's okay to feel good about that.

So while I don't think it was ever misguided to focus on liking yourself and wearing flattering things at whatever weight you were -- and wow it is TOTALLY right to be accepting of others wherever they are, and being open to beauty in whatever form it appears -- we are allowed to have personal, internal signals. This feels good, this doesn't feel good, this feels great. It's our metering system. The best we can hope for at this point is to grab it back from social pressure -- or protect it from, in the case of kids.

At a loss for words
Guest May 16, 12:38 p.m.
QUESTION: Why are you even saying when you will go back? Oversharing = push back. When are you going back? Not sure, haven‚Äôt made plans yet. Rinse and repeat. This will be true until you buy plane tickets.

avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: So, yes -- you're right. I'll say that upfront. The less we say the less we have to explain.

But. For one thing, that's a tough lesson to apply in arrears. OP may have been chitchatting as usual, and suddenly there was Husband's Cancer Embargo to deal with, and oops, suddenly OP needs creative phrasing where none was ever needed before. It's like learning a new dance step and tripping over your own feet.

And, further, this dovetails with the need for caregiver to have care: The OP probably really wants to go back to see the daughter and grandbaby. And is worried about husband, too. OP is talking to friends! So, "Not sure, haven‚Äôt made plans yet," is not just info withholding, it's emotional withholding. That's hard.

So that's why the breezy deflection you suggest is 100 percent effective and right but ... ugh. Not. For a person like OP, newly in this spot. (Maybe eventually, with time.)

And why the pushing is so hard, too, from friends. Wish they had their sensors on.

Aaaaand why "keep my cancer a complete secret all summer" is just not a fair ask. Again. I say this already spotting anyone sick a huge sympathy advantage.

EAP followup
Guest May 16, 12:59 p.m.
QUESTION: I work at a large employer with a mental-health benefit/EAP. I've been struggling to feel productive at work, wondering if I'm burned out, or depressed, or just bored with my job, etc. I recently signed up with the EAP and did indeed get to talk with someone in less than a week, which was great. I found that the therapy she provided seemed very "results focused," with the desired results being that I was able to be productive at work. To be fair, that is the problem I brought to her that I wanted a solution for. However, I also mentioned that some of my poor mental health state had to do with some issues I am having with my wife. She did not follow up on that at all. We talked instead about attention issues, possible ADHD, etc., all very reasonable things, ... but all focused on my ability to work.

I had somewhat of a feeling that maybe the employer-provided mental health providers were mostly focused on ensuring that employees were able to be productive at work and not necessarily as interested in probing "outside the box" of what I described to find deeper issues.

Just something to keep in mind.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Thank you. Yes, that's one potential limitation. Another is literal, in that the number of sessions is typically capped. But it's what I think of as a gateway -- a trained and licensed mental health provider who can identify areas of concern and refer out if needed.

That might not be something all of them can do, will do, are empowered to do. Mileages always seem to vary. But by my understanding, because the number of sessions is capped, part of the function is to refer out people who need more care.

For Bring Jax Home
Guest May 16, 1:07 p.m.
QUESTION: Two of my husband's brothers and a niece and nephew were injured in a serious car accident in which they were all injured (one brother-in-law's neck was broken, though he survived). I became the conduit of information, both to and from, for friends and family.

It almost broke me, especially when people made suggestions they intended to be helpful (but they did not themselves help). About three weeks into this, seeing that I was reaching capacity to do this while working and caring for my young kids, two friends became my "communications secretaries." I gave them information and they made the contacts with relevant people. They filtered the return responses and suggestions from others, so that I only had to deal with appropriate stuff. And because they were one remove from the situation, they could simply say, "I'm sorry, I don't have the answer to that, but I should know more when I call you next."

Maybe Jax's friend could enlist some people to do that for them, especially if they have become "information central" for a wide circle. Because it's not sustainable, especially when you're dealing with your own anxiety.

school fundraiser misbehavior
Guest May 16, 2:01 p.m.
QUESTION: My daughter's school has an annual silent auction fundraiser. We typically do some small bids, but there was a particular piece of art we liked this year that was a little more expensive. We were the last ones to bid on the item before the auction closed. Yet, we were informed a few days later that there was a "mix up" and someone else bid after us and was considered the winner. Neither my husband or I understand how this is possible, as there was a bell and announcement, it was not unclear. My husband thinks we should ask the school to fix this, which involves physically getting the art back, refunding the other person, and allowing us to pay and take it. I have suggested letting it go (after all, the school got the funds, which was the point). We save our money, buy other art, maintain harmony - although I also believe we should decline to attend poorly-run auctions at the school in the future, and contribute to school fundraising in other ways. What do you think? And if we want to find out the details of what happened, are we just stirring the pot?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Heh. I think you definitely let it go, because there are a few reasons the auction could have been messed up but there's no good outcome to complaining.

I also wouldn't "decline to attend poorly-run auctions at the school in the future," either. Next time don't leave without it when you win :)

By let it go, I mean really really let it go. But next time don't leave without your winnings when you win :)

(Clearly there was inside finagling, right? It's just a matter of why. Was the wrong piece put up for auction by mistake? Okay, school auction, you let that go. Did someone with more juice want it? That sucks. Did they drop it? Etc. None of it matters at this point, it's over, you aren't out any money, so forget it happened and la la la ...)

Group Conversations
Guest May 16, 2:06 p.m.
QUESTION: We are going on a trip with a small group (6) of long time friends. We love the group chats but a couple of them like to start side conversations during a group time. I find this very distracting, no matter if I‚Äôm the one being sidelined or the one in the larger group. I don‚Äôt want to miss any discussion! Should I make a request to the group: Can we agree to keep our conversation A group one so I don‚Äôt miss any of your stories? Or should I try to guide the side talker back to the group? Unfortunately, the most active side talker likes to pigeon hole my husband and seems oblivious to his constant chatter.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: That irritates me too. I don't think there's much you can do about it, though. You can certainly try to make your request to the group -- especially if you have, say, a hearing or focus impairment that makes following it all extra challenging -- but what if some group members dislike group talk and prefer 1 x 1?

The person being split off is the one who has the reins, and can say to the one trying to divert them off to a side comversation, "Hang on, I'm following this [main group] story." So, I say make a strong appeal to your husband.

My husband (the oldest in his family) was pretty horrified at the second class citizen treatment we both received because the favoritism extended to my choice of spouse and our kids. My in-laws, however, welcomed me in a way I'd never experienced from family so we don't spend as much time with my family.

But ever since my husband came on the scene, my family decided he is "abusive" and keeps me from contacting my family. They have severe amnesia of all the times I complained about being ignored and the emotional distance in the previous 20 years. I'm not sure why my husband's presence has changed their outlook, but they're convinced his behavior, not their behavior, is the reason I don't come around anymore.

My family doesn't believe me just like all the other times they didn't believe me when I expressed my feelings to them. That's the other side.

185Press Enter to expand
Elementary Teacher PLEADING with caregivers: A PSA
Guest May 17, 10:49 a.m.
QUESTION: Dear Carolyn,
Please let me advocate for caregivers to refrain from giving children phones until the end of middle school at the earliest.
I work in a small mid-westerrn town teaching 4th grade. Many of the students in my class have smart phones, smart watches, and unlimited access to the internet. On top of this, parents allow students to have phones whenever they want, so kids come to school having stayed up until after midnight on their phones (or gaming systems). One parent told me she couldn't control her 9 year olds phone usage because they have different phones, thus different operating systems! Others tell me the kids have phones so parents can have peace of mind of knowing where they are all time time (emotionally unhealthy for other reasons, but I have a soapbox for that too), in case the bus breaks down, in case they feel sick at school, in case we're on a field trip and something happens, etc. I realize there may be a very, very slight chance a child would need to get in touch with a parent because of an extreme situation at school, but even then there are overlapping systems in place to keep parents informed. Teachers spend precious hours in PD reviewing, updating, and practicing how to contact parents in many different scenarios.
Caregivers, please, please, please consider: the positive benefit of your child having a phone is so small compared to the negative impact of your child having this device on physical, social, emotional, and psychological well-being.
If you insist your child have a phone, please please, please consider the following: strict parental control through the device's system and through your authority as the parent; give your child an emergency only flip phone; set limits and enforce them.
Each year, the age of students with smart devices gets lower and lower. There are many research studies showing the negative influence these have on child development.

Thank you Carolyn for all the good work you do and for all the Nuts who contribute to making this chat a success.

Don't want belly rubs from a cheater
Guest May 9, 11:06 a.m.
QUESTION: Dear Carolyn,

In February, my partner started cheating on me. I found out in March and immediately ended the relationship. He was upset about getting caught, but not deeply remorseful, and the woman he cheated with is still in his life and in his phone (though I don't think they are going to get together).

A week after breaking up with him and blocking his number, I found out that I was pregnant. This was not exactly planned but it was also not UNplanned. I am 36 and we pulled the goalie a while ago, feeling some time pressure. Our thinking was that we would get married soon regardless, sooner if I happened to get pregnant.

I contacted him to tell him that I'm pregnant. He asked if I want to get back together, which I considered, but the answer is no. The trust is gone. Then he asked if I want to end the pregnancy, and the answer is absolutely no. I am 17 weeks now and so attached to this baby.

My ex-partner is very upset, because he wanted to be a father and wants to be involved. I shared ultrasound photos with him, but then he was upset because I didn't invite him to the appointment. He has called, crying, to ask if he can come over and rub my belly (he swears this is all he wants). I do not want to be touched by him because I'm too angry, but I can't decide whether this is unfair.

We will co-parent in some way (we have set up counseling to start planning for it), but do I have to involve him in the pregnancy too? It has turned out to be a much more tender and vulnerable experience than I had expected, and I wanted to share that with him but not under these circumstances.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: This is an unusually intimate heartbreak, I am so sorry.

I am sorry specifically that he made such a needy and clueless ask of you. I mean. No! Okay? No. Talk about a forfeited right. It's time for him to help *you* feel better, not the other way around.

I think he needs to hear this. That having broken it, he might consider thinking how to fix it -- instead of coming to you and expecting you, the person he just devastated and is now trying to handle her own pain, to take on his pain and make it all better, too.

Plus: You are going to be partners in raising this child, and you are going to have to find ways to get along, and that is going to require levels of forgiveness you never knew you had in you -- and that might be true even if there were no cheating involved! parenting is like that -- but tender-sweet-vulnerable access to your body is something to put way down on the access list right now. If it ever makes the list again.

So to answer your specific question: Your body is the one going through so much physically and emotionally, the latter of which he's responsible for, so you can decide, without being "unfair," what lines you don't want him to cross. You just can.

If you ever eventually want to give him some access to the experience of the pregnancy, as your anger recedes, or as something you decide will be better for the child and their bond, or because you believe you might regret your hard line later (just offering these, not endorsing any of them) -- then here's a way to think about it.

First, decide on your terms, not in answer to tears.

Second, think what is about you and what is about the baby. For example: Most incremental OB appointments are about your health, so, nope. If there's an ultrasound scheduled later on to measure fetal growth, then (quietly) keep an open mind about letting him be there for that.

You've got counseling coming. It's okay just to say no now and decide to decide later. "I have a lot of anger yet to process, pressuring me on top of that is not okay."

(Plus a 17-week belly is kind of just a belly. But that's beside all the points.)

Congrats on the baby and good luck through the extensive deep breathing process ahead.

So much advice
Guest May 9, 11:27 a.m.
QUESTION: My husband and I are the parents of two teens, who are generally sensible kids who share their lives pretty freely with us. In the past year, my husband started seizing on every opportunity to advise them about whatever subject the kids mentioned. It‚Äôs to the point where the kids can‚Äôt mention anything without being talked at for ten minutes by their dad. The kids zone out, exchange looks with one another and have also said (to my husband) that they feel condescended to and sometimes they‚Äôre just venting. I know my husband feels the inevitable, and normal, drift as the kids grow independent and he‚Äôs trying to be superdad in these last daily opportunities to parent. I‚Äôve said as much to him and said I get his instinct to parent more but we need to back off and let the kids heed their own judgment before we jump in. My husband got very angry and accused me of ‚Äúshirking‚Äù my duty as a parent and said the kids will get his advice as long as he‚Äôs alive (he‚Äôs perfectly healthy) and he owes them his ‚Äúwisdom.‚Äù It was out of character and bizarre. I tried to raise it again weeks later and he took the same angry stance. The kids now avoid him as much as possible so they don‚Äôt constantly hear about what they ‚Äúshould‚Äù do or have done.. What can I do?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Ironic, isn't it. He's living proof that advice is pointless when people don't want to hear it.

I'll just assume he won't appreciate it if you put it to him that way -- unless you know how to disarm him, or catch him at a particularly receptive moment.

Once you're dealing with someone so dug in as to be angry-reactive upon approach, questions and generalized concern are often a better entry point: "You okay?" Maybe a long walk, too, away from the kids. This is oddly specific, but there are reasons for that: A literal change of secenery from the source of stress is helpful to clarity of thought. Also helpful, physical activity. Movement can jar our deeper, scarier thoughts loose from their embedded places. Walking in particular, if we're able, has this power. Also, when people are facing forward, vs facing each other, they often find it easier to talk about difficult things (why cars can be such a great place for kids to unburden).

So, you have a partner who clearly is knotted up over some stuff and acting out. And not only is the path he's taking unproductive, but it's also directly undermining his own started purpose. He's pushing his kids away and they're tuning him out on contact.

This is something you might, might be able to point out to him eventually. If he does start talking a bit about this anxiety he's feeling, no doubt about the passage of time, about the kids' futures, about his own relevance -- then you can be supportive and sympathetic. The timing is pretty typical for a parent to go through a lecturing spasm.

But ideally he won't need you to point it out, because once someone gets to the point of admitting this stuff out loud, it's often self-correcting. Just saying it can reduce the anxiety dramatically. Then: "So I just need to shut up and trust that I've already shared whatever wisdom I have, and it's up to them to ask me or figure out how to use it."

Maybe way too optimistic. But keeping it in your mind as an anxiety response and dealing with him accordingly might get you there.

And the occasional: "My duty as a parent is also to recognize when something isn't working -- and if they're pushing me away, then I need to adjust my approach."

This is all Part 1. Part 2 is for your husband to talk to someone, about the anxiety and anger, if he is completely inflexible. (Or you do, if he's inflexible on that.)
