Credit card fraud?
Guest May 9, 11:38 a.m.
QUESTION: Hi Carolyn,
I'm writing on behalf of my husband with his knowledge. His best friend's wife recently called him asking for a loan. Apparently she has a history of poor financial management, took out a credit card in her husband's name (how?) and maxed out the card. She called my husband asking for money to pay the company. Even worse, her intentions is not to pay right away and make a deal with the company for a lower amount. My husband is ducking her calls. I told him he must confront her, tell her if she doesn't come clean to her husband, he'll have to tell his best friend himself. She has put my husband in a terrible place: keep a secret from his best friend (unacceptable )which could ruin the poor guy's credit or blow up a marriage. Any advise to me, the advisor?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: If this were my friend's spouse calling me for the loan, then I would go straight to my friend with this information. Meaning, I would not apply the usual, "You have 24 hours to tell my friend the truth or I will tell," here. Here's why:

I already know I can't trust the spouse (fraud), and there's room for plenty more damage in those 24 hours. So I tip my friend off asap. And I give my friend the info for the National Foundation for Credit Counseling. And the name of a good lawyer, if I know of one.

Any damage to the friend's credit or marriage already falls squarely on the shoulders of the spouse.

If there is damage to my friendship for doing this, then I will own that. A fair risk for doing what's right for my friend.

My advice to you, the advisor, is decide whether you agree with that hypothetical, and counsel your husband accordingly.
Non Profit Credit Counseling Services | Get Out of Debt | NFCC

SIL blocked me
Guest May 9, 12:13 p.m.
QUESTION: My brother has been married for 33 years to ‚ÄúLynn‚Äù. In those years, she built a wall between him and his family. She has held grudges and has never tried to get to know his family. She once accused me of trying to steal their tent I borrowed because my brother wouldn‚Äôt make plans to meet so I could return it. When our mother died, my brother didn‚Äôt come to the funeral because she refused to come. I recently discovered that my SIL has been blocking me on social media. I don‚Äôt know what I did to upset her, but it makes me sad that I am missing out on my brother‚Äôs life, my nephew‚Äôs life, and my SIL‚Äôs life, and I will never know why or if there is anything I can do to change it.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I'm sorry. This is mostly a brother problem. Lynn is the source, but he is the one who could have said this level of control and division was not okay, and instead he chose to go along with it. Who knows whether marrying her was part of his decision to separate from his family, or if the estrangement was an unintended consequence of being with her. It's a known phenomenon, though, for people who want some emotional separation (for whatever reason) but lack the strength to follow through, to find a partner who does the dirty work for them. If that's the case with your brother, then there really is very little you can do.

Either way, whether this was his choice or a slow-motion accident, all you have to counteract the distance between you is consistent, non-obnoxious effort to stay in touch -- with him, with her, with them. However you can manage it, in good faith, with as much patience as you can muster.

That may sound awful and self-erasing, but the alternative is to give up. Because that's the only one she has left you.

Her blocking you on social media is a sad thing and does cut you off from the feed of their news, but it really is just the last superficial tie. You are "missing out on my brother‚Äôs life, my nephew‚Äôs life, and my SIL‚Äôs life" already if you are not involved in it. So, focus on that -- in what ways are you still able to be involved? And try to get the most out of those.

Response to sister‚Äôs concerns!
Guest May 9, 12:15 p.m.
QUESTION: My sister is going down a rabbit hole about her 4 year old‚Äôs health. He has some eczema issues and she has started banning all sorts of food and products with certain chemicals in an attempt to solve the problem. I‚Äôm a child of the 80s where we ate and played with anything and everything so it‚Äôs hard for me to understand this level of anxiety. I obviously want to validate her feelings and be supportive. Are there any generic statements that I can make, even though I don‚Äôt fully understand or even agree with?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: The first thing you can do is not equate your sister's parenting experience with your 1980s childhood. That's basically saying it's her fault for being a headcase, instead of recognizing she has to work with too many different variables to count.

I mean -- do you know for sure her child isn't reacting to a food additive? Even the word "anxiety" comes with judgment attached.

I'm from Planet 80s, too, so I get where you're coming from. But that cred didn't make, say, my kids' reactions to scented laundry products any more real. Some products and exposures are just different now from 40 or 50 years ago.

The best thing you can do for your sister is "fully understand" that, as much as humans don't fundamentally change from generation to generation, the conditions parents have to deal with can change a lot. Just the exposure to information is an enormous change. My older sisters got their parenting advice from other people, TV, radio, print. Just the 10 year gap means I started to get mine from email and online, and socials by the end. Another 10 years and parents are saturated with so much information, misinformation and disinformation that the ones who aren't anxious might be the outliers.

So maybe commit to not judging her, understand what you don't understand, know it's not your place to "agree" unless you're a stakeholder in possession of all the relevant information, and ask if there's anything you can do to help.

So Much advice- here's just a little more (sorry)

Doesn't everyone deserve a second chance?
Guest May 9, 12:55 p.m.
QUESTION: I had some set-backs (a break-up which left me homeless combined with job loss) that were a wake-up call for me. I deserved all of it because I cheated on my boyfriend who was supporting me and had gotten me a job with his family‚Äôs company. After a lot of therapy, I came to realize that I had coasted through my 20s taking advantage of various people. I intend to change my life for the better and so I took a new job that pays much less but one I am qualified for. My brother and SiL were nice enough to rent me a room in their house which is very close to work. That's important because the only car I can afford is not very reliable.
In the past I was snarky and judgmental about my SiL for superficial reasons and she knew it so our relationship is strained. As part of my new life I‚Äôm trying to mend fences so I‚Äôve apologized and tried to befriend my SiL. She blew me off, saying that I‚Äôm only trying to ‚Äúmake nice‚Äù now that I need her and to forget it. My brother accepted my apology and we‚Äôve become close again but he takes his wife side in this so whenever she is around her animosity makes things difficult. Do I just accept this is how it‚Äôs going to be while I‚Äôm living here? I wish she‚Äôd give me another chance, I know she doesn‚Äôt owe me that but her cold shoulder is really wearing on me. Should I try again to explain myself or am I just going to have to live with this until I can save up enough money to move out?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I don't think explaining yourself is the way to go. You have already explained, and SIL didn't buy it.

Instead I suggest saying to SIL that you can see why she didn't buy it. For example:

"You have every reason not to believe my apology is sincere. I spent the last [number] years taking whatever I wanted from people and not caring about how I treated them. I also wasn't kind to you. If I were you, then I wouldn't believe me, either."

I mean, would you believe you? Be honest.

Your brother probably did because he's your brother. He either wants to believe you, or he's better at reading you, so he has more than your word to go on.

After you say this to your SIL, then say: "I am grateful you are letting me stay here, even though you don't believe me. I hope time will let me prove to you that I mean it. I created this mess, so it's on me to clean it up."

Then don't try to "befriend" your SIL. Be an excellent tenant, helper, guest, sibling and general citizen. That's how you live down past mistakes.
Post has 0 replies and 960 Likes
960Press Enter to expand
Can‚Äôt make anyone feel
Guest May 9, 1:25 p.m.
QUESTION: I‚Äôve heard this- you don‚Äôt have control over anyone‚Äôs feelings. Okay, makes sense. But is the reserve true- that you are not accountable to anyone‚Äôs feelings? So if I tell someone ‚Äúyou are making me feel sad‚Äù is this wrong? Isn‚Äôt there some accountability for affecting people‚Äôs feelings? I may have been talking to someone else who misinterpreted this. I just want some clarity.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Well ... as manipulators across time have always understood, there's a lot we can do to control other people's feelings.

What you're getting at is where the lines of reponsibility go. It's a really good concept to have down, because it's what differentiates healthy relationships from unhealthy ones.

Each of us is responsible for what we choose to do. That includes taking into account its possible effect on others.

But that doesn't mean anything I do that hurts someone else is wrong. I don't have to make plans with someone I don't like, for example, just because saying no would hurt them. My time is my responsibility, not theirs.

If instead I did something hurtful just to hurt, like ... I broke something of theirs out of spite, then their hard feelings would totally be my fault.

So to take your example, "You are making me feel sad." It depends on the context, but in general, it's okay to tell someone when you are feeling sad in response to their actions toward you. "When you say these things to me, I feel really sad." Even if it's not something they're doing to you, it's okay. Like if someone moves away: "I'm sad we don't see each other anymore."

But that doesn't translate into another person's responsibility to make you un-sad. You hope the person will stop saying the things to you -- but if they don't, then it's up to you to do what you need to protect yourself from that person.

I'm taking forever on this, explaining it through examples. In any situation, though, you can reason it out by tracing the baseline responsibility. Whose choice it is, whose business it is, whose time it is, who has the last word. When you're trying to get other people to make you feel better, or trying to manipulate how others feel for your own advantage, then you're crossing lines.

Summer with Nephew...
Guest May 2, 11:03 a.m.
QUESTION: My sister (single mom if that matters) is having a challenging time with her 15yo son and asked if he can spend 8 weeks with us this summer for "change of pace." We have two teenage daughters and they are excited at the prospect of his coming. My husband and I asked to talk about expectations - contribution to household chores, cleaning up after one's self in the kitchen, do own laundry, and he needs a job or volunteer thing or participate in a sport. We have these expectations of our own kids. Nothing different. He's not welcome to scroll through social media for 8 weeks on our couch. My sister was indignant saying that of course her son would help out and of course he'd clean up after himself and that we are expecting the worse in her son and if we aren't going to support them, then why even bother? I really want to provide her a break and her son an opportunity to be some place new, but I know myself and I don't do well with kids just laying about all summer. I also know my nephew right now is disrespectful to my sister leaving messes all over the house, routinely skipping school, and doesn't contribute to the household at all. Our girls work at our local pool and we can probably get nephew the check-in desk job for a few hours a week and maybe a local rec summer baseball team could take him on, but it all requires planning. Ideas for how to re-engage my sister again? - so that she can hear we are trying to support her and our nephew but not at the expense of our sanity?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: You've said what you need to say about your rules. If you mean to follow through with hosting your nephew, then this is one of those things best not over-talked. Say to her that you weren't "expecting the worst" -- or the best! -- or expecting anything. You were just spelling out *your* expectations to preempt any misunderstandings. Your basic abundance of caution. So now if he and she are on board, great, then you'll start the arrangements, "let me know."

The more you say, the more you validate the idea that you're worried about him and the the deeper you dig the hole.

Dog with cancer
Guest May 2, 11:18 a.m.
QUESTION: I read last week‚Äôs chat after the fact, but I feel compelled to comment on the person whose dog has cancer and the vet bills are pushing them to the brink. A lot of people suggested ways to pay for the dog‚Äôs care. If the OP can find a way to do that that doesn‚Äôt cause them irreparable financial harm, great. But I‚Äôd also like to give the family permission to say a kind, humane goodbye if they need to, without guilt. For every ‚Äúmy dog was cured and lived to 16!‚Äù story I‚Äôve heard, I‚Äôve heard three ‚Äúwe went into debt and got 3 not-great months and watched the dog suffer‚Äù stories. I have said goodbye to many, many dogs in my life‚Äîsome when they were way too young to die. All of them were gut-wrenching and I sobbed for days, sometimes weeks. But every dog who has left me has also opened the door for another dog to come into my life. And that new dog helps to heal me, and because I always get rescues, I hope I‚Äôm helping to heal them, too.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Yes, thanks for bringing this back up. I meant to get back to it but got chat tired and never finished the thought. It is a completely defensible position -- for the family and the animal, not just the finances -- to choose not to subject a pet to extensive medical intervention in the face of a dire prognosis.

We decided upfront for our pets, for example, that for a cancer, we'd do surgery if that was an option but not a bunch of cycles of chemo, because making a dog sick over and over without being able to explain what it is and why was not something we had the stomach to do. Other pet parents and their vets might go a different way for different reasons, no judgment, but that was a part of our pre-adoption conversation.

Dogs and cats have short lives relative to humans, too, so it's fair to factor that into the calculation of suffering in treatment for lifespan gained.

The more comfortable we get with having conversations like this toward humane and compassionate outcomes, the better, IMO -- about pets and people. Thanks again.

My daughter wants me to be more excited about her wedding
Guest May 2, 11:24 a.m.
QUESTION: I accidentally hurt my daughter‚Äôs (‚ÄúLisa‚Äù) feelings and I want to make amends. Lisa is 24 and just got engaged. Personally, I think she‚Äôs a little young to settle down but since I had her at 21, I‚Äôm not one to talk. And I like her fiance, ‚ÄúJohn‚Äù a lot and do think they‚Äôre a great couple so it‚Äôs all good. On Easter Sunday Lisa announced to the whole family that she and John were getting married in 2026 and told the story of the elaborate proposal he‚Äôd arranged. Later my sister asked me why I wasn‚Äôt more surprised and emotional and I said that they‚Äôd been discussing marriage for months and ring shopping so there was nothing surprising about it. Lisa later told me she was devastated that I down-played the very romantic proposal John had arranged for her and wished she had a ‚Äúnormal‚Äù mom who would be as excited as she was. I apologized but Lisa still seemed upset. Last night we talked it out and she asked me to be excited about her wedding and behave like a ‚Äúreal‚Äù mother-of-the bride. It‚Äôs true that I‚Äôm pretty laid back about these sorts of things, probably because I‚Äôve never been married or even interested in getting married. I love Lisa so much and am sorry she thinks I carped on her big announcement. I'm giving them almost half the money they need for the wedding but otherwise I‚Äôm not sure I can fake an 18-month interest in wedding details. What should I do?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I think this is a fascinating question.

Which means Lisa would probably wish I would behave like a "real" advice columnist and tell you to rally around your daughter.

Which of course I do advise, but you're only going to be able to do this well, credibly, in a way that is true. And while there's always some Lycra in that -- we can always find something new to learn about others and ourselves -- you're still you and you're never going to thrill at the idea of mother-daughter facials. (Or are you? Who knows.)

So I think the first thing you have to do is go back to Lisa to say you love her, genuinely 100 percent are there for her, and 100 percent want what is best for her in life. And you think John is great and great for her. Say you never want her questioning these things. [This is where you wait for her to nod or something.]

Then you point out that you can only be who you are. Which means if she wants a mother who is genuinely swept up in a lot of the wedding details, then you are going to disappoint her -- possibly many times over by the time the I-dos are all-dones. Which is exactly the last thing you want to do!! Truly. Tell her it breaks your heart that you let her down already. [Pause again for evidence of daughterly absorption.]

Then the message: So here is what you are proposing:

You will love her and love how excited she is about this and pay amply for it and, where it counts most, rally to be the mom she wishes you were.

And in return, you will ask her to behold you, the mother-of-the-bride she actually has, and find it in her heart to see you as "real."

(Also maybe note that if she zeroes in on a few top priorities for her Real Mother of the Bride to rally for, then this will set you up for success.)

Security person here on Tracking Stalker
Guest May 2, 11:38 a.m.
QUESTION: 1. OP should do a thorough review of all their computers and devices to ensure that THEIR devices haven't been compromised in some way.
2. They should also change all their passwords, ensure multifactor authentication is on for their email and all accounts possible.
3. They should also review the privacy settings and connections on social media to ensure their information isn't publicly available and isn't leaking to the tracking stalker through a shared connection.
4. They should document this most recent encounter.
5. If there is any further contact they should clearly tell the tracking stalker clearly and simply that the stalker's contact is not welcome or wanted and they are to stop making contact. That encounter should also be documented.
6. If the stalker continues to contact the OP, they should document it regularly, contact the police and look at other physical security measures like self-defense courses.

They boyfriend can/should do all this too but at this point the OP's focus needs to be on themselves and the tracking stalker is after them as well.

Take this seriously, read De Becker's Gift of Fear, take action. Do hesitate. Do not rely on "orders of protection". Assume this person is armed, dangerous, unpredictable and will take violent action.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: This is in reference to last week's chat. I saw it only on a trip through the outtakes. I was not as concerned about the possibility of other access because OP mentioned the ex had access to the date's text messages, ergo the phone number. However, 1-2 is just good hygiene anyway, 3-4 is sensible after a weird encounter, and 5-6 apply to anyone who crosses a line more than once. I'm happy to post it.

Preemptive comment: A lot of people respond by seeing these as overreactions, but if you read carefully it's preparing, assuming, reading, documenting. I.e. not being the one who is surprised if it's the rare worst case.

Mental Load Backfire
Guest May 2, 11:50 a.m.
QUESTION: Hi Carolyn, I wrote a question about this to you a couple of months ago when it first erupted, but I don't think it ever ran. I'll try to summarize the background as quickly as possible to get to the awful new update.

Background: Husband and I both work full time, but I usually do 99% of the administrative stuff for my household, like lots of moms. Summer camp/activity planning has always been my domain until last year, when I got super fed up with it and my husband agreed to take it on for 2025. He disregarded all of my tips about timing, ignored the emails I forwarded with the kids' favorite places and their deadlines, and we ended up with 0 weeks scheduled for each one (later updated to 2 weeks of a crappy backup place that my husband's brother recommended). My husband knew he blew it and agreed that he would be in charge of keeping the kids occupied during 75% of work hours over the summer (I agreed to cover the rest). The kids, to provide all needed info, are 9 and 7. They can play together for a while but do need supervision and need help with food. Not the most fun summer for them, but doable because we both telework.

Okay that brings us to now. My husband's work abruptly called everybody back into the office effective June 1. I still telework. This means that even though we had agreed my husband would take on the primary responsibility for the kids, it's actually ALL GOING TO FALL TO ME. Our family focus right now is on my husband's frustration about the big change at work, but I am quietly seething with rage because I would have just booked the camps myself if I had known I would spend all summer being interrupted for snacks and noises. I am so mad I can hardly start thinking my way to solutions. Is there anything I can do to salvage the fairness of the arrangement?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Ooh, I remember this one -- it was in my queue to answer but I never got to it. And I have to tell you, just reading it gave me a somatic stress response. Then and now, with the update.

It's not just the camp fail. It's the underreaction to the fail and what it means. Saying the equivalent of, "My bad, I'll be in charge of keeping the kids occupied 75 percent," is not enough.

This, to me, is what enough would have looked like:

"You were trying to make a point by handing this to me, not only that you are overwhelmed but that I completely take it for granted that you will handle all the intricate details of our family's management. You do it seamlessly and I just let you.

"So the one time you make it clear you've had enough, you hand one responsibility over to me -- and not only that, you spoon feed it to me, with all the instructions and warnings and necessary links and emails and secret codes. And what do I do?

"I totally ignore it. And trash our kids' summer -- and yours -- because I can't be even minimally arsed.

"So. Obviously I am on the hook for their care this summer. That goes without saying. It will be only to the extent I can, of course, with my job, and I will need your help, but I will be the Mental Load Boss of their care. That's what I'm saying. This summer and ever after too. Because I get it now.

"More important, though, I am telling you -- I get it now. I have been a complete barnacle on this ship that you have been running singlehandedly for years. And there is no excuse for that. That injustice ends now."

Right?

So the fact that your household is now focused on his RTO feelings and not on your "enjoy getting that 75 percent child-care back!" feelings, and your rage is "quiet-seethe," is proof there's a way bigger problem than his biffing camp deadlines.

Fairness can be salvaged only if he can grasp fairness. Can he? Can he? (Said like Cam in "Modern Family.")

BTW -- if any camps are available still, at outrageous late signup cost, then perhaps there's something of his he can give up to finance them. That too would be fair.

528Press Enter to expand
Mental Load Backfire
Guest May 2, 12:38 p.m.
QUESTION: It would be so lovely if the husband so beautifully understood and followed through.
Absent that, what does OP do? Are acceptance and resignation vs Divorce the only choices?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I think there are a lot of in-between steps, all geared toward that overdue aha moment -- certainly a few that involve couples counseling.

I also think the "acceptance and resignation" side can include some bargains with reality that push it more toward a fair deal of some kind, just not the fairness we're talking about (50-50 domestic labor split). The carriers of 99 percent can extract 99 percent of something else of value in return, especially after the kids start to launch -- they get to choose where the couple retires, for example, or get to pursue a "dream" career later in life.

Actually, a version of this alternative bargain happens all the time -- the parent who does most of the work has most of the relationship with the kids. It doesn't always work out that way, but that is one of the known outcomes.

But it's super fraught. If the one doing less is actively shirking the responsibility, and also earns more, then the one carrying the domestic weight can get unceremoniously dumped into a bad financial situation as soon as their housework and childrearing services aren't needed.

So, better option is to keep talking and documenting toward that beautiful moment of understanding and follow through. If there is zero progress and acceptance is out, then, divorce.

ANSWER: (This is in response to last week.)

Please know everywhere that you did nothing to provoke this.

And no, I would not encourage you to try hard to get through to her. It's time for you to take care of you. Not a psychiatrist, though -- a psychologist. How did I not need to have this? If you were me you would have invited them for dinner and then been okay with everyone sitting everywhere for the next 30yrs? Or just avoided inviting them for a meal ever? That wasn't going to be possible. We invited them. I served dinner at the table and both FIL and MIL got up and sat in the living room and asked for TV trays (which we don't own for no specific reason - just don't have them).

We both asked if we could all sit at the table and FIL said no, he wasn't a kid, and I couldn't force him. So, we carried our plates to living room and watched TV as we ate.

We just haven't invited them back for a meal.

And the readers are too kind - there is no reason why they can't sit at a table. They go to friends' houses and business associates and sit at their tables. avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: So, hi! Thanks for writing back. This is a useful follow up.

He sounds deeply emotionally stunted. In a manner, in fact, to which we're all becoming a little too well acquainted these days.

The answer to your question is, yes, sort of -- I would have been "okay" with *their* sitting everyhere, letting them be weird for the next 30 years. You say, "So, we carried our plates to living room and watched TV as we ate," and that's where we differ. I would have let them go sit alone, then and forever. Their choice. We'd be at the table. My house, you see.

And for future meals, yeah, the menu would get super simple and I wouldn't be the one cooking -- and I and my spouse and whoever else was there would eat at the table. The invitations to dinner would also drop in frequency, sure. But not to zero if zero gave them the satisfaction of being able to complain to their friends about me.

I'm just saying my method would be to concede as little as possible and add as little fuel as possible and exert as little effort as possible. And to show as little irritation as possible, with this as my general unspoken messaging:

If you lunatics think this is your big dramatic power play, then go ahead and eat your reheated frozen cocktail weenies in front of the TV and gloat, congratu-freakin-lations. The grownups will be in here. [tight smile]

It's not for everyone, but it's a way of opting out of an overt power struggle by identifying and claiming all winnable turf in advance.

He was opposed to her continuing the pregnancy and got violently abusive when she decided to have it (e.g. he rammed her car with his truck when she was 5 months pregnant and drove her into a ditch). She got a restraining order against him and doesn‚Äôt want child support or anything from him but to leave her and the baby alone. That RO hasn‚Äôt stopped him from harassing us and stalking her. His father is on the police force so they do the bare minimum when we report the violations. We keep detailed logs of all his behavior, which ranged from trying to get into the hospital when she was in labor, endless harassing calls (she blocks his number but he keeps getting new ones), banging on our door all hours of the day and night, slashing her tires, vandalizing our home, following her car home from work, the latest was him trying to break into my daughter‚Äôs room in the middle of the night. Our dog went berserk and that scared him off but it looks like he‚Äôs never going to stop. Like always, we turned over the logs to the cops and they said (like every other time) ‚Äúcall us if he actually does anything to her.‚Äù
I talked to my sister who lives over 400 miles away and she thinks we should leave quickly and quietly, letting no one know where we‚Äôre going except my boss who is aware of the situation. She said we can stay with her until we find a new home and jobs there. This is such a drastic step - the cost of living there is much higher and if I quit I won‚Äôt be able to collect unemployment. We can live on my savings for a while but I‚Äôm worried about selling our house remotely, and what happens if it takes me and my daughter too long to find jobs. I‚Äôm an administrative assistant and would take excellent references with me and she‚Äôs an LPN who can work in any hospital or clinic but in this economy, who knows? We‚Äôve lived in this town since my daughter was 4 months old; my husband is buried here but I don‚Äôt know what else to do. Neither of us can take this much longer. Is moving the right thing to do?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Call 1-800-799-SAFE -- the National Domestic Violence Hotline. You concerns for your ability to support yourself are real and valid, and of course this is your home where you have ties. This is one of the many reasons the stalking is such a horror. It robs all of you of your agency, your peace of mind, your security, your home -- this is the power the ex is abusing.

But being right means squat if you-daughter-grand aren't alive. So talk to the hotline and get trained support asap to develop a plan to get your safety back in one form or another.

The police inaction is an outrage. The "actually does anything to her" is a false standard if he's committing property crimes and trespassing, which are more than enough -- I don't think one needs a law degree to 2 + 2 that one.

Chronic guilt pit
Guest May 2, 1:50 p.m.
QUESTION: I am crowd sourcing the group here: What is everyone doing about the guilt pit they swallowed that sits in the pit of their stomach like a weight? What, is that not normal? Just me?

I feel guilt for moving away from my family, I feel guilty for not calling my friend more, I feel guilty for not going on that jog, I feel guilty for not paying my babysitter more, I feel guilty for not taking on that extra project at work, I feel guilty for not being 'in-the-mood' more, I feel guilty for not putting more effort into my hair, I feel guilty for not enjoying the weather UGH

Any one thing I could fix relatively quickly, but it just is feeling like a game of whack-a-mole. Knock one thing out and there are just endless more guilt pits of varying sizes!
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: By whose life instructions are you living?

That's my main question.

I for one can't be part of your crowd to answer your "guilt pit" question because I don't have the pit. I am, though, upon quick inspection, just as "guilty" as you are -- moved away from my hometown (5.5-8 hour drive, depending on traffic); crappy, out-of-touch friend (too long a rap sheet to document here); chair jockey (üëã); hair tragedy (attached).

But you know what? Who gives a shirt. I loved my parents, and they knew it. If I felt guilty for leaving, then I wouldn't have moved or would have moved back -- but I didn't because my reasons for leaving were better than my reasons for staying. Are yours? If they're not, then consider moving back. (This is a walk I walked -- moved back-ish 18 years after I moved away, when circumstances changed.) And if you can't, then you can't, and it's not up to you so no guilt. It really can be that simple.

I love my friends, and they know it, and they aren't great about calling me, either. They've got stuff to do.

Yes, I sit too much. Arrest me.

And what is not to love about the free time made available by this hair.

Again -- for whom are you living? Not you, currently. That's the issue. In order of appearance, you see yourself living in service of family, friends, societal standards, your employee, your employer, your partner, societal standards again, and you're at the mercy of the passage of time. Whew.

It feels like the most flippant, heartless advice ever to tell a well-meaning stressed out person, "You need to figure out who you are," but I suspect you are in fact suffering the accumulated effects of working hard at checking what you were told were the boxes of being a good person -- without having fully reckoned with who you really are and want to be. Or why anyone's opinion of you matters.

I hope I'm wrong and you're just flat out freaking exhausted. That works, too, since you're a working parent of young kid(s) with a partner and well that's way more than enough for one person's to-do list. But it's enough for a lot of people not to need any encouragement to say "erf my hair."

So that's the arcing wire -- who/what is saying you need nice hair and muscle tone and a perfect attendance award to matter? You don't. You matter as-is.

How to be positive for teen
Guest May 2, 1:57 p.m.
QUESTION: I‚Äôm having a hard time balancing my own negative feelings about the state of the U.S. and the world with trying to be a positive example for my teen. He is autistic and has depression and anxiety, and has become even more negative about everything with the new administration. I feel very negative myself and don‚Äôt know how to help my kid be more positive when I am not feeling it myself. I don‚Äôt stick my head in the sand or want us to be fake happy, but I also don‚Äôt want to think about negative stuff all the time. ALL he ever talks about is very negative stuff. He is seriously Debbie Downer. Yes, there‚Äôs a lot of awful stuff going on, and yes, feline aids is the number one killer of domestic cats, but I cannot focus on that constantly. He is in therapy and on medication, btw. As am I. I feel like I am failing him. How can I be real without projecting a feeling of hopelessness.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Yes, there are objectively bad things happening right now that contribute to a sense of negativity, and yes this feeling will be heavier for a teen with autism and for a parent of a teen with autism. I won't try to fake-happy you either.

I'm reading this, though, as I look out the window on one of the more dazzling May days I've ever seen.

And this part of it all is not fake: The backdrop of the things dragging you down is a big noisy complicated world that has nature, art, music, dance, food, literature, crafts, sports, movies ... and love, and family, and humor, and about a thousand things at any given time that can break your heart with their beauty.

So I don't know. Maybe it will feel "fake" to drag yourself outside to look at a beautiful thing, like a crescent moon or a cityscape or a museum exhibit. Or to put on music in your home that you can sing along to, if your son isn't noise-sensitive. Or to put a game on TV and start following a team he likes. Or to make yourselves get in the car to go to a store to buy the ingredients or materials for a meal or a project you can work on together. Or go to your local library to take advantage of the progamming, classes, materials there. Or start doing yoga with him every day.

But at some point, maybe 15 or 30 minutes in, it won't feel fake anymore. Or maybe if you make a habit of it, after a day or two the practice won't feel fake anymore, and the anticipation of your walk or meal or project or music or game time will start to feel genuine.

There is a whole lot of life out there, is what I'm saying, that was there before all this and will be there after, because that's how humanity works. We over focus sometimes and forget that. I mean -- you're probably upset in part because so many of these great things are getting squeezed right now, or threatened even, I get that. But if anything now is the time to go all in and live them to show what they mean to you. So give your humanity a kick. That's the mood lift. And that's the antidote to hopelessness.

Re: Mom and reality
Guest May 2, 1:59 p.m.
QUESTION: My mother blamed us for things that we didn't do (and for things that never actually happened).

My sisters and I used to "go beige," as we called it. We wouldn't challenge her or ignore her. If she said something like, "I was really offended that you went on vacation with your in-laws again, but you never have time for me," we would say, "Oh, we're sorry that you're feeling bad. Let's plan something fun we can do together." If the in-law trip never happened, we didn't address that. If she perseverated on the phone or in person, we wouldn't cut her off mid-sentence, but when she stopped for breath, we would say, "Well, you've given me something to think about, thanks. But I have to go now."

We never addressed (with her, but we did with our dad) that she needed psychiatric care, and we didn't get angry or defend ourselves.

Our mother still went into rages and made bizarre accusations, but we reminded ourselves that it had NOTHING to do with us, what we had done or done done--and that we couldn't fix it with discussions, reality, or even love.

Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: üëÜÔ∏è

Dementia Breaking Boundaries
Guest May 2, 2:05 p.m.
QUESTION: Hi Carolyn. My father wrestled with mental health issues for most of his life that led him to be unstable and emotionally and verbally abusive. Over the past decade, I have constructed a set of boundaries that keeps him at arms length to protect myself and my young family. Recently, he was diagnosed with Alzheimer‚Äôs, which explains why things have gotten worse over the past few years. I am trying to figure out what to do with my carefully constructed and maintained boundaries now. My brother says that our father needs us and that I need to be there with my dad, engage with him by phone and text, and just take his abuse and let it roll off me when it comes - chalking it up to the Alzheimer‚Äôs over which dad has no control. Since my father was abusive before the Alzheimer‚Äôs, I‚Äôm having a hard time giving him a free pass on his behavior even now and feel like we cannot really tell what behavior is good-ole-dad being horrible or the result of dementia. But perhaps I am ignorant and need some education about this new diagnosis and I am willing to learn. It also feels like the impact on me and my family is the same, regardless of the cause of the abusive behavior. Having my father send me texts where he calls me a good for nothing piece of garbage hurts today just as these actions hurt 5, 10, 20, and 40+ years ago. And I do not want my wife or three children (ages 11, 13, and 16) to be exposed to someone who thinks nothing of ruining a family dinner with yelling, swearing, and name calling because there is too much butter on the vegetables in his opinion. (True story.) I do not want to be a bad son, brother, or person. But I don‚Äôt know how to be a good son while also being a good husband and father and while also keeping my own mental health in tact - under these challenging circumstances. Do I re-engage with my abusive father in service to him knowing it puts me and my family at risk? Or do I keep my guards up, protect myself and my family, and let my father and brother down in this time of need? I can‚Äôt find the middle ground here - or even any stable ground from which to approach this problem. It seems like heartbreak all around no matter what I choose.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: You do not "need" to "be" anywhere or do anything. You are in charge of yourself and you make your own decisions. Your brother has no right to order you around like that.

You may decide your father's care is a responsibility you want to share with your brother, or feel you owe him, or your own sense of decency. Fine. But that's your call, not his.

You may also decide to help out at arm's length, which is valid and fair. Finding him good care, helping to pay for it as you're able, or not. Protecting yourself and family.

Here is the Alzheimer's Association. It has a help line. Lean and learn.

I'm sorry you were so badly mistreated. Don't let him anywhere near your kids.
Alzheimer's Association | Alzheimer's Disease & Dementia Help

Psychiatry vs psychology
Guest May 2, 2:22 p.m.
QUESTION: Why do you recommend psychologists over psychiatrists? It is true that a lot of psychiatry residency programs focus on medication rather than psychotherapy.
But not all of them‚Äîand not the best ones. Psychiatrists can be excellent psychotherapists depending on their inclination and where they trained. I think I am that kind of psychiatrist. My medical background and my expertise in medications are in the background as I do psychotherapy to be called on to rule out somatic causes for psychic distress. Also, the meds background helps me to prescribe appropriately to decrease the patient‚Äôs pain and suffering.
How do you find a psychiatrist that does psychotherapy? You ask them if they do or if they farm it out to non-medical therapists. Non-medical therapists can be good at what they do.

How do you tell if a therapist/psychologist/psychiatrist is a good choice for you?
1.) the relationship feels, on a gut level, like a good fit.
2.) Problems are on the way to being solved by 4 months.
3.) The professional understands you‚Äîon a racial/gender and background level. They should be willing to accept that your background/problems are just as severe as you say. Though, in a reversal, they may be able to point out aspects of the patient‚Äôs problems that are worse than the patient feels they are.
4.) Always, always there should be respect and acceptance.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: This is great to add to the search criteria in the caregiver search, thanks.

I recommended that as a shortcut -- the searching process is arduous, and if someone is calling practices, a lot of psychiatry offices will focus on medication or illness management and say no to psychotherapy. Trying to spare somebody some calls.

Psychiatry vs psychology
Guest May 2, 2:45 p.m.
QUESTION: A shout-out, too, to social workers. One of the best counselors I saw had that degree, and came highly recommended by a psychiatrist. Don‚Äôt focus as much on the degree as you do on the relationship and the quality of the insights.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Yes, the social work family is the go-to in most cases for talk therapy for a lot of reasons. In this case the mom's apparent mental illness pointed to a Venn diagram situation of a talk-therapy provider with a doctorate-level credential. I should have explained my reasoning (clearly), but that was a one-off. Also clearly (from the past 25-30 years) I do not get specific about credentials in most of my recs

Homophobic Relatives
Guest Apr 25, 11:04 a.m.
QUESTION: Dear Carolyn,

In light of Everything, I am trying to get my actions in better alignment with my principles. Among other things, this means no longer tolerating bigotry of any kind from my acquaintances. If they want to be in community with me, I'm going to challenge them on it; if they don't want to change, I don't want the relationship. Even though I happen to be cis and straight, I love a lot of people who aren't, including my sister who is pan/poly and currently in a relationship with another woman. Therein lies my question. How do I handle it if my sister wants to remain on friendly terms with people I think have disrespected her horribly? Our parents are 90% of the way to acceptance, but their siblings and some older family friends are a mess.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Huh. This took a turn I wasn't expecting.

I was ready for you to ask how best to stand up for your sister.

I guess that is still the question in the broadest sense. But the reality is much more nuanced once you factor in that your sister is both on friendly terms with people you're objecting to, and not asking you to stand up for her.

Typical reality. Refusing to make things easy.

The short answer for you is to do -- keep doing -- whatever you think is right. If you don't like these relatives/friends, whether it's because their morals offend you or their politics annoy you or their conversation bores you, then keep your civil distance. That's your prerogative. If you hear them say something offensive or that you know to be untrue and harmful, then politely but firmly correct the record. Nothing new or exciting here. "That is disinformation. It's _____. I'm sorry you were misled." Or: "That is unkind and untrue." And/or: "Don't talk about _____ like that in my presence."

The longer and more interesting answer is to pay some attention to your sister. She is the one with more on the line, yet has chosen a friendly path with those who aren't respectful of who she is. Why has she made that choice, do you know? Is it a kind of Stockholm Syndrome, or is she a total badass, or ____? Have you two talked about this? If she thinks the path to progress is through converting hostile people over time with her humanity, then she might not even agree with your decision to start slamming doors.

It would be interesting to know what brought your parents to their 90 percent (presumably they started at a much lower acceptance number). Time, pressure? Did shame from the "mess" cohort slow things down? Did shame from allies speed things up? Did pressures work in combination -- the pull of your sister's grace and the push of impatience from less BS-tolerant allies like you?

Anyway. Not to get too deep into a thought exercise, which is also a risk. Sometimes you do find yourself staring down a bigot, a true believer in wiping out anything "other," and you need to be ready to declare that person dead to you. Vulnerable people don't need their stronger allies playing footsie with storm troopers.

I think all the interesting stuff is right here: "if they don't want to change." Do you trust yourself to know?

It is okay, in general, just to commit to being braver on a day-to-day basis.

Helping a child choose a college
Guest Apr 25, 11:34 a.m.
QUESTION: My daughter applied to numerous colleges. Only her safety schools admitted her. She‚Äôs struggling between two similarly selective (40-50%) school, one very rural and one in a large city. She‚Äôs terribly disappointed and frozen. We‚Äôve done the pros and cons lists. A huge health issue upset her academic Apple cart last year and torpedoed her hopes of a super selective school. We as her parents are supportive but certainly don‚Äôt want to choose. One school has offered a lot more money which does matter to some extent. Any suggestions on how to provide support here?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I'm sorry she's feeling so disappointed. It sounds as if she is in a pretty decent spot except by comparison to where she was hoping she would be. I know there's no cheerleading someone out of that, she is just going to have to keep moving forward until she realizes she likes where she is (or wants to transfer).

If the only way she moves forward is with a gentle shove from you, then you may, de facto, be choosing for her. It's not great but it's something.

So here are the steps I'd take in that position.

First, don't rush her. May 1, right? If she processes quietly, then her "frozen"might be thought and recovery.

Next: If there is a specific program she wants, then that of course goes to the top of the list, but as long as each has a selection then that can be secondary.

Next: The obvious is to say, "Take the money!!!" And not just because it's money!!! but there is something to be said for a school that really wants you. That's a case you can make to her that isn't a bunch of spin.

But ... agh. I'm not going to advise that. Because the difference between a rural and urban school is so huge that I think you are better off thinking hard about your kid's natural habitat -- or, if she is coming from the burbs and hasn't been in either environment, what that is likely to be. Which one is going to be right for her temperament.

Then make that case to her and say why: "You love underground art and music and I just see you in a city." Or, "You've always been drawn to quiet places." Reflect your kid back to her and see if she sees the same thing.

Last thing, an insane wild card. Does she have a seriously plugged in college counselor? Because some schools that haven't hit their numbers may take a late app.

Navigating family estrangement
Guest Apr 25, 11:53 a.m.
QUESTION: Hi Carolyn. I cut ties with my sister about ten years ago. Sister's untreated mental illness was sucking my parents and other siblings into a spiral. The other members of my family enable my sister's disfunction, and beg me to make up with her, even if that means sweeping issues under the rug. I think I would like to be on better terms, but I want to be an enabler either. I want my sister to acknowledge the harm her behavior has caused -- something other family members don't confront explicitly. How can I keep the door open to reconciliation, but with firm boundaries? I don't want to get sucked in to an unproductive fight again.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: The way to reconcile with firm boundaries is to understand what you do and don't control, and then be very clear in your purpose for and management of the part you control.

Let's look at your list of wants and don't wants:

1. "I want my sister to acknowledge the harm her behavior has caused." That is not something you control. You control whether you ask for it, sure, but it's up to your sister whether you get it. So I caution against this, because it puts a big part of your path forward in your sister's hands.

2. "I don't want to get sucked in to an unproductive fight again." This is something you control. When you feel a fight coming on or hitting an unproductive point, then you can walk away from it. Entirely up to you.

3. "I would like to be on better terms, but I don't want to be an enabler either." This is something you control. You can make an overture to your sister to resume the relationship, carefully. You can decide beforehand what behaviors you will not enable, accept or encourage. What that means in practice is that when you are, say, subjected to abusive behavior like getting screamed at, you will not listen to it or scream back or apologize for whatever set it off. You will simply end the conversation and step away. Knowing it is a health issue, you may choose to call again at a pre-determined interval, and continue this arm's length engagement. Or you may choose to check in less often or not at all.

As for your family, you let them know you are engaging on your terms and are not seeking input on this. If they start to intrude.

So yes, full menu. Thanks.

Cancer dog
Guest Apr 25, 12:14 p.m.
QUESTION: We just used the last of our savings to pay for a young and otherwise healthy dog -- our child's best, sleeping on the bed and meeting the bus with joy, buddy -- to have a cancerous tumor removed. All the signs are good and the vet believes the chances of recurrence are low. But if it comes back we _cannot_ afford to treat it again, not without taking on debt. We currently have no debt. But both adult jobs are unstable right now, and while the kids would 100% give up their extracurriculars to save the dog's life, another round of treatment would cost more than all their activities combined for a year, and I don't want to kneecap their life launch for a dog. But I'm typing this while I hold the dog and cry into his fur at the thought of killing him so my kids can what, take karate? I can't think and I'm so tired. He's such a good boy but the kids have to come first. How do I stick to my logical guns if I had to make this decision again? Husband is in the same boat as me, mentally.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Two thoughts. First, you just did this, you're stressed, tired and broke, so this is not the time to think about it. You don't have to decide now so don't.

Second, now may be the time to rethink the extracurriculars. Does everyone need all of the stuff? I mean -- I'm pro enrichment opportunities. But only if they're actually enriching, and not a reflexive signup at this point.

If you're down to no savings and the jobs are wobbly, then this seems like a great time to think about the real return on your activity investment. There's nothing wrong with being more mindful about how you do this, and seeing whether your kids' interests have evolved or come into tighter focus.

Any other cost shaving you can do elsewhere would also be good. This is the thinking to do now -- you know you may have an issue coming, so, do what you can on the money side to get ahead of it. Much better than dwelling on the dread side.

Political differences
Guest Apr 25, 1:02 p.m.
QUESTION: After much effort to build a close relationship with my dad despite geographical distance, the U.S.'s political chaos has revealed a huge wedge between us. His rule is that my siblings and I cannot discuss politics when we are together (because we are intractably opposed), but now when he and I speak on a call and I provide my perspective on an item of news he disagrees with, it's like the call has suddenly dropped. He just...doesn't engage. I feel like our relationship is becoming superficial and emotionally dishonest because we can't discuss reality. I'm not sure of my question exactly but I'm feeling loneliness in a relationship that used to provide me great comfort.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I think a ton of people are going through a version of this. It's anecdotal, but I'm seeing a lot of questions from people in mismatched coping pairs: The ones who want to process it all are coming home to the ones who want to live in a complete news blackout, and no one feels understood.

I don't think the wall you've hit is the end of it all with your dad, though. It's just a wall on the politics path. There are so many other conversational places to go.

The problem is, The Chaos (tm) means the others aren't front of mind -- and you're having these conversations by phone, so it's not like you can putter around your kitchen for a while in companionable silence until a new topic bubbles up.

So ... I'm going to suggest something that feels really artificial, but is so only in cutting out that gap time you don't have the luxury of allowing to elapse naturally. During the course of your days, any time you think of your dad, write down whatever topic you have in mind to run by him. Talk to your notes app, whatever. Even a couple of items will give you a pivot for when he disengages. It's something you had in mind, right? So it's valid -- it's just a little memory jog.

Try it, see if there's enough reality to discuss of other varieties to restore your faith in your relationship.

Couple of things.

So if they want to come live by their rules in your home, then can bring the food.

But really, I am throwing that out there because I look for possible solutions. If I were the person living with this, then I would hope my husband was talking to his parents separately in a "You have one shot at this, is this really the best you've got? Trashing us to your friends? Refusing &^%$! bingo?" conversation. After which they get told they know where to find him once they get their heads out of [places].

(What's the dad's issue with sitting -- does your husband know?)

Feeling like a failure
Guest Apr 25, 1:42 p.m.
QUESTION: Is it just a normal part of adult (or mom) life to constantly feel like you're failing at basically everything, or is that just depression? I can't figure out if what I feel is a normal reaction to societal pressure to be an impossible level of the perfect mom/wife/employee/other, or if it's just depression raising its stupid and ugly head.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Certainly you want to go through the steps of treating it as depression, because if it is (and it sure sounds as if it could be), then you don't want to leave it untreated.

As you have the medical side in progress, you can also have the nonmedical side going too, since those best practices are similar:

1. Tell societal pressure to erf off. It is not a contest, or a race, or a reality show. Praise dogs. Wouldn't it be awful if it were?

2. Streamline whatever you can streamline. When I'm in a state of unraveling, I sit down with a yellow pad and a Sharpie and dump out everything, all the stressful pieces, then cut it down to the have-tos.

3. Give yourself grace, breaks, forgiveness, belly laughs, deep breathing (box breathing is great), a serious reality check -- whatever it is you need or have closest at hand. The reality check is that perfection doesn't exist, for one, and that the closer people get to achieving it, the less likable they tend to be. K? So.

4. Eat nutritiously, exercise regularly, sleep on a schedule, and tend to other habits that help counteract depression.

This is about anxiety, but so much of the conversation applies to depression too. Specifically, it covers why it can be tempting to push back against "take a walk"-type advice but taking a walk is still good advice.

Anyway. Also, both are "normal" -- overwhelm and depression. Not a failure, and nothing stupid about it. Take care.
did you take your stupid mental health walk for your stupid mental health?

Plus, the counselors absolutely drove home your main point, not to apply if they wouldn't want to go. Choosing the "likelies" was some of the hardest thinking they had to do.

Emotional turmoil
Guest Apr 11, 11:16 a.m.
QUESTION: How do I stop hiding things from my husband and my family? I walk around terrified all the time. I always think I have to handle everything myself, that I can never ask for help. But then I end up not dealing with things in the best way sometimes and I get in trouble. I am trying to fix everything, but I am drowning. I'm sick to my stomach all the time, I can't sleep, and I think about running away a lot. I'm so scared I can't breathe. And I don't know what to do. Everyone expects me to take care of everything all the time - my job supports everyone, I pay all the bills. I take care of everyone but no one takes care of me. I don't know how to break out of this circle of hell.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I'm going to give you this first: It's information for the Crisis Text Line, 741741. It's an immediate place to get help when you're terrified and thinking you can't ask for help. You can. It's right there.

Of a similar nature, if you want to talk, is the 988 Lifeline, though you can also text or chat with this service as well. Use it, please.

As I'm sure you understand on some level, what you're doing is not sustainable because it wouldn't be for anyone -- meaning, it's not your fault or your failure. People just can't carry everyone and everything indefinitely without support. But the thing is, the longer you put off getting help, the more help you will end up needing.

That's why I urge you please to interrupt this cycle asap. Use these free resources. Therapy is where all roads lead in your scenario, but that can be hard to find, arrange, pay for and make time for privately -- so here's a possible shortcut, also a free resource. Even better would be if your employer offers an EAP, which could get you some counseling sessions right away. Let the focus of these first couple of steps be very narrow, just on getting someone to help you take the bigger steps. Take care. Check back in too if you can, if it's not another burden to deal with.
988 Lifeline
FindTreatment.gov
Home Page

Tired of being Google for my Husband
Guest Apr 11, 11:20 a.m.
QUESTION: Something snapped in me the other day when I realized my husband of 30 years tends to use me as Google instead of doing the research himself. "You want to hire this contractor, is there a better one?", "What are we going to go and what are we going to do on vacation?", "Where should we go to dinner?" and even better "what should I do after I retire?" On the one hand, I appreciate he is asking my opinion, on the other my opinions are most often then rejected out of hand or I am inundated with follow up questions about WHY I made a particular choice or know a particular fact with requests for followup reasoning. Maybe it's analysis paralysis, but I'd just like him to switch shoes and do some research and make a decision! How do I maintain my cool now that I bristle every time we have an exchange like this.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: You keep your cool by recognizing that you both pitched into this dynamic for a long time without noticing either your contribution or what you were creating.

And you recognize that if he were the one who had the sudden snap of unpleasant recognition, not you, then you would feel awful if he went from being his normal self to bristling and losing his cool at you without explanation, when for all you knew everything was the same between you as it was yesterday.

I'm not saying you aren't right to be annoyed about some/many/most/all of the things that now annoy you. My point is separate from justification. It's about the fact of long relationships and, in your case, 30 years of slowly building something together. Even if you didn't take active part and just contributed passively with your consent.

If you're going to pivot, then say so, and say why. And the less irritated you can sound about it, the better -- again, because that's what you'd want if your husband were the one abruptly waking up to his unhappiness. Ideally you want to make this a joint project toward a better quality of life together.

Just to give you an idea of what I'm thinking: The nuisance behaviors of his that you describe could stem from habits both of you have developed over the years. If, for example, you've always done the research on X or Y for both of you, year after year by default, then his abilities in that area may have atrophied without either of you realizing it, and now you're tired of always having to research X and Y and he's a weird combination of helpless at it and annoyed he has no agency. Again, just a riff.

The answer is not to be annoyed with each other. It's to say, huh, we let this slide to a place neither of us seems to want, let's try to correct it.

Forgotten grandchildren‚Äôs birthday
Guest Apr 11, 11:32 a.m.
QUESTION: My mother in law is a sweet, quiet woman who has begun to have some memory issues. We live far from her and see her once or twice a year. For the last two years, she has forgotten my twin son‚Äôs birthdays (they are 10). My kids don‚Äôt need gifts but it makes me sad that they don‚Äôt even hear from her . I know if she realized she had forgotten she would be sad and embarrassed. Should I say anything to her? Or just let it lie?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Contact her before the birthday every year (and before every other milestone she would want to mark) to "help" her "figure out what they'd like." It's the kind thing for all involved.

If she has already missed it this year, then, sure, let it lie -- because you're the one who's sad, right? If the boys are sad, then put them in touch ... but regularly, not just for their birthdays, because, really, who cares about a birthday. The point is for them to have a relationship with their grandmother, isn't it?

I don't know. Seems to me the issue is not where's-the-once-a-year-contact-for-my-children-on-an-arbitrary-day, but how can I make sure this sweet, quiet woman who raised my spouse (now living far away from her) isn't completely detached from her child's family as she slips into memory loss?

If she is the one holding herself apart, then I'll merge safely back into my lane, but "sad and embarrassed" suggest she does care about staying in touch.

LW Somebody that I used to know
Guest Apr 11, 11:53 a.m.
QUESTION: Earlier this week you had a letter from somebody who, when she runs into her ex-fiance professionally pretends not to know him. The ex has called her out and wants her to knock it off. You do, too, and I think you were too harsh. Her little game of pretending never to have met him when a work acquaintance tries to introduce them is petty af and I am here for it! Not that long ago it was accepted etiquette that divorced people were considered permanently estranged and need not recognize or acknowledge one another if they met socially. It's taking it a bit far to pretend never to have met him, but it is less awkward for the person trying to be polite by introducing them than an icy "we've met" or God forbid the actual truth. Maybe it would be healthier for her to get beyond it, but in the meantime her behavior is merely annoying to the ex (to whom she owes nothing) and neither impolite not truly hurtful. Hurtful is leaving someone at the altar and then expecting them to be okay with it.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I agree with you on one point -- that the LW's charade is "not truly hurtful." Certainly not on the level of walking away from an engagement.

But that's it.

Because I maintain that, while doing so clearly causes pain, walking away from an engagement is the right and brave thing to do if the idea of going through with marriage doesn't feel right. Because that half-hearted, "It would hurt her too much not to follow through" marriage will cause so much more pain over time. So I will not jump on any bandwagon to vilify someone who pulls the plug on an engagement.

Ideally people will face down their conflicted feelings sooner rather than right at the edge of the aisle runner. But even leaving someone at the literal altar is better than going through with a wedding with doubts.

So I'm supposed to cheer on a, what, decade-long campaign of pettiness because it supposedly gives a guy what he richly deserves? No. No no no. It perpetuates that whole terrible "he's the villain" mind set that actually pressures some couples to go through with marriages they really shouldn't.

I wish LW hadn't been hurt. I wish for LW's sake that the fiance, as I said, had come to terms with his doubts way sooner. But that's not how things always play out, and sometimes things end messy.

When they do, our best response is to have the tears and/or the fury and/or the dissection of recent history to see what signs we may have missed that this was coming, then square up and say to ourselves, "You know what? I wish it hadn't ended messy but thank the merciful dogs it ended before we got married." Then regroup, then rebuild, then say when you see your ex at a business conference, ice-free!, "Yes, we've met. You're looking well." Because you can say that, because you've recovered -- or at least you grasp that sounding as if you've recovered seamlessly is really, if this is what we're doing here, the pettiest play of them all.

I need a reality check....
Guest Apr 11, 11:58 a.m.
QUESTION: Are many teenagers sullen, messy, and completely annoyed by everything the parent says? Just feeling like I am the only parent right now....
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Did you miss every teen movie and book ever? And your own 12-17-ish?

Sorry, I still have some adolescent in me I guess.

People that age are a roiling mess of self-doubt and feelings and huge new thoughts and they take it out hardest on the ones they trust most to love them through anything and not abandon them. So, you. Plus you annoy them so much you make their cells vibrate with annoyance when you're within two rooms of them. It's not personal.

And it's not only not just you, it's pretty much everyone in your spot at some point.

Be present but not up in their business unless you have to. Be open to talking when the need to talk strikes them, even if it's midnight. The better you are at not taking it personally, the better you will be at the availability part. Getting defensive tends to escalate things needlessly. Last unsolicited thing, "don't take it personally" is not the same thing as being a doormat. Good luck!

Re: tired of being google

QUESTION: Just something to consider. My ex-husband and I had a similar dynamic. At one point we did have a conversation in which he pointed out that I tend to be controlling and often dismissive of his ideas. It was a real eye opener for me as I could, in hindsight, see this behavior in myself. He also had a tendency to not speak up for himself. The combination resulted in a lot of hurt feelings. Once we examined the dynamic it was easier for me to spot when I was being dismissive and I could stop myself, or at least apologize and be a better listener.

ANSWER: Great stuff, thank you. It's so hard to do a self-inventory like this.

I appreciate the example, too, because it's easy to see how this could develop into a lopsided situation over the years without either of you realizing it, like in the OP's case. Years of dismissing his ideas in favor of yours turns into his not offering any ideas at all anymore, and you're doing everything, and ... there you go.

Took guts for both of you to be honest.

AdRIFt
Guest Apr 11, 12:23 p.m.
QUESTION: Hi Carolyn, I was let go from my dream job at NIH as part of the so-called April Fool's Massacre. I loved our mission, my projects, and my colleagues. Now all that has been torn away and I'm on the outside, looking in. My friends have been amazing, so supportive it just blows me away. My family, not so much. (MAGA really is a cult.) But I have a lot to be grateful for, including severance pay, which I realize those in private industry don't always get. What I'm struggling with is just how passive I've been. I always thought when I reached retirement or otherwise had a block of free time I would do amazing things with it. Instead, I pretty much just... Sleep. And time is ticking by, along with my life. I'm already on medication and working with a counselor for depression and anxiety. How do I get out of this funk and do something instead of just stagnating and wasting the time I do have to try to find work or at least do all those projects I'd dreamed of? Obviously my job was just an excuse before. I have no motivation or ambition. Advice and strategies welcome! Thank you for your light during this dark time for our country.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: First, I am so sorry this is the way your country thanked you for your work. Even if this were about saving taxpayers money, which it never seemed to be, since there were such obviously better ways to do it, and even if it were actually saving taxpayers money, which it pretty glaringly is not, this is just a despicable and inhumane way to go about it and I am horrified it is being done in my name as a taxpayer. Rage.

Second: Whoa, slow down. Barely a week so far is "ticking by" since this all went down. You went through a wringer of uncertainty for months before that, no? Then very abruptly showed up to your new reality. Meanwhile, people who retire or just leave a job with agency (!) and the usual preparation time typically find themselves sleeping a lot and drifting in that big open sea of unstructured days.

So, please be kinder to yourself and more forgiving. Allow yourself some extra sleep. Let the work with the counselor unfold. Let your dreams reconstitute.

The one thing I will say is not to allow yourself too much extra sleep, because that can be a trap. It's a depression accelerator.

And I will also suggest that you spend some of this recovery time outside, walking, if you're able. It can be a surprisingly therapeutic but still unstructured use of your newly free time. Plus if you live near the NIH mothership then you're in the peak moment of earth's renewal, which is a well-timed bit of counterprogramming.

Type to me again in a couple of weeks if the "be patient with yourself" plan hasn't helped clear your head by then

327Press Enter to expand
15 year old LW with college and career plans???
Guest Apr 11, 12:24 p.m.
QUESTION: Carolyn, the LW this week who is a 15-year old planing on being a vet and going to college in the UK‚Ä¶ left me with my jaw on the floor as the parent of 14-year old twins. Are 15 year olds planning their college years already? Do they really know (like actually) what they want to be ‚Äúwhen they grow up‚Äù with such specificity? My kids are pretty good students, play sports, hang with their friends‚Ä¶ and could not tell you what they want to do as a career or even name a college (unless it‚Äôs sports related). Am I way behind all the other parents and their ambitious kids in getting mine focused on college and career in their early teens?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Ack, no, please don't take that message way from the column!!

A couple of things to keep in mind. First, the obvious, but reminders never hurt: People have their own developmental pace.

That means some 15-year-olds are thinking college and do have a career goal taking shape. And good for them! That doesn't mean there's something wrong with other kids that age who haven't progressed to that point. (I didn't know what I wanted to do with my life till I was 30.)

What is wrong is when kids push themselves to have it all figured out by X point because they think they're supposed to, and burn their best exploration years checking boxes for the college-admissions chase and/or for a major and career path they think will have a high ROI. What a waste. Worse is when their parents push it on them.

The best thing you can do in this situation is love your kids and give them room to figure it out. If one or both of them catches onto a passion or ambition, then, great -- follow their lead in feeding it. If neither does, then instead of panicking, be grateful for how long that keeps the window of wider exploration open. Both outcomes have their advantages.

It's important not to get sucked into the zero-sum thinking -- that because there are kids like this one, your kids will get squeezed out. There are places for all the kids who want educations.

Snide sister
Guest Apr 11, 1:09 p.m.
QUESTION: Today's question about the snide sister constantly putting down her brother really hit home for me. We have a sibling like this in our family. Sibling was clearly one parent's favorite and got far more than the rest of us not only emotionally but materially. When parent did this, parent would rub it in our faces and then demand to know why we weren't happier for sib. It felt like emotional waterboarding.

Sib also feels that need to share harsh and unkind observations, and is accustomed to having the rest of us 'be the bigger person,' or 'let it go, as we were brought up to do. After getting counseling (thank you, couple of excellent therapists) I've gotten better at calling it out. Your 'wow,' and 'that was a really hurtful thing to say' help too.

A couple of years ago, though, sib accused me of something that was not only unkind but also blatantly untrue. Sib had all the evidence to know this was not true but still accused me of going behind their back and plotting to hurt them. I was deeply hurt and massively offended that sib would think I was capable of any of this, let alone fabricate that I'd actually done it.

Sib, when asked to consider the facts, provided an 'I'm sorry if I hurt you' cursory apology followed by a lot of explanation that they were under stress, so I couldn't hold it against them. Sib then whined extensively to favoring parent that I wasn't behaving warmly towards them and parent tried to extract a promise that I'd work things out with Sib. I declined to make the promise, A year ago favoring parent died.

Sib now apparently wants to have a relationship with me but says 'I don't ever want to talk about the past.' I just can't do it on those terms. I think I've forgiven sib--I understand that all the favoritism very much set them up to feel they were never wrong, and much of my anger is at parent. But I also believe forgiveness is one thing but walking back up to the person and saying 'thank you sir, please sir may I have another,' is an entirely different matter.

My rational mind knows it's fine if I believe I also have a say in any terms of reconciliation, but I can't quite get past the suspicion that I'm being horrifically unfair and unforgiving. How do I let go of the idea that it's on me to make this right when I have zero trust or affection left?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: You sound remarkably clear-headed about it all, so I don't have much to add.

A few things. First, this seems right, that "all the favoritism very much set them up to feel they were never wrong"; it's been a long-running theme here that favoritism stunts the favorites. Still, some do recover, even to the point of apologizing to the unfavored siblings -- so your sib is not without agency here. You need to hold your healthy boundaries for your own reasons, but it would be a nice side benefit if smacking into them delivered a clue to your sibling.

Second, your rational mind has it right: You have a say in any terms of reconciliation. Period. "I'd be open to the possibility of a relationship, but honest discussion of the past is the only way forward for me. Let me know if you change your mind."

Third, your last sentence is the emotional garble from all the years of cluckery, so just ignore it. Here:

It's on me to make this right but I have zero trust or affection left.

It's not about responsibility or trust or affection. It's just about your sib's offer: You aren't okay with the terms. For reasons. So, you decline it, and propose the terms you would accept. Your sib is free to accept them or decline them. That's why it's really really okay to strip it down to that, without guilt.

As for when "it's time," to me the answer is, when you're asking whether it's time. Might as well go.
