Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: People whose opinions will "inevitably be snarky and snotty" are rude. Don't plan your daughter's joyful event around rude people.

Do you have to invite them? Seriously. That might be the ultimate wedding gift to your daughter, to show her she doesn't have to sell out her joy to unkind people just because they're related.

It can't be your daughter, you want to "dump out" here. A place to de-stress, have some giggles.

The how-to-start-therapy link isn't just for anxiety, it's also for people who've been emotionally ground down to the point where they think they have to pass everyone else's tests.

About sleeping too much
Guest Apr 11, 2:08 p.m.
QUESTION: Hi Carolyn,

Just had a question about your response where you said sleeping too much can be a depression accelerator. Can you say more about this? I have struggled with depression for years, and when it is bad I am just exhausted all the time and can barely function without sleeping in or taking naps. Without the extra sleep, how are you supposed to get through it?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I am going to refer you to a licensed health professional, which I am not. The caution not to sleep beyond a typical night's sleep is passed along from best practices I discussed with a doctor recently, in the specific context of things people with depression can do outside of medical treatment to ease their depression symptoms and boost the effectiveness of their treatment.

So -- medical treatment would be things like medication and therapy.

These other things included regular exercise; social interaction; attention to nutrition; regular, short (ie not overwhelming), structured attention to keeping spaces clean and uncluttered (e.g. 2 x 15 minutes a day on clearing workspace, doing laundry, basic management); connection with purposeful activities. That last one is a broad category, but covers hobbies, causes, arts, music, etc. Among these was a regular sleep schedule; sleeping in and napping were considered disruptive to the overnight sleep schedule, which contributed to nighttime restlessness and anxiety, daytime grogginess, sense of sluggishness and futility.

Again -- your care plan is personal and that's why the medical consult is your advice.

I spelled out my backing info for context. I also want to say these non-medical things are not depression cures or treatments -- there's often a conflation problem when careful life management is helpful. If anyone tries to use any of what I'm saying here as a justification not to treat depression as an illness or not to go to a doctor, or to yell at me for implying that people can exercise their depression away, or that they can't! then allow me to state here these are all willful misreadings of my point. Not cool. Thank you.

RE: AdRift
Guest Apr 11, 2:08 p.m.
QUESTION: Structure is your friend (now and always) and when you lost your job your structure went away. I 100% agree with Carolyn that you need to be patient with yourself and keep expectations reasonable. BUT you can also use structure to help manage this. Plan lunch dates with friends, or even better, walking dates. Set small goals that are low expectation - read a book, watch a movie, or listen to music rather than get lost online. Commit to a yoga class once a week. Figure out what time of day is most productive and do an hour's worth of chores, then let the rest of the day go. Make a checklist of those chores and check off one per day. Give yourself several months of not doing anything bigger than managing your time (you can even set an end date on the calendar) and then let yourself just observe what it all feels like. This is HARD work, so be kind to yourself!

Petty Ex Left at Alter
Guest Apr 11, 2:32 p.m.
QUESTION: I feel you, Carolyn. But like this person, I suck at moving forward once someone has burned me. It‚Äôs much more natural (for me) to hold a grudge and ice people out. I don‚Äôt like this quality about myself and wish I could be more forgiving or understanding or whatever the quality is you‚Äôre channeling. How do I develop it?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: It's right there in the framing -- "burned me."

It's great that you want to develop a more forgiving and understanding mind set. The quickest path is to familiarize yourself with the "fundamental attibution error." Here's one explanation that seems to cover it, but you can plop it in your browser and read a lot more. It's basically the rationale for getting out of the victim mind set and applying the "we're all just people trying to get by" framework to other people's choices. It's quite freeing. It's nice not to think everyone is a jerk, attacking us, making us carry yet another grudge.

And it takes some practice. If your mental pathway goes right to grrr, then you'll need the mental override to, "Wait, I break up with people sometimes, and I'm not 'burning' them when I do that and I don't deserve to be iced out as if I don't exist." Or, when you get cut off in traffic, say -- instead of getting enraged, remind yourself of the handful of times you did something rude witout realizing it because you didn't understand the merge pattern or you didn't see that car in your blind spot, really! If you keep doing that, then it becomes reflex (except maybe when you're hangry and frazzled and the old reflex breaks through).

Some people will be actual jerks, sure, and acting with malice. It's not all fields of daisies.
Fundamental Attribution Error: What It Is & How to Avoid It

A promise is a promise
Guest Apr 4, 11:05 a.m.
QUESTION: When my wife and I were dating, I told her I'd only wanted to have kids if they didn't consume my whole life and I was able to still do things that are important to me like go to the gym before work. My now-wife promised I'd be able to maintain my morning gym workouts. We have an 18mo and an infant. My wife is complaining that my days are too long. They are long. My routine includes leaving the house to get coffee, check email, workout and then work. I get back in time to kiss my 18mo before bed and then spend the rest of the night cleaning up the house and packing for the next day. My wife is complaining she is exhausted with two kids all week and needs some help. In a few months, she is returning to work for the first time since we've had kids and pushing for change from me. I'm feeling cheated and angry. How can I get her to understand that she promised and a promise is a promise is a promise?
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Yep, a promise is a promise.

You are you and you have your needs and you foresaw them, I do get that. But a reality like this -- how much work kids will require -- is not 100 percent foreseeable. So to enforce it as if it is, at the expense of your wife and kids, is pretty spectacularly selfish and crappy.

So. What are you ready to say about yourself, your child, your wife, your priorities and the variables of life in defending the territory you staked out with this promise?

Do you think your wife agreed to this in bad faith -- i.e., knew full well it was unrealistic to add two kids to your life without making any changes to your schedule, but expected you would change, because who doesn't change to spend more time with his kids?

If that's the case, then you need to say that to her: "I believe you entered our original promise knowing I would have to give up things that were important to me eventually, and figuring I would eventually do so willingly. And I am struggling with that right now."

Or, alternately -- if you don't think it was bad faith, just bad foresight: then you need to be willing to hire out some help to make space for the things you value, instead of expecting your wife to absorb all the extra work displaced by maintaining your routine. Or just pick your priorities and let some other things go.

Because, damn.

They took months to get back to me (meanwhile remaining completely warm and chatty on unrelated topics!) and then finally said that they had decided against it, partly because they don't want to lose their financial security‚Äîbut mostly because they "don't understand" IVF, feel that it's a little bit "unnatural," and vaguely believe that God would give us a baby if it were meant to be.

The hurt and disappointment I felt is hard to express here. I don't feel entitled to their money, but they have helped me and my siblings pay for other things they approved of (college, weddings, expensive grandparent gifts for my nephew).

My husband and I wound up saving aggressively for the next two years and funded our own IVF. We were successful on the first cycle and I'm 18 weeks pregnant. We have shared the news with our family and my parents are jubilant, which really irks me. My mom texts or calls every day to ask about the pregnancy and to squeal about how excited she is to have another grandchild. avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Are you up for a conversation?: "Mom, you turned down our request for financial help -- which of course was your prerogative -- saying you felt IVF was unnatural and that 'God would give us a baby if it were meant to be.' But now that we conceived through IVF with our own money, you're jubilant without any qualms. I hope you can understand why I'm having mixed feelings about your response."

Think about it. It's a fair complaint, for one. Plus, leaving it to harden into a grudge denies the whole family a chance to get past it.

But think about it -- see if time does anything to move you past it naturally. For example, if you start to see this reflects who your parents have always been, committed to their way of seeing things whether there's any integrity to it or not.

Pets
Guest Apr 4, 11:38 a.m.
QUESTION: My husband and I are missing our elderly cats who both crossed The rainbow bridge last December. We are elderly as well and wonder whether we should just enjoy our kids‚Äô cats and dogs from time to time. Ever since my husband developed mobility problems the litter box task was mine and was somewhat overwhelming as the cats developed issues associated with aging. Also we don‚Äôt have a fenced yard to accommodate a dog‚Äîyet! Just looking for advice about 75 year olds adding a pet to our family because we miss their company every day!
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Since I am not there to assess the situation, I can only advise that you bring 100 percent pragmatism and foresight to assessing the situation yourself to counteract any wishful thinking. Wishful thinking can be harmful to animals especially, since they are so vulnerable, but also to the humans who care for them.

Lots of things to consider, but first I wonder if your husband is still ambulatory. Someone with difficulty walking + a pet may = an increased risk of falls.

Second, because you already mentioned overwhelm with prior pet care, I would say the minimum would be to have backup care in place before taking on a pet. By that I mean both daily drop-ins from a paid dogwalker/catsitter (great job for a reliable neighborhood kid) and a backup place for the pet to go in the event the animal needs a new home.

Third, think cat, not dog, unless you're sure you can meet the exercise and socialization needs of a dog.

Possible fourth point, if you have a person in your life (adult child, neighbor, other quasi-caregiver) to whom the care of or responsibility for this animal might devolve, then talk to that person, too, as a courtesy.

Of course the easy answer is not to do it, for all the reasons, but animal companionship is so rewarding that it is worth considering if you can pre-answer all of these questions and issues -- especially if you can afford to.

456Press Enter to expand
Letting go of my brother due to politics
Guest Apr 4, 11:45 a.m.
QUESTION: Is it common nowadays to completely end a relationship with an only sibling? He is 70 and I‚Äôm 65. He became more and more combative in our weekly phone conversations. (We live in different states so we only have phone visits.) I feel a little sad, but I think it‚Äôs healthier for me.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Things don't have to be permanent. The conversations are too combative, so you send the message that you won't continue to take part in them on these terms. You do that by taking a break and saying why.

You can check in again in, say, a month to see if pulling back was effective. If he brings up the same stuff and gets combative again, then you remind him you're not interested in this experience with him, you're hanging up now, you'll try again in a bit.

Repeat, once a month, see what happens. If you feel you can be healthy as you maintain this schedule, then keep at it. See if he can shift to other topics with you. If it's still too much, then try 6 weeks, then 2 months. Etc.

The "completely end" may happen, but there can be a lot of interim steps before you get there, if you want there to be. That's what I'm saying.

Divorce
Guest Apr 4, 11:51 a.m.
QUESTION: I was married for 40 years and just filed for divorce. I was expecting to feel relief, but am just feeling sad even though I think it was the right decision. Also, a little fearful of the future. Any advice or input from anyone who has been there
And how to move forward.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: This is definitely one of those YMMV situations, but I think this part is pretty common -- that when you have been in a certain place or status for a long time, the first days of Something Else have an element of fear to them. It's just hard to envision what the new thing will be. It's kind of a big gray blank. And when we can't envision things, there's a sense of low level animal panic. In my experience at least.

The best advice I have for that is to take deep breaths and trust yourself and get through the days the best you can -- making good decisions for yourself, being your best advocate. Because the other thing that's more than common, probably universal, is that we are built to adapt. You will get used to this as your normal, and before you know it your ability to envision a future will come back to you.

Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: This didn't go where I thought it was going. It is/was your neighbor's responsibility, but if they're not dealing with it then, yes, go for it. ASAP. You don't want this out there drawing other vermin.

avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I can't argue with much here, nice touch of John Mellencamp.

A couple of things before I make a huge ask. First, well, maybe it's the only thing? The money hit to people like your neighbors is getting the big attention, but the flouting of due process is what I wish had been the universal Find Out from the "{redacted} around." People getting grabbed off the street by unmarked, sometimes masked law enforcement, spirited off and locked up (do I need to specify inhumanely?) only on the administration's say-so, without their presenting any case/evidence before a judge: That is the death of freedom for everyone. Especially when the judge says "You can't do that" and they don't turn the planes around. That means anyone, literally anyone, can become the next person they decide doesn't have rights because the administration welp just doesn't like what they said. We are kidding ourselves if we think people who smirk about acting with impunity toward Group A will suddenly color within the lines with their treatment of Group B. If one set of papers doesn't matter, then no one's papers matter. This is the 2 + 2 of the past few weeks. A couple of links:

Are you freaking kidding me.

Robin Givhan has a roundup here.

But I could post 20 if I had time.

Okay, here's my ask. I'm going to zero in on your "yet." Take your time and do some breathing or pillow-punching or whatever it takes, but: There can't be a "too late."

There just can't.

Anyone who "now feel[s] lied to and apologize[s]" needs to hear, "Apology accepted, thank you, welcome." That's because the cruelty is killing people, and we need a way out of the cruelty, and that means making it as easy as possibly -- appealing even -- for people to walk away from it. Which means not punishing anyone with the courage to regret voting for it.

avatar
Carolyn Hax

Gruge holding husband
Guest Apr 4, 1:10 p.m.
QUESTION: My husband (73) can hold a grudge longer than anyone I know. He will never just let things go. It become a problem when I try to convince him to forgive and forget and he takes that as my siding with the offender and a huge fight will ensue. Who needs counseling, him or me? (he has refused BTW)
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Well, then what's the point of my saying he's the one who "needs" it?

If you want things to change between you, then apparently you're the one who will have to make the changes. Therapy can be one of those changes. Go and have some good talks. Another change can be a decision not to "try" anymore to "convince him to forgive and forget." If he wants to stew in his old resentments, then let him. You can live your life breathing the clean air of forgiveness.

If he's trying to, for example, keep you from seeing friends because they're on his grudge list, then that's something else -- but you do have agency, right? You don't need him to give you permission?

Or is that the real issue, he uses his resentments to limit what the two of you do as a couple. If so, that's more than just grudge-holding. That's control, and another point in favor of your talking to a therapist yourself.

Tired of being polite
Guest Apr 4, 1:59 p.m.
QUESTION: This has been building for years now, but I feel nearly depleted by the lack of consideration and basic manners in society. I‚Äôm forever stepping off the sidewalk so people can walk two abreast, or saying ‚Äúthank you‚Äù to cashiers that literally don‚Äôt say a word to me, or waving the other person through when there‚Äôs confusion at a 4-way stop. And it feels like no one ever does any of that for me. I let the person with a few items go ahead of me at the store, or wait patiently when someone has trouble parking. No one lets me go ahead and I get honked at. I‚Äôm in my 50s and certainly not a saint, but I‚Äôm so tired of giving others consideration that‚Äôs rarely extended to me. How do I not throw up my hands and just become the type of person I resent? It sounds more appealing than being walked on all the time.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: I feel you. But the alternative doesn't make much sense (except maybe with the sidewalk-stepping). Thank-yous and other kindnesses to people, especially those in service work, are never wasted. Even when not reciprocated, they are a net benefit to the system. I liken it to a 20-minute walk. Even if you're not doing it every day and getting in shape from it or making progress toward your fitness goal, you're still better for having done it than you would have been if you hadn't.

Waving people through intersections and letting people go ahead in checkout lines involve a small sacrifice from you, so carry a different benefit. I think those can actually send someone off into their day a teeny bit more optimistic about humanity. A wee smidge. So even if it's not coming back around to you, I think it's a great practice to continue, if you can remain forward-thinking enough to bear it. Maybe that person will be less angry with others a mile down the road. And that's to your credit.

The parking situation? Not stooping to impatience is purely for you. You gain nothing from honking except reducing yourself to an angry troll and knowing you inflicted more stress on the person just trying to park. So don't even think about it.

(And note to anyone out there who does this: Just freaking stop. Thank you. It never once in recorded automotive history ever made anyone park any faster, just more anxiously, at the likely expense of neighboring cars' paint jobs. Yours, at some point, if there's any cosmic justice.)

Last thing, the sidewalk: Don't step off. That's excessive deference to the entitled. Plowing through them isn't ideal, either, tempting though it may be, so I've settled on standing still to allow them to flow past. I'll post others' ideas if you send them in.

Anyway -- I hope this approach > "fork it."

Aunt in white jeans at wedding
Guest Apr 4, 2:00 p.m.
QUESTION: I still think the aunt was issuing some kind of (bleep) you, perhaps to the couple but maybe to the whole family.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Maybe so, but the only play was to ignore it and be exactly as gracious a host to her as to everyone else.

Here's why. If she left her home thinking this was the right thing to wear, then of course the thing to do is to treat her as if she fits right in and looks lovely. Right?

If she chose her outfit as some kind of [bleep] you, then what better response than to be unfailingly gracious to her? Then she gets to feel one of two ways: 1. Nobody even noticed her big bird flip. 2. Anyone who did notice was too polished or kind (or too wily) to take her bait. It's the [bleep] you falling in a forest, with no one there to hear.

Win-win.

just how
Guest Apr 4, 2:04 p.m.
QUESTION: Chaos is the goal here right? Everything is insane and getting crazier by the second but HOW DO WE FUNCTION? How do we keep on keeping on? I refuse to stick my head in the sand but I need help on how to function
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: How do we ever function amid chaos? Do the right thing, such as the right thing can be determined.

Whenever it seems impossible to tell what that is, make the decision you're trying to make smaller: Do the next right thing. Be kind, be principled, be strong, be helpful, have hope, whichever the situation calls for.

Or just stop scrolling, go to bed at a decent hour and get back to it tomorrow.

Another farewell
Guest Apr 4, 2:20 p.m.
QUESTION: Hi Carolyn.

I'm not writing this with the intent of it being published in the chat, but I certainly don't mind if it is. Like numerous folks before, this is a farewell. I cancelled my subscription just before the election, and my access is coming to an end in a few days.

I kept hoping ... SOMETHING ... would happen that would make me change my mind. Instead, as the current administration has been even worse than I could have imagined even in fever dreams, the Post's ownership and editorial leadership has increasingly bent the knee to the current occupant of the people's house, who seems to want nothing so much as to punish the entire country for daring to reject him in 2020. The constant exit of bright, talented, passionate writers, hitting its peak (nadir?) with Eugene Robinson's resignation has cemented my decision.

I've not ever had a question for you, but reading your advice over the decades (!) has helped me along the way - before needing to ask them in many cases. Even before I subscribed to the Post, I followed you back in your syndicated days, and I will miss the daily dose and (especially) the holiday and wedding hoots and the weekly Ned fixes. My best to you and yours, and a fervent hope we all survive the next four years (somehow we still have almost a full four years of this ... insanity left) mostly unscathed.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: Thank you for the note. I too am heartbroken to see Gene go; he is not only a great columnist, but also a remarkably decent human being. He led the Style section back in the early days of my column and his support was invaluable. Best office holiday parties ever, too.

I will also say this: Given the announced changes to the Opinion section, I expect there will be more turnover. If you had said to me before that you were looking to this part of The Post for your "SOMETHING," then I would have suggested you look instead to the news side.

That is where The Post ownership is not directing coverage. Instead, it news side is behind the same wall it has always been -- which means the owner and publisher hire top editors and then let them do their jobs.

I never know when a reader asking about the changes at The Post is aware of the difference, so I err on the side of over-explaining, because it's such an important distinction: News side is its own thing, and is covering the administration and everything else it covers under the direction of editors, not ownership. The Post's news coverage has borne this out.

I've been confused by people who balk that I get my updates from the Post (they don't) and it dawned on me that all they are hearing is how controlling the owners are, but haven't actually read the quite informed and facts-driven reporting. I am lucky in that as a federal employee I have access to the Post for free, and if I'm being honest, I probably wouldn't pay for a subscription on my own given the uncertainty in my job status. Here's to hoping the administration does not take this perk away from us and thank you for your wonderfully direct advice and much needed candor as the world gets a bit more insane each day.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: You're welcome, and thanks for the anecdote, because I'm never sure where my information is landing.

Here's another thing. I'll link to my colleague Erik Wemple on the fact that 3,200 newspapers have closed in the past decade. Do you know who loves this? People in power who want to get away with stuff without being held accountable. People who love low-information voters.

So as long as The Post is fighting to hold people accountable, I will support The Post. I support other news outlets, too -- the narrowing of the business is catastrophic. It is part of why we are where we are.

Also, there is great stuff coming out of the Opinion side too. Monica Hesse is a must-read. Dana Milbank was born to bring black humor to the end times. Just for two because all this linking is taking me forever. As I said, that part of the paper is in flux, but they're there now and I read every word.

Combative Phone Coversations
Guest Apr 4, 3:04 p.m.
QUESTION: My husband has this method with his parents: If they start talking politics, he ends the call. He told them he would start doing this, and he even started to time it. "10 minutes in and you brought up politics, bye." And, it worked! On the last call, his dad made it 50 minutes. Even then, the calls are still fewer and farrer between because, for his isolated parents who only consume propaganda, their reality is very distorted and they are not interested in anything but the topic du jour that they are being told to be angry about.
avatar
Carolyn Hax
Advice Columnist
ANSWER: It really is remarkable how effective it can be. Similar to "gray rock" responses to people who like to stir the pot. We really do control what we talk about with people. No means no if you hold the line.

QUESTION: I saw my letter this week. Things are okay. My daughter and I went to family therapy together and worked some stuff out. It can still get a little weird between us sometimes but we accept that. My parents abstained but we accept that too. They said they were going to tell her all when she was 18 and she seems to believe them. Her bio-dad started all of this because he told his fiancee about our daughter and the fiancee wanted to meet her. He went directly to our daughter because he figured my parents would say no. He thought maybe he still had some rights since he never signed any papers but my folks sent notices to his work place and his sister's house (his last known residence) and the judge said 'good enough' and terminated his rights. Our daughter didn't want to meet his fiancee or him and that's the last we've heard from him. I think things worked out as well as can be expected
